“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tlxes Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
is Ike only paper which has a consistent ami dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person,- one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the i
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.'
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin,
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th j
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success- in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clahns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
-The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most Widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so. .closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
tothe bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent imshman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—-that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Out of the Train: In Richmond.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I am lying on my bedstead in a little dingy room,
Lying list’ning to the landlady who’s growling
down below,
And more fearsome than the dampness and the
dreariness of gloom
Is the thought of all she’ll utter as she tells me
what I owe.
And I pull the blanket round me with a nervous
thrill of pain,
As I gaze upon the clothes-line through the dirty
window-pane.
I am used to washing struggles, and I reck not of
the cost
As that clothes-line brings me glimpses of the
other boarder’s togs,
But I can’t help thinking sadly of the linen I have
lost,
As my single shirt dries slowly in the midst of
Richmond fogs,
My only under-raiment that I’m waiting for in
vain
I see floating from the clothes-line through the
dirty window-pane.
One time, not so very long ago, I had a lot of
things—
Quite a decent sort of wardrobe as to collars,
shirts, and ties,
But they’ve “melted” in the washing, or to
uncle’s taken wings,
And now I’m nudely waiting till my scanty
garment dries.
It is thin and old and ragged, yet it’s all that I
retain,
And I watch it, with emotion, through the dirty
window-pane.
Then, ah, then 1 When it is ready will the missus
take it down,
Quickly iron it and starch it in a streaky sort of
way;
Then she’ll lay it on my door-mat—“lf you’re
going into town,
It’s just time as you was moving; don’t get
lying there all day !”
Though she knows, for all it profits, that I might
as well remain
Gazing idly at the clothes-line through the dirty
window-pane.
And a wild and futile longing all at once I seem
to feel
To get up and cut that clothes-line and set trail-
ing in the dirt
All the other lodgers’ washing and then back to
bed to steal
And to contemplate the ruin, but, alas I my
only shirt
Would be spoiled with all the others, so I don’t
see what I’d gain,
But I curse the crowded clothes-line ll.iough
the dirty window-pane.
And soon gently o’er my spirit comes a sense of
grateful rest,
For it’s started raining softly, and the missis
won’t invade
E’en the threshold of my chamber till she knows
that I am dressed,
So I’m thankful that the drying of my shirt has
been delayed.
And I turn once more to slumber—with a blessing
on the rain,
And a last look at the clothes-line through the
dirty window-pane.
Melb., Oct. 13, 1893.
P.B.G. and A.V.G.
Dear Bulletin, —Did not the Khedive of
Egypt (that one who spent millions in personal
extravagance) make an arrangement with the
national bondholders, some 20 years ago, said
arrangement entailing a sacrifice by creditors of a
portion of their claim ? Yours, Unearned In-
crement. [That wasn’t bankruptcy, but recon-
struction. — Ed. B.]
The Smithsonian Institute, U.S., has a
skeleton of the Hypsirhophus, the fossil kangaroo
of America. It was 30ft. long, its back studded
with spines of great size. The tail had a
double row of sharp spines. A coat of mail
covered the entire body, and the mouth had a bony
beak.
The British navy still continues to hold
the record for muddlemeut, hideous faultiness of
construction, and general disaster. The latest
news is that the Resolution, a huge iron tank of
14,150 tons, has been so badly strained somewhere
in the innards that she was glad to crawl back
into port again to be disembowelled and fixed up.
The Bulletin’s Missing Word Compe-
tition No. 10 closes on Wednesday, Jan. 24, 1894.
A wandering Australian artist recently
painted the portrait of a Maoriland farmer’s wife,
and presented it to her as a souvenir. Then the
she-Goth looked at him suspiciously, and asked,
in an aggressive tone, where the other eleven were?
She had an idea that oil-paintings went by the
dozen, like photographs.
In ’72, at Maitland (N.S.W.) one Jimmy
and a mate (who had been a local preacher) were
arraigned for robbery from a dwelling. “ Have
you anything to say why the sentence of the
Court,”&c.? Jimmy: “Nothing, yer Honor; only
I wish to remind you that life is short, so make
my sentence shorter.” Two years. “Have you
anything,’’ &c. ? The ex-L.P. soared into a
brilliant harangue that quite “fetched ” the Court
with its eloquence. In the midst of a most
pathetic flight, the gaol-bell rang, whereupon
Jimmy, who knew the ropes, leaped up, and
shouted: —“Gut it short, or we’ll miss our
dinner.”

With a smile;

FRANK CRENNAN.
The German’s Tailings.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“The machine works well enough,” said
Jack to his mate ; “ but it can’t make the gold, if
the gold’s not there. However, we may drop
across it, like the others, if we keep pegging on.”
“We might,” grunted his mate.
This was after four days of hard and fruitless
toil with a patent dry-blower.
The two mates were known at Hannan’s to be
real chickens. The sun blazed down and seamed
their unwashed hides with ugly cracks, but they
persevered undauntedly till they were down to
dry damper and a gallon of water a man.
The gold had no run. That was patent. The
party above them were all right; they just were
on it. The German “hatter” below them was
also evidently in luck, for he could be seen trans-
ferring specks from his dish to his “shammy.”
“I wonder what Bismarck would take for his
tailings?” said Jack, one evening; “he can’t be
saving all his gold with only dishes.”
“ Not half of it. Take my word, Dutchy’s got
a real good thing. Have you twigged the shammy
at his belt ? - It was light enough when he came
here last month, and now look at it! Notice, too,
how the beggar shepherds his claim. As though
he was scared that we’d jump it.”
That was true enough. The German slept on
his claim, and seldom left it for longer than a
minute. His behaviour was a patent defamation
of his neighbours’ characters, and earned him
their hearty dislike, which found occasional voice.
But Bismarck was a gentle-spoken, peace-loving
man, and conciliated the mates so self-depre-
catingly that their words were never actually
translated into actions.
* * *
“Yah ! ouph you vant, you can haf mein
dailinks. You can haf dem vor yoost dree
ounzes. Ach ! dere vas a man vhat vood gif me
vife ounzes ; putt no madders. You been voork-
ing alongside me, coot napers, und you can haf
dem vor yoost dree.”
The gold was weighed out, and changed hands
one Sunday evening,
• • •
That night was a bright, moony one.
“ My lug vhas yoost right in ; I can mai'g tventy
miles py dis light,” said a solitary swagman, as
he struck along the Ooolgardie road.
After a while, the swagman sat down on his
bundle for a spell and a smoke. Then, he took a
well-filled chamois-leather bag from his belt, and
recklessly emptied its contents on the ground.
“Yah! I dinks you vhas hardly voart dree
ounzes,” and he laughed softly. “ I suppose
dey’ll yoomp my glaim.” And he laughed loudly.
* # #
On the Wednesday, two men went into
Hannan’s and took the first bid that offered for a
patent dry-blower. They appeared to have lost
confidence in it.
Perth, W.A.
THOS. A. BRATTIE.
Some stupid jester, in 1848, advertised J.
N. Brunker’s death in the S.M. Heeald.
Mr. Brunker, 46 years after, is still very much
alive.
Before undergoing operative or other
treatment for Piles, consult Mr. A. Sharp on the
new and successful “ Cannalin ” treatment, 257
George-st., close to Bridge-st., Sydney.*
Scientist: “You talk of the statesman
biting pieces out of tumblers. Try it yourself,
putting tooth and tumbler under water, and you
needn’t be the lady with the iron jaw,”
The Times has discovered that “the roil
is the true source of colonial wealth.” Ever and
anon some great, stale truth like this strikes the
editor of an Australian daily, and is diffidently
set forth in a leading article, and travels round to
the editor of an English daily, and he incorporates
it in a leading article, and it is straightway called
back to Australia, and appears in large type
under an enormous heading, and people bow down
and worship it as an unheard-of inspiration of
British genius. Some folk would never know
they were alive if they didn’t read in an English
newspaper that the number of idiots in the
colonies is increasing.
Dr. G. R. Ferris, before the Indian Opium
Commission The conclusion I have been
obliged to come to is that, in a country like India,
having regard to the habits of the people, the
character of their avocation, the peculiarities of
the climate, and the particular character of their
food, opium is distinctly beneficial—that it is not
harmful, that it is not a vice, that it does not
promote in any way immorality, that it does not
increase, but distinctly decreases, mortality, and
that without it the vital returns of ma.ny parts of
the country would be simply appalling.” In a
word, opium in India does no more harm than
does claret in France. Years ago the present
writer met an old doctor who, for 30 years, had
been an official physician in Hongkong. He
strongly defended the use of opium, and said that
his unrivalled experience of opium-smokers was
that they were not necessarily any more unhealthy
than peopl« who used tobacco.

By sendiny direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques , Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed 3. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

Notice to Subscribers In Victoria, Queens-
land, South Australia and Tasmania.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £l, with 3s, 2d.
added for postage.
No papers will be sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this rule.
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd.
SATURDAY, JANUARY 20, 1894.
“Watering the Tree of Protection.”
The Victorians were told that (he sacrifice would be
only for a time, but they had been watering the tree of
Protection for 21 years, and was there one of those indus-
tries ready to say to the Victorian people, “Take off the
duties; we can stand on our own feet?”—G. 11.
Reid, of New South Wales.
The above sample of mixed metaphor, de-
livered the other day by G. H. Reid, the
figurehead of the New South Wales Foreign-
traders, is an interesting example of how
the Australian Foreign - trade politician
comes out of the same hole which he went
in at, and keeps on repeating the process ad
infinitum. Something like 21 years ago the
logicians of the Calico-Jimmy party said that
the Victorian Protectionists had said some-
thing wliich they never did say, and ever
since then they have risen with enthusiasm
at short intervals to point out that the thing
that wasn’t said hasn’t been verified. And
though it may be proved over and over again
that the alleged statement was purely a
Foreign-trader’s invention, and a particularly
dull invention at that, the fact that the
prophecy which no one ever uttered has not
c me true, and that Protection hasn’t kept
the promise which it never made, will be
dished up at regular intervals till G. H.
Reid’s latest successor has fallen into the
sere and bald-headed leaf, and his creed is a
toothless superstition speaking feebly out of
a tomb.
a a #
Neither the Victorians nor any other
people were ever told by any Protectionist
statesman that “ the sacrifice would be only
for a time.” For, in the first place, Pro-
tection is not a sacrifice in any sense of the
term, and it is not for a time but for the
best part of an eternity. It wall end, and
there will be universal Freetrade, when all
the nations of the earth have attained to
practically the same standard of civilisation,
when wages are equal throughout the five
continents ; when, in the use of machinery
and all manner of labour-saving appliances,
all races are on an equality, when there is a
universal agreement as to the hours of
labour, when cheap niggers and cheap China-
men and cheap non-unionists are all
abolished, when war is extinct, and
there are no more blockades to make
it essential that a nation should be
self-dependent—and not till then. And, as
these events seem to be so far distant as to
lie beyond the outer rim of Time and further
off than Futurity, it may be said that Pro-
tection, to all intents and purposes, is going
to last for ever. It was not introduced apolo-
getically as a temporary evil which was to
be abolished later on, when it had served a
temporary purpose, to make room for a long
and blessed eternity of Foreign-trade. The
reason of its establishment was that Foreign-
trade was a permanent curse, and the Pro-
tectionist no more looks forward to its final
restoration than G. H. Reid looks forward
to a glad future when ho will be disem-
bowelled.
# # #
The assertion that Victoria was persuaded
to submit to Protection, with the assurance
that it would only be of temporary duration,
is the one permanent joke of the otherwise
humourless Foreign-trade faction. The pro-
vince was utterly beggared by Foreign-trade,
and, according to G. H. Reid, it submitted
to be relieved, relying on the promise that
it would by-and-bye be allowed to go back to
its old, joyous condition of beggary. It started
to build up manufactures in the blessed hope
that they would presently be demolished
by the return of Freetrade, and through an
act of unexampled treachery they were not
demolished. It deliberately founded a new
policy in the happy assurance that it wouldn’t
last, and it proceeded to shut out the shoddy
goods made by foreign pauper labour and to
provide work for its own citizens, relying
entirely on the hope that the pauper-made
shoddy would presently come back again,
and that its citizens would be out of work as
before. The mirthless G. H. Reid makes
these assertions seriously, and from out his
vitreous eye he sees the evil that is to come
—or rather the evil which has already arrived
—through that breach of faith, and weeps
lest New South Wales should go and do
likewise,
• • »
As the same harmless little man patheti-
cally observes, after 21 years of continuous
Protection, there is not one Victorian indus-
try of any importance which could stand a
return to Freetrade principles without giving
up the ghost. The Bulletin firmly believes
in the accuracy of this statement, but fails to
see where the horror of it comes in. If the
fact that the protected manufactures of Vic-
toria can’t stand Freetrade without dying of
it is a reason why they should be killed, then
the equally relevant fact that G. H. Reid,
after being protected by the law, in common
with other citizens, between 40 and 50 years,
can’t bear being slain without becoming a
corpse as the immediate consequence, is a
sufficient reason why the law should cease
to protect him and he should be made an
outlaw. And, by the same process of reason-
ing, the fact that Great Britain, after being
protected by its navy for many centuries,
can’t keep off an invader without a navy any
better now than when it first started, is a
sufficient proof that the navy is a failure and
should be sold at the nearest marine-store
for old iron. Also, the argument applies to
Freetrade as well as to Protection. It is the
bitter wail of every Foreign-trader that the
once crowded wharves of Sydney are becom-
ing more and more desolate because the
blighting influence of Protection has killed
the commerce of the city, but if that com-
merce, after more than half-a-century of
Freetrade, is still so weak that it can’t stand
even a small amount of Protection, then the
fact that Freetrade is a failure is too con-
spicuous to need further comment.
• • *
The circumstance that Victoria’s indus-
tries, after 21 years of Protection, still want
Protection as badly as ever, would be lament-
able if the Victorians either expected or
desired them to do otherwise. But, as it
happens, they neither expected nor desired
it. So long as the Victorian artisan is better
paid, and works shorter hours, and lives in a
house which is more like the habitation of a
human being and less like the lair of a hog,
and is less miserable, ignorant, squalid and
hopeless than his principal rivals, the British
pauper, the Chinaman, the Hindoo, and
others, so long Protection must continue to
be necessary. And the fact that it is as
necessary now as it was 21 years ago is
chiefly an evidence that the squalid Foreign-
trader above mentioned remains about as
cheap and as squalid as he was, and that 21
years of sound Foreign - trade principles
haven’t improved his condition in any way.
The most, visible i-esult of Victoria’s policy is
that the province, after only half as long an
existence as that of N.S. Wales, and with
little more than one-fourth the area, supports
a practically equal population ; that she is fully
as rich as the “mother” province despite the
latter’s stupendous asset of an additional
220,000 square miles of soil ; that the cost of
living in Victoria is as low as in any other
of the Australias, and that, with her vastly
smaller area, she is nearly self - sup-
porting in the matter of food-supplies,
which New South Wales, under the Foreign-
trade regime, never was and never even
promised to be. Under these circumstances
the fact that the province can’t afford to
abandon its present policy, and try weird
experiments which promise nothing, is just
as dreadful as the fact that G. H. Reid
can’t see his way to abandon the solid earth
and habitually walk on a wire in mid-air at
half his present salary.
* « •
The principal curse of Protection, accord-
ing to Foreign-trade logic, is that it is a
cumulative disease. It has no finality. The
10 per cent, duty grows to 20 per cent., then
to 30, and so on ; there is a constant demand
for more, and the alleged evil never has an
end. This circumstance is said to be a
positive evidence of the nefariousness of the
protective principle ; but it is just possible
that it arises from the circumstance that the
people at large find Protection to be bene-
ficial, and that they would not want more of
it under any other circumstances. Mean-
while, there is no recorded instance of any
freetrade community putiing in a general
demand for more Freetrade, any more
than there is of a person with a boil
petitioning for a crippled back and an
inflamed corn as an additional blessing.
The cause of this is not seriously explained,
but probably it arises from the circumstance
that Freetrade has been tried and has proved
a pathetic failure. Otherwise the steady
and continuous dropping away of one com-
munity after another from the blessed gospel
of imported shoddy is unaccountable. Vic-
toria, it is true, has “ watered the tree of
Protection ” for two decades, and—barring
the fact that she supports about as large a
population and produces as much wealth
upon 80,000 square miles as New
South Wales does on 300,000 and that
she can show as much material progress
on her fiftieth birthday as her neighbour can
on her hundredth—the results amount to
comparatively little. But, on the other
hand, New South Wales has watered the
barren stump of Freetrade for fifty years,
and all she has to show for it is that she has
fallen fifty years behind her competitor, and
that her other advantages have been mostly
wasted. The “ tree ” may not amount to
much, but the stump is dead and will bloom
in the spring no more.
Federation—Reid on a Rail.
Editor Bulletin, — By his recent Riverine
utterances re federation, Mr. G. H. Reid has
established a claim to succeed “ Janus ” Parkes
as the two-faced “Jim Crow” of Australian
public life. The only difference between the
two is this : that whereas Parkes, when “turn-
ing his back upon himself,” has the audacity to
declare that he always thought and spoke the
same, Reid, with the innocent air of the
Heathen Chinee, apes the sincerity of a saint,
when in the very act of perpetrating the most
transparent tricks of political poltroonery. He
has been among the New South Wales border
farmers at Berrigan, Mulwala and Corowa as
the champion of Freetrade and Federation.
These farmers he is pleased to represent as con-
verts to Cobdenism. As a matter of fact they
are what they ever w'ere National Pro-
tectionists, i.e. Federationists who want
federation on the basis of Freetrade
within Australian limits and Protection
against the cheap labour and over-production
of the outside world. With their policy it is
impossible for Reid to be in sincere sympathy.
He is the accepted champion of the “ no-sur-
render ” Freetrade faction, and the recognised
exponent of provincialism. How impossible it
is for him to advocate the fiscal and federal
views of the border farmers without hum-
bugging them, a few extracts from his re-
ported speeches within the last three years will
show.
• # »
In May, 1890, he distinguished himself by
leading the opposition to Parkes’ Federal Con-
vention resolutions, and, in doing so, he
advanced as a reason against Federation the
prosperity achieved by the different Australian
States under different policies, which he con-
demned in the following terms ( vide Han-
sard : .
But we also know that in working out their destinies
the different colonies, for many years past, have thought
more of their individual interests, and more of advancing
their own prosperity and importance, than of advancing
what is called the Cause of Australian Unity. In doing
that they have pursued a course . . . which, in my
mind, has had this effect; It has vastly stimulated the
remarkable progress of the whole of the Australias.
These rivalries animating each have stimulated all, and I
look upon the past history of the country as a history of a
progress which has been possible in these lands under
what is called a separate Government of the various
colonies.
This is the language used in 1890 of the man
who, in 1894, declares that Protection is a
policy of public robbery, tyranny, oppression,
and a survival of barbarism, in order to make
the federationists of Riverina believe that he
favours federation while posing as a freetrader,
although he did not condescend to explain to
them by what process of reasoning he had
become converted to acceptance of the federal
idea. A few more Hansard extracts from his
1890 speech will suffice to prove one of two
things, either that he was humbugging the
country in 1890 or is trying to do
thing in 1894.
# * «
Thus he continues to declaim against Federa-
tion in 1890 :
Now, the beginning of this movement was not an
impulse of brotherly love. It was not that sort of out-
pouring of fraternal affection which might bring brothers
together. It is the old story— at the heart of this move-
ment is the military spirit. . . . When you talk to
him (the average man) about precipitating us into a
nation, his shrewd common-sense tells him that you are
pretty much telling him to take a leap in the dark. It is
the fashionable way of inviting you to shut your eyes and
open your mouth, and you will get something very nice.
Then, as regards the fiscal phase of Federation,
he was, if possible, even more explicit and
emphatic :
I have never dropped the other half of Freetrade, that
is, international Freetrade. ... I do not want any of
their half-and-half Freetrade. ... I cannot tamper
with Protection, because I believe it is slavery and
restriction. ... I can look with no satisfaction on
any kind of Federation which will drag this country into
the mire of Protection. ... I am not prepared to
erect across Port Jackson barriers against the world. . .
I am not prepared to do that for all your Federation. I
will not federate until I have a better idea—a more
rational idea—that my principles will not be sacrificed ;
and I say that the man who believes that New South
Wales, handing over these powers to a Federal Parliament
of all the Australias, will result at once in Freetrade, is a
madman.
# w *
Just so, the man who believes that a Federal
Parliament will not maintain the present
national fiscal policy of Protection against the
outside world must be a madman. And in tin
face of the above decisive declarations against
Australian Federation and in favour of inter*
national Freetrade, the public are entitled to
ask from Mr. Reid, when he poses as the Free-
trade advocate of Federation in the border
districts, for straight answers to the following
string of pertinent questions :—ls there less of
the military spirit at the bottom of the move-
ment to-day than three years ago? Does Mr.
Reid dare to say that there is any better
chance of getting international Freetrade now
than then ? Has not the cause of Protection
been advanced rather than retarded in Austra-
lia by the adoption of that policy in New South
Wales, and by the imposition of further border
duties and stock taxes? Wriggling won’t save
Reid from one or other of the horns of the
dilemma in which these questions place him. If
he was sincere in opposing Federation for the
sake of Freetrade in 1890, lie cannot be sincere
in supporting it while still advocating Free-
trade in 1894. When was the fit/ of sincerity
on him, first or last? Perhaps he will endeavour
to wriggle out of the difficulty by denying that
he ever suffered from that complaint. Pro-
bably he never did, as it is of rare occur-
rence among Australian politicians. Besides, we
have Mr Reid’s own authority for doubt-
ing his sincerity, because during the speech
from which the foregoing pieces de convictions
are taken he was reproached by Mr. John
&ee with only arguing one side of the question,
to which he cynically rejoined : “ I have
always heen one-sided on that question. I may
perhaps sit on a rail some day.” This candid
warning of his possible future insincerity is
about the only sincere tiling worth recording
about Mr. Reid’s politically insincere career,
except it be that he was on a rail then, is on a
rail now, and will be on a rail to-morrow.—
Yours, &c., John Norton. Sydney, January
12, 1894. 3
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Spasm of Virtue.
liiENew South Wales Department of Justice
has issued a minute setting forth that the un-
lawful and dishonest “ tote ” is in future to be
put down with a strong hand. The Depart-
ment perpetrates this document in a surprised
tone of voice, as if ii had just heard of the
tote for the first time as an entirely new evil,
and was resolved to nip it in the bud. Hitherto
a hundred or so of notoriously dishonest
“ totes have pursued their calling openly and
in the light of day, so that every one who
passed could hear the brazen “ sport ” conduct-
ing his prohibited trade inside. And about
once a month, on an average, the law would
seize upon one of the multitude of “tote”-
fiends and line him about half-a-day’s takings,
and even while he was at the court being fined
his clerk or his partner would be running the
business as usual. The Department of Justice
now professes to have heard of these things for
the first time, and in its wild astonishment it
su gg es ts that imprisonment, instead of a mere
fine, might possibly do something to meet
the case. It probably never occurs to the
bland and simple-minded Department that a
“tote with any capital at the back of it will
get over this new difficulty by keeping on the
premises a bogus proprietor, whose duty it will
be to go to gaol on behalf of the real proprietor,
and do bis sentence “on his head,” or other-
wise ; and as the law, so far as appears, will
only drop on each fraudful “sport” once in
18 months or so, as heretofore, even imprison-
ment may not affect the evil so very much after
all. The shortest cut to the abolition of
“ totes ” would be the arrest and imprisonment
for six months, “without the option,”of the rich,
fat, good man of unblemished respectability who
lets his shops for purposes of dishonest gambling,
at double the rent which any honest tenant
could afford to pay. And, in like manner, the
shortest road to the abolition of houses of
ill-fame is a period of hard labour in gaol
for the good, oily church-member who owns
the premises, and charges 100 per cent,
more rent because of his tenant’s immo-
rality. It is perfectly easy to find the pious
landlord in each case—the wildly-astonished
Department of Justice could lay its hands on
something like a hundred of him inside of 24
hours if it wanted to, only it doesn’t want to.
And among the hundred would be members of
both Houses of Parliament, parsons, - respectable
and serious lawyers, bulky merchants, bankers,
and, in fact, specimens of every variety of
person with a stake in the country. If Parlia-
ment will pass an act decreeing that the land-
lord of every gambling den, “ tote ’’-shop, and
house of ill-fame shall be imprisoned for any
period from one up to six months and fined,
say, £6o—half the fine going to the informer-
then it will begin to look as if it was reasonably
serious in the matter for the first time. And
if Minister Slattery will draft a measure to
that effect, it will then begin to appear as if he
was reasonably serious.
Haynes Coloured and Haynes Plain.
John Haynes, M.L.A., addressing the down-
trodden farmers at Molong (N.S.W.): —
The Cd. per bushel duty on wheat had done the fanner
double wrong, as it had enabled a band of city schemers
to fix the load of taxation for their own gain on the
fanners’ shoulders, and at the same time the tax had
induced rich men with capital and machinery at their
command to put 1000 and more acres under wheat, and
so produce such a sudden increase in the wheat yield
that the ordinary farmer found himself swamped and
undersold in the market. The moment the country
reached the level of its own demand that moment all
protection ceased, and as the surplus had to be exported
*he price for the local market was ruled by an outside
figure.
11l other words, John Haynes and his kind
complain bitterly in the country because Pro-
tection has made food cheap, and for this reason
it is accursed and should be swept away. And
in the city John Haynes and his band complain
with equal bitterness that Protection makes
food dear, and for that reason it is accursed
also, and ought to be abolished. This is the
difference between Haynes coloured and
Haynes plain. Haynes (coloured) wails to the
farmers of Molong that Protection has caused a
sudden and great increase in the wheat-growing
industry, which increase is altogether objection'


British Australasian quotes this from
Ihe Bulletin, winch, it says, is “apparently
reconsidering its attitude ” J
It is only f.Ur that, t here should be a Colonial Party in
1 -Ti of There has always been an
Lnghsh party in the Australian legislatures.
J. Bull is even duller than he was thought .to be.
able; and Haynes (plain) tells the city in-
habitant that Protection doesn’t foster
any industry, wheat-growing or other-
wise ; that agriculture ought to be en-
couraged, and that the utterly objection-
able feature about Protection is that it
doesn’t encourage it. Furthermore, Haynes
(coloured) objects because “Protection will
cease ” when the country once produces enough
grain for its own requirements, which either
means that it is sinful for the country to grow
enough grain for its own consumption or else
that Protection ought not to cease. Failing one
of these things, there is absolutely nothing to
grumble about. And, meanwhile, the Foreign-
trade Daily Telegraph announces in a leading
article that the increased New South Wales
wheat-production is not owing to the nefarious
influences of “rich men” at all, but simply arises
from the fact that “ the large influx of Vic-
torian and S.A. selectors which took place two
years ago is beginning to show its effect in a
considerably-increased area under wheat culti-
vation.” Which statement is a serious asper-
sion on the veracity of Haynes (coloured), and
practically means that an influx of farmers—
the most valuable class in the community—
began as soon as Protection made New South
Wales a country which was worth living in.
Piety—in its Affectionate Aspect.
The troubles of the shepherd of a new church—
constructed on the principle of brotherly and
sisterly affection—call attention once more to the
buggy variety of religion. The charges brought
against the holy man relate principally to much
alleged cordial embracing of the young ladies
of the church ; an undue distribution of the kiss
of peace ; a fervent, partial hugging of the flock
at picnics ; a tendency to walk home with
the tenderest lambs at late hours and to
wander with them into the bush on festive
occasions, and a general yearning to press the
church to his bosom. The good pastor’s church
was founded on solid hugging principles in the
first place, but the expounder is accused of run-
ning the principle into the ground, so to speak,
and leaving no room for the other brethren.
Apart from this excess of fervour on his part,
their seems to be little difference between his
church and most other churches. The aggregate
church has more trouble through the°pastor
embracing somebody in the vestry than from
any other cause, and its next greatest trouble
arises out of the mutual hugging of the choir.
Tuere is a warm spiritual affection about most
congregations which leads the male members to
pi op up the aching heads of the female mem-
bers at picnics, and to break out into kiss-in-
the-ring and other violent games, and to lift
the female parishioners down awkward places
with needless fervour when the church goes
finalising with hymns and inferior buns, and to
rush witn unnecessary energy to the assistance
of the distressed girl who is supposed to have a
caterpillar down her back. The members paw
each other altogether too much with hot, damp,
shapeless hands and unmeaning giggles, and
there is a superfluity of the kiss of peace. When
a new church starts with an unwonted burst of
fervour it almost always transpires that the
members thereof call each other “brothers”
and “ sisters ” and distribute the aforesaid kiss
of peace in a copious fashion, and that
sacred and spiritual hugging is a conspicuous
feature in the devotions, and that the" pastor
is fair to see and beautifully slim in the legs.
And when the church bursts up it is invariably
because the hugging was unevenly distributed,
and the parson with the slim legs got more
than his share. A very large proportion of
pastors may be divided into two classes—the
young and lovely kind who hug in a brotherly
sp rit, and the old, bald, benevolent, fatherly
kind, who pat the heads of the female members
of the congregation and call them “ child.”
Half the joy of church attendance consists in
the facilities it affords for being mildly affection-
ate and calling it “ spiritual consolation,” or of
mauling the ears and back hair of a
young “sister,” and doing it strictly “in
the Loard.” And the whole joy of that church
picnic, to which the good snufflebuster is so
powerfully addicted, lies in the pious horse-
play which is sandwiched in between the
hymns and the bucketful of weak tea and the
great masses, of indigestible tea-cake. Still,
the chinch picnic is a healthy entertainment in
its w r ay so far as the young are concerned, and
combines all the advantages of a horse-race, a
pious exhortation and a shampoo—the shampoo
consisting principally of violent head-friction
by the pastor and principal saints. It is only
the elderly saint, who gets cold while sitting
neglected on the grass, who finds it seriously
unwholesome. J
A subscription is being taken up in Mel-
bourne for a monument to William Robert
bATCHELL, the young man who lost his life in a
heroic endeavour to save that of Hannah
M'Lennan. It would, perhaps, be a hundred
times more to the point to give the money to
jus parents—if living—or other near relatives,
but Australia has got the monument disease
badly, and if anybody dies under peculiarly
heroic circumstances it will cheerfully raise a
soulless slab to his memory—even while his
family is in want or is being sued for his funeral
bill. One reason, probably, is that an ordinary
subscription can’t be unveiled with much pomp,
and hard eating and drinking, and many bad
»peecaes by tin-pot local celebrities, which
speeches will be afterwards reported at great
length in the local paper. Neither can
the subscription have two lines engraved
upon it stating the name and deeds of
die person in whose honour it was
erected, and twelve lines announcing that
Vj was Axed up by John Smith, three times
Mayor and 12 years alderman of some tenth-
rate municipality, and unveiled by him in the
presence of, and assisted by, John Jones, John
»Rown, and Daniel Jenkins, aldermen, and
Henry Tomkinson, town-clerk. Australia is
great upon raising monuments to those of its
'lead who are a little more important than the
[ uck > and if . after years, the old, penniless
ather of the deceased is arrested on a charge
°j being homeless and sleeping on the lee side
his son’s statue—well, what does it matter?
Sidelights on New Australia. From P. J.
Macnamara, in Brisbane Courier :
We are only just making a start in the most primitive
way under every disadvantage, and it will take six
months at least before we can fei I really sure how things
will go. . . . It is especially hard on married people
with young children now, but in a few months we expect
to have more comforts and conveniences than are possible
at the start. . . . Our little township at present con-
sists of huts made of thatch, with mud walls. . .
The life we lead at present is very rough-and-tumble, as
might be expected by the pioneers. We have little
variety of food, and have no chance of “ home comforts”
yet awhile; but we have enough to eat, and have to be
content with what there is. Some grumble a lot, but
that is ine\ Stable with human nature, as we have it.
The settlement may not go ahead so well or so fast as we
hope, but we believe it will (urn out right in the long
ru " - ■ • ■ It is easii rto break up a home than to
make a new one in a foreign country', and I would not
like to advise anyone to do so (ill I felt sure that I could
do so with a clear conscience.
From Mrs. H. Watson, in Barrier Miner:—
From Villa Rica to the settlement is 40 miles. We
were four days and three nights on the road. The disgust
of our men, who were walking, was a terrible thing.
. . . There is not much large timb r, and stone is very
scarce, so that building will be slow work. . . . The
gardeners with us have no doubt that they will be able to
raise plenty of vegetables. Till then the rations and the
life will be very rough. ... At present we are living
in tents. We have had but very little sickness, and
f-Ttunately so, for there is no doctor with us, ours havino-
gono back with the Tar, though there are, of course"
medical men at Villa Rica. Mrs. Lane, unfortunately’
was taken ill at Asuncion, and had to be left there, but
she is reported to be improving now.
Reading between the lines, one begins to see
flies on New Australia already.
What will come out of the New South Wales
political muddle no man can prophesy. Most
of the Ministers are politically homeless,
wanderers who have not where "to lay their
heads. Dibbs is leaving his present constitu-
ency and obviously has on several
electorates ; ex-Minister Barton doesn’t know
whether to stand for Potts Point and Woolloo-
mooloo or travel to Wellington and oppose
John Haynes; Copeland is in a fog, if any-
thing, and halts between Armidale and
Bombala; Slattery is to be vigorously opposed
at Burrowa by shearer Mooney. Parkes
will probably give up North Shore, and, making
a virtue of necessity, will parade his resolve to
seek out Dibbs in his lair—when he gets a lair
and fight him to the death. Cannot a good
Democrat be found to go in and beat both of
them out of Parliament ? The nrovince pines
for such a consummation. Wanted, an honest,
clean-living citizen, with brains and discretion
and no axes to grind—a man whom money could
not buy and royalty could not bribe—in many
respects the very antithesis of both Parkes and
Dibbs. Where is that man ? Nowhere !
Under new ownership and editorship Brisbane
Courier (as it says itself) is “getting along
famously” (characteristic phrase of editor F.
W. Ward). Dealing with Miss Shaw’s doctrine
that Northern Australia is for the coloured
man, the Courier says :
Is it quite certain that the native-born Australian
working-man will not. be able to successfully cope with
the northern climate? . . . Miss Shaw saw North
Queensland just after she had seen South Africa, where
the native peoples are very numerous, and the English
capitalist is a grand sort of settler. But we have yet to
see what the English working man can make of tropical
Australia. . . . If there are possibilities in the North
of establishing agricultural industries which would mean
dense settlement, we may find that white men are quite
tough enough to conquer the climate. Or it may happen
that there are considerable tracts in North Australia
which have a climate which is not prohibitory to white
labour. It is too early, anyhow, to rush to general and
unqualified conclusions upon the matter.
Good !
Writes a correspondent to The Bulletin
“I’ll will my property as I like,” snarled an ogre to
his wife as he lay in bed last New Year’s Day and signed
certain documents for his solicitor. He and his wife had
begun life on the plane of common hard work—he in a
woodyard and she at a washtub ; time flew, prosperity
came to them. They bought a wood and coal business-
he working hard, but she as hard. They bought land
built houses, let them off to tenants-he still industrious’
but she no less so. And when, at the end of forty vears
of married life, he thought last New Year’s Day that he
was about to die, he said to his wrinkled partner - “I'll
will my property as I like ” What is the good of talking
so much about fiscal theories, and Australian Federation
and Imperial Institutes and such things while the law
enables ogres like that to rob their life-long partners
simply because the partners are women and wives’ The
particular ogre referred to here has not yet died, and it is
to be hoped that before he #ets a chance to dissipate the
partnership property, as ill-will to his old co-worker and
caprice and vanity dictate, legislation will come to the
woman s aid.
Bishop Webber—a Queensland man of God
now in England trying to cadge £50,000 to fill
the Kanaka-blasted country with parsons—rises
on solemn fat legs to declare that the crying
need of Australia, is inert and women—more men
and women, myriads more men and women
Australian churches are empty, for the native-
born population won’t go; so we must import
godly people to fill them, and more parsons at
salaries to preach to the people. How, in the
name of Malthus, a population which can’t
now, as things go, find bread-and-cheese, is
going to be helped by the arrival of more popu-
lation, this apostle of common claptrap doesn’t
stop to consider. In Australia, just now, a
man without money is merely a month. Unless
Bishop Webber is prepared to fill that mouth
with something more substantial than psalms
and Scripture texts, he had better shut his own.
Two members of the Speight v. Syme jury were
seemingly convinced that the Age couldn’t go
wrong in the case. Ihey partially gave way
on a couple of counts, but each time they
nullified their votes for Speight by considering
that the pioved libel had been fair comment.
When the Age made a gratuitous false state-
ment concerning the accumulation of useless
railway stores, it was fair comment. Also, the
Age theory of a reckless increase in the cost
of each employ^—an alleged increase of £4O
per head—was held to be “fair comment” by
no less than four jurymen who agreed that the
figures were both libellous and false. Here the
finding promised much comfort for journalism.
Say that a newspaper urges the dismissal of a
certain manager of an unprosperous bank on
the public ground that banks are national in-
stitutions which, for the credit of the country,
should be made to pay. Among other “proofsi ”
of the manager’s incompetence the newspaper
declares that the average rate of salary in the
bank has been raised 30 per cent, unnecessarily.
Ibis statement, although unsupported by the
balance-sheet and unproved by the alienator,
assists towards the manager’s “ chucking out,”
He has no claim for damages against the organ
that erred in its figures. The yarn was fair
comment.
Mr. Florence O’Driscoll, M.P., writes from
the House of Commons ;—•
Dear Bulletin, —I am sorry your London correspondent
Should have misinformed you regarding ray attitude
towards the so-called “ Australian Party." lam one of
those who from the first refused to be in any way con-
nected with it, on the grounds:—
Ist. Of its non-representative character.
2nd. That those who call themselves members of the
Australian party are self-appointed.
3rd. That the people of Australia never asked any of us
to become guardians of their affairs.
4th. That irresponsible meddling with subjects about
which no detailed information or knowledge is possessed
by members of the self-appointed party, is likely to lead
to confusion and unpleasantness.
sth. That I have every confidence in the ability of the
Australians to manage their own business.
I am more than a casual visitor to Australia. I lived
there continuously from childhood until long after I had
reached manhood; some 17 years in all. My signa-
ture to a letter of condolence to Lady Tryon was
an attempted act of courtesy which no Australian
would refuse to join in offering, if requested to do so by
those who desired it should be made, and it was an act
which tew people would misconstrue.—Yours sincerely,
r lorencb O’Driscoll, London, 6th Dec. 1893.
An Australian in England writes to The
Bulletin :
About six firms of brokers monopolise the wool-selling
business of London, and it is practically impossible for a
new firm of wool-brokers to start business. These brokers
keep a check on all Australian wool—from the shed to
Loleman-street and stand by to help the squatter or the
squatter s London agent with capital at every step in the
transition of the wool. Consequently, it requires an
enormous capital to conduct the business. Apparently
the other strong argument against a new firm secur-
mg any of the trade, is that buyers pay attention
to the cachet given to a clip by the firm who are
in the habit of selling it. It is a huge monopoly,
similar to that enjoyed by stock-brokers in Paris. The
wool-sales at the Coleman-street auction-room take place
fon? to i or 8 in the evening, and the noise made by
the bidders resembles nothing more than the intermittent
yelping of a pack of fox-terriers. Most of the chief buyers
are foreigners. It is curious how all the staples of Aus-
tralia are wrapped in the tentacles of rings-in London.
Uur meat, wheat, wool, silver, and butter are sold at the
sweet will of various combinations, and until the Austra-
lian Commonwealth avoids politicians, and appoints
business men as Agents-General, it is hard to see how the
system can be altered.
Ihe Bulletin has been thinking about Coroner
Youl, of Melbourne. At the recent inquest
on Hannah M'Lennan, who jumped off the
South Melbourne pier (apparently just to spite
her recalcitrant lover, Birtles), this apostle of
a new faith observed to Birtles : “ There are
times when the truth should not be spoken.
If you will do these things you should protect a
woman’s character even if a lie is wanted.” As
the witness had just previously been sworn to
tell the truth, and severely cautioned against
evasiveness, this sort of treatment must have
depressed his spirits. No doubt there are
cases where a lie to save a woman’s sole posses-
sion (i.e., her character) is excusable, but the
province of Justice consists in criticising and
not in constructing the witness’s action or
speech. If a woman’s reputation is the first
living consideration, the perjurer who gets the
victim of blackmail hanged not only is a higher
type of civilisation than the convict, but the
aid of the iState should also be gratuitously
supplied to him to enable him to concoct and
express his lies. The Bulletin is sorry for
poor Hannah M‘Lennan. It is also sorry for
Birtles —and for Coroner Youl.
The proprietor of the Age is practically travel-
ling tile length and breadth of Victoria, once
every 24 hours, with his own hat in his hand and
a prayei for assistance on Ms lips. He asks for
donations on the ground that he is a public-
spiiited man, a sworn exposer of abuses, a
defender of the poor toiler against the fat
jobber—but Mr. DavidSyme’s appeal lias been,
all circumstances considered, the ghastliest
failure on record. No independent reader of
the Age gives it credit for high motives in its
determination to ruin Speight. The journal
that bonnetted for the Premier Building Society
crowd and gave Mat Larkin what looked
much more like sympathy than fair play
(as long as he was supposed to be only
a ten or fifteen thousand pounder) will
never be able to raise a national subscription
foi itself. Ihe Age sacrificing money upon the
altar of Duty to Society ! Where are' its ex-
posures of banking swindles and swindlers, or
its “ bold articles ” upon the scandals that have
cost Victoria untold millions in loss of moral
tone ? It may be fairly urged that the Age did
honour to the character of Speight by attack-
ing his work so persistently.
Jerome K. Jerome announces that he
will write no more professedly humorous work,
i,.^ r()^ humorous” is good. Jerome has
hitherto obviously considered himself a humorist •
so his renunciation of the humour may perhaps
be taken in the light of an awakening and a
repentance. After all, man grasps at the unreach-
able.
The English peeresses are becoming a very
‘ mixed ” lot. There is the Marchioness of Ailes-
bury who was Dolly Tester-the Viscountess
Hinton, future Countess Poulett, who was Lydia
Ann bheppey, ballet-dancer the Countess of
Clancarty, who was Belle Bilton—the Countess of
Orkney, who was Connie Gilchrist-the Countess
of Luston, future Duchess of Grafton, who was
the notorious Kate Cook-and, finally, the future
Duchess of Psewcastle, as May Yohe expects to
be through her marriage to Lord Francis Pelham
Clin ton-Hope, younger brother of the present
childless and invalid duke. The Viscountess
Hinton is a peeress in strange circum-
stances. Her husband’s father, Earl Poulett
while quite a youngster, had a flirtation with
the daughter of a coast-pilot, and after a few
weeks was compelled to marry her at the point
of a pistol held by her irate father. Six months
later she gave birth to the Viscount Hinton, whom
Bord Poulett never recognised as his son : but as
the law recognised that relationship, the earl is
using every effort to alienate and ruin the estate
which he must transmit to the present viscount.
1 he latter thought at first that he could soften the
old gentleman s heart, and supported himself for
some years by acting as a pantomimist and clown
under an assumed name. But, that failing him,
he began issuing post-obits to the extent of his
ability, and spent the proceeds like water. It was
during this time that he married Lydia Ann
bheppey a ballet-dancer, and at present the Vis-
counfi and Viscountess Hinton and their progeny
• the Hon. William Henry George” and “the
Hon. Maud Marie'—wander through London
with a barrel-organ, begging their bread.

SUNDRY SHOWS.

Criterion.. “Charley’s Aunt.”

Aliiamrra Variety Co.
A company whose intentions are evidently
honourable—said company consisting principally
of Messrs. Walton and Howe and Miss Hilda
Spong—produced “The Grip of Iron” at Sydney
Royal on Saturday. This unpleasantly designated
drama takes its name from the big paws of the
boss strangler—an affable person who goes round
choking people by hand. When this fact dawns
on the public a good deal of the interest drops out
of the piece. The swollen head and the staring
eyes and the out-hanging tongue of the strangled
person are not actually visible, but none the less
they seem to pervade things generally, and, more-
over, the strangler constantly shakes his shapeless
extremities horribly above his head, until they
seem to suggest an octopus giving three cheers and
whirling his tentacles. This blood-boltered per-
sonage is a respectable, middle-aged law-clerk who
suffocates his patients by night or in the dewy
morning, and pursues his legal avocations during
the day. Among the various anomalies of his
character the most remarkable is the unreasoning
manner in which he marries his pet daughter to
his partner and fellow ghoul, in the blessed hope
that they will live happily for ever after-
wards. Then he bestows on the pair the pro-
ceeds of a long and honourable career of stran-
gulation, and being convicted in his respectable
top-bat and his green spectacles he starts for New
Caledonia. But on the way he learns from a
fellow-passenger, who has been convicted on per-
jured evidence, that his daughter is being habitu-
ally dragged round by the hair in the mansion
which was bought with his money, and the tiger
wakens within him, and he runs amok all over the
ship and goes overboard. Then it transpires that
the fellow-convict is the strangler’s former son-in-
law ; they arrive at the mansion just as the
daughter has been butchered in evening dress by
her husband, and the curtain falls as the strangler
chokes his old partner and laughs a maniacal
laugh while he treads on the tail of the corpse’s
coat.
* * *
There are several strange discrepancies in
the story, which hangs together rather badly in
places, but no one takes much notice of them. The
great flat hands of the strangler overshadow
everything else, and when he whirls them in the
air, slab-fashion, they are an awesome spectacle.
Walter Howe’s rendering of this part is far above
his usual average; he is consistently gruesome,
and somehow suggests the gentle gorilla when he
has captured his enemy, and proceeds to flap him to
death with his large, moist palms. t Geo. Walton’s
detective is a gay but unconvincing burlesque, and
Miss Hilda Spong, with a small and unsatisfactory
part, is chiefly remarkable for a swoon which is
done at an unhappy angle and presents nothing of
the lady, save her boot-soles, to the audience.
The rest of the company, with the exception of
Mrs. Trve Hayman, consists of a number of de-
pressing individuals who struggle hard against the
burden of their own identities, but with compara-
tively little result. The fellow-ghoul is foolishly
addicted to giving his name at house-doors while his
mate is strangling the house-father inside, and to
leaving the plunder lying around in insecure
places. The lawyer is abjectly credulous. Thede-
tective has an idiotic habit of shouting “ white-
wash,” for no earthly purpose ; and the horizon of
the stormy sea has a sharp corner in it which is
not true to nature, unless nature has got badly
bent since The Bulletin last saw her.
** * *
Good old “ Maritana ” was roused out of
her repose last Saturday to do another turn of
service at Sydney Opera House. The general
verdict was that the ancient opera is still going
strong for its years, but, at the same time, it needs
a pretty long spell at grass in between each ap-
pearance. “Maritana” is more a reminiscence
than anything else —a thing thatawakens pleasant
memories in the old, hairless father, and makes
him think of days that have departed. Mr. Jules
Simonsen’s Don Caesar is sung in excellent style,
and even the old, old wail about that tattered
cavalier’s desire to fall upon a plain sounds almost
like a new wail under the influence of his fine
tenor voice. As for Mr. Simonsen’s acting, it
takes care of itself in a somewhat ramshackle
fashion, and gets through anyhow. Edward
Farley’s Don Jose is the same grand old baritone
villain as in past days. Maritana, by Miss
Colborne Baber, is a somewhat uneven character
who pursues her melody over merged country;
and that small, bright maid. Miss E. Lam-
bert, is a specially picturesque Lazarillo, with
a voice which will some day deserve a front
seat among the choirs of Paradise. The Bul-
letin’s views about that discouraging despot, the
King of Spain, are regretfully suppressed. The
“Grand Duchess ” follows to-morrow (Friday).
** * *
There is still no news about the with-
drawal of “ Charley’s Aunt,” which seems likely
to fill the Criterion for some weeks yet. “ A Bad
Penny,” the one-act episode which serves as a cur-
tain-raiser, also pursues its lamentable way up to
date. It is a brief story concerning a beery wreck
who calls and truculently demands his daughter,
and not getting her he weeps tears of cheap gin
upon the piano, and gathering up his exploded hat
goes out into the stormy world and comes back no
more—never any more. The rest of the party
stand and listen as the flap of his torn boot-soles
is heard upon the stair, and when, at last, they
shuffle into the unrecognisable distance the curtain
goes down, and the purposeless little item is
ended. It isn’t even well acted, and the pathos of
it is all embodied in that sound of departing feet
and broken boots upon the stair.
*# * *
“Beauty and the Beast” is still filling
Sydney Lyceum to the doors, and its oppressive
gorgeousness apparently hasn't even begun to pall.
The rival show, “Jack, the Giant Killer,” at Her
Majesty’s, will be withdrawn in another week or
two, though no date has yet been fixed. Then
that good old story, which never becomes al-
together old, of how G. Kignold was unjustly
accused, through the machinations of A. Norman,
of bigamy with violence, or horse-stealing in the
third degree, and how he nobly bore up against
the charge in an attic, and finally cleared his
■character and came back to the moated grange,
while Norman was arrested in his fatally-beautiful
bat, will begin to be told once more —The Bulle-
tin trusts with profitable results,
** * »
An ultra-fashionable concert is to be held
at Sydney Town Hall, on February 2, in aid of
the almost destitute children of the late Mr. and
Mrs. La Meslee, who were so unhappily drowned
by the foundering of the Ripple. The arrange-
ments are in good hands. Mr. \V. Ridley, of the
Government Statistician’s Dept., is treasurer ; W.
H. R. Cherry and J. P. Gandon are hon. secs.,
and M. Albin Villeval is musical organiser.
Among the artists who have promised their ser-
vices are Misses Colbourne Baber, Josephine
Deakin and Cecily Staunton, and of the inferior
sex, Messrs. Kowalski, Wiegand, Henri Staell
(violinist), Edgar Strauss (cellist), Claudius Des-
louis, William Walshe, Frank Adams (a new
English tenor), Harry Fitzmaurice, J. T. Dono-
van, and Alec. Edward. The proceedings are under
the patronage of Governor and Lady Duff, Mayor
Manning, Premier Dibbs and Ministry, Critchetb
Walker, all the Foreign Consuls, and everybody
who is anybody, or thinks he is. •
** * *
“True Metal,’' a long, rambling melo-
drama of the poor but honest kind, had a gracious
reception at Melbourne Alexandra on Saturday.
It moved a bit stiffly for want of more rehearsal
and stage management; an incidental mule tried
to back its cart into the Thames, and a terrific
thunderstorm was out of gear, but such troubles
are peculiar to first nights. A boy in the rag-and-
bone line gives touches of frowsy nature to this
production, and sends the smell of the rubbish-
box across the footlights. He also acts as a
moral antidote to a pair of dingy outcasts who
live by conspiring against the hero, the hero being
an unsober nephew, with little to recommend him
beyond his chivalry in “ shouting ” a new donkey
for the boy aforesaid. A millionaire villain is
remarkable for alwpys waiting round the corner
to arrange more plots with bis dingy minions,
whilst uncle Frank Cates, in holy orders, and an
elderly miller, with a daughter who loves her
young man at intervals, are each and all of them
remarkable for nothing in particular. Murderers
are conspicuous by their absence, neither is the
bitter cry of betrayed womanhood heard in the
land. “True Metal” deals for the most part
with meat-bones, furtive pen’orths of gin and
pangs of hunger. Food is so scarce in the second
act that a ravenous old female would eat rats if
she could catch them, and her husband’s mouth
waters at the mention of cold black beetles.
Dramas written for the East End of London
audiences seem to grow more hungry, homeless
and bare-footed every year, whereby hangs a
moral. Miss Dora Mostyn is a complete success
as the rag and refuse boy, her sniff of recognition
when she comes across some bones that she has
met before being true art. Dan Barry and Miss
Sydney make a convincing couple of dead-beats,
and the hero, a smaller edition of ’Gustus Glover
in voice and bearing, has the finest “stouching”
method of any man on the Australian stage, He
aims one lightning blow with his left, and the
object of attack appears to be knocked out clean.
Several other people, including very minor
characters, do credit to the piece, which, like its
predecessor, would be unendurable if not natural-
ised by a fair all-round company.
»» » »
Pantomimes are still prospering. The
Royal show, so far from being eclipsed by the
firm’s more expensive spectacle, is in a fair way to
become known as the cheeriest and most indefatig-
able panto, seen in Melbourne these last six years.
Maggie Moore, Martyn Hagan and the rest of the
company, without exception, work like people who
have one common end in view. A spirit of humour
pervades the latter half of the proceedings, where
new ideas —one new idea is better than ten
new costumes—blossom in the wilderness from
time to time. R. Stewart, senr., as a pagan
emperor, is a genial influence, and the harle
quinade is a brief burst of activity, terminating in
a general fall off the roof. “Sinbad the Sailor ”

At the Princess’ they are still suffering
from the belief that success is a mere question of
cash outlay—a satisfactory faith as long as the
public continue to accept it, and the bubble
reputation remains intact. Here the opening
scenes are the best, and gilded dullness is the rule
for the last hour or two. Perhaps “Red Riding
Hood ” touches its lowest depth of inanity
about 9.30 p.m. when the flow of proces-
sions is interrupted to permit of the wolf and
fox interviewing Violet Yarley. The times when
this pantomime grows anyway funny are few
indeed. Also, they are solely dependent upon
Courtneidge, whose “ fat ” is none too greasy, and
whose disposition to raise a laugh receives no en-
couragement from his surroundings. A man in
skirts must have lively individuals to play up to
him, or, at least, meet him half-way. A monkey
performance by one Pongo, said to be the wire-
jumper’s brother, was introduced a few nights
ago, and wisely abandoned the night following.

An association of the Melh. Stage Stricken held a grand
Shakesperian performance, in costume. They could not

were visible on the screen.

“ Bothwell,” which Swinburne published in ’74, is the
longest play in existence. One speech alone covers 14
pages. The play is full of the most magnificent poetry,
and the last scene between Mary and Darhley contains
the most really dramatic of Swinburne’s work.

“ Charley’s Aunt ” has been produced in Germany, and

A man masquerading in skirts comes well within the royal

An actor says of a certain skilled performer in mid air,
that his thoughts never soar higher than his business.
Having learned all the business necessary to secure a
good annual income his mind would degenerate into a
blank but for the daily task of studying sundry poles
and ropes to satisfy himself that they are “all right.”
When the “ traps ” have been duly tested his intellect
goes into vacation for the next 24 hours.
Maria Jones, the deceased Gaiety “old woman,” was
breaking up when she left Australia some 15 months ago.
Her fat was of the dropsical order, and her incidental
diseases were many, yet she appeared a jolly, wholesome
sort of soul upon the stage. The “boys and girls” of
the Gaiety Co. affected to think that Maria was old enough
to be the mother of them all (which she was not), and
dutifully presented her with a gold watch before she
sailed.
Melbourne Age, in a burst of renewed friendship
towards the Princess’s panto., published a yarn about the
“distressing position ” of an acrobatic performer, whose
foot got caught in a rope loop when he was on the point
of lowering himself to the stage after completing his act.
This incident was painted in high colours, and with the
Age’s broadest brush. The audience, unaware of the
man’s awful danger, applauded his entangled foot, “but
those who realised the true state of affairs ” trembled for
the consequences of his being “kept long in a position
with his head down 11!” After a few seconds, “anxious
seconds,” the suspended person disposed of the loop
“ with a lucky kick, and came to the stage as quickly as
possible.” The enthusiastic reporter on the thrilling ad-
vertisement lay is indeed funny. What’s the matter with
an acrobat who remains hanging with his head down
awhile? One of the Silbons seldom hangs any other way.
Trapezists in general suspend themselves by their toes,
and seem to enjoy it. Men can stand on their heads for
fifteen minutes at a stretch, drinking stout and cracking
nuts meanwhile. We’ve seen them do it—on a tight-rope,
to boot.

Dear Bulletin, — Going well and strong — nearly con-
cluding tour—and leave for England to get lot of new
matter, and then at once for Australia. Although I have
done fair business here, yet the whole place cannot com-
pare to Australia as a land to settle in. Johannesburg is
a dirty, unhealthy dust-heap, and I was sorry to see so
many Australians out of work. You may he very sure I
placed them all on ray free-list and showed them some
Australian scenes. How glad they were to see the
monotonous Australian bush after the hot, interminable,
treeless plains of S. Africa I They all want to get back.—
Yours, G. H. Snazblle, Port Elizabeth, 10th Dec. 1893.

The Italian opera co.|s Melbourne concert was, in one
way, a melancholy thing. [ltalian operatic romance
evaporates in dress-suits. He who looks a hero strutting
in mulberry-coloured knickerbockers among Sicilian
(canvas) mountains, looks absolutely idiotic in a claw-
hammer. Under the fierce glare of the concert platform,
romantic illusion vanished with a screech. Caruson, in a
dress-suit, looked like a first murderer, and sang like
one. Giraud looked a very ordinary person oppressed
with the conviction that he had no business to be there
at all, and didn’t know what to do with his hands.
Signorina Del Torre, in a low-necked dress with puffed
sleeves, and an unconsulted roll of music in her hand, was
like champagne in a milk-jug.

Charles Coghlan and an actress of flippant reputation
in London dropped into the showy Continental Hotel
restaurant for breakfast. They had finished, and had just
stepped out, when a cab was driven furiously up and
stopped in front of them, and Mrs. CJoghlan, the actor’l
wife, jumped out and faced the culprits. Without turn-
ing a hair, Mr. Coghlan looked at his wife inquiringly for
a moment, then raised his hat, bowed to each of his com-
panions in turn, and said, suavely: “As you ladies
doubtless have something of a personal nature to say to
one another, I will not disturb you.” Then he stepped
into the cab and drove away.—Ex.
Arthur Garner is going to establish a first-class music-
hall in Sydney-. “I purpose,” he says, “presenling an
entertainment equally artistic and amusing, attractive
alike to young and old, and one that can he witnessed
without bringing a blush to the cheek of the m< st
modest of her sex. It is impossible to convey to the
untravelled and narrow-minded critics the rapid strides
made during the past few years in the artistic and social
position of this branch of the profession. The buildings
are handsome and well-ventilated theatres, surrounded
with comfort, and luxurious in their appointments. Men
of respectability are no longer afraid to take their wives
and families, and even Royalty itself has given its patron-
age. The atmosphere no lomrer reeks with the fumes of
stale beer and rank tobacco, but all interest is centred In
the stage, where a constant succession of trained artisla
receives the well-merited applause of the delighted spec-
tators. It is on these lines that I propose establishing a
musio-hall in Sydney.” Nice news for the theatres.

Australia’s most popular actress—we are now speaking
of the gallery as well as the dress-circle vote—became so
very enthused on New Year’s morning that she, for luck’s
sake, kissed and hugged the good-natured assistant-stage-
manager, because he happened to be the first dark man
she met on entering the theatre. Three other super-
stitiously-inclined principal ladies, for “luck’s sake,”
followed suit, much to the dismay and astonishment of
the a.-s.-ra.
“What devils we women are ! ’’—Charley’s Aunt.
Clever Annie Pixley, who lately died in London, was
regarded almost as a daughter of California, though she
was born in New York. While still a child she made
frequent appearances on the stage in variety performances
with her parents in the mining-camps of California ami
Nevada. During a visit to Australia she met and married
Robert Fulford, and when they returned to ’Frisco they
both became members of a local stock company. In 1878,
she appeared in the title-rdle of a dramatization of Bret
Harte’s “M’liss," which proved popular for a decade or
more, and six years ago she found another success in
“The Deacon’s Daughter. She tried comic opera in ’91,
but without success.

Maorilander, while at the Empire Palace, a most gorgeous

Tenor Giraud cares not if his clothes are thrown on
with a pitchfork and tied with string, so long as they are
pretty and bright. No fancy colours like “elephant’s
breath,” or “ eau de nil plain red and yaller are quite
good enough for him. His pet aversion is reporters ; the
sight of one puts him in a fury. He strides his room
shouting: “Ha! bastard!” and stabbing them with’a
dagger, or slashing their throats with his sword.
Caruson cannot sing if a button or a spangle is missing
from his clothes. He comes down to the theatre before
anyone else ; dresses, and hunts for lost buttons. If his
dress had as many as the Pearly King and one came off,
he w-ould rush to the wardrobe, sh tiling—“ Madame !
von button,” and the show must stop till it is sewn on.
A certain muscular danseuse is furiously jealous of all
rivals, and wants the best of everything in Die wardrobe.
If not, she sulks ; if one has a beautiful dress, slie must
have a better. The town must be ransacked for trim-
mings to make her’s the dress of the night. When all is
over and she has all she wants, she struts complacent Iv
and sings—with a voice like a woodpecker who lias found
a rich lode of grubs.

land town; her fattier did not like the demoialising
influences of stage life. The writer watched her working
over the fence, and she wheels a harrow-load of bricks or
clay with a graceful technical knowledge which must
have been inherited.

A letter written by the condemned wo main
Frances Knorr, has been published by the Vic.
authorities. The object of the letter is to warn
others, and “to act as a strong deterrent.” The
letter bears evidence on its face of having been
manufactured at the instigation of somebody. A
woman of Knorr’s education and position would
never talk about “strong deterrents.” Such
phrases belong to the lingo of the Penal Depart-
ment, and characterise the letter as a fraud.
Who worded the letter for her? When you
have a helpless woman stuck up in a rat-trap you
can get her to do anything. Probably the same
source that brought forth certain confessions
brought forth this deliciously fraudulent attempt
to pass off this letter as a letter voluntarily issuing
from the condemned.

The Wild Cat Column.
The A.M.P. Society has decided in future to
estimate the probable average rate of interest on invest-
ments at 3i per cent, instead of 4 per cent., as heretofore.
This will necessitate an increase of reserves and a tem-
porary reduction in the bonus rates, but the reduction
will probably only last a very few years, and in view of
the steady reduction in interest rates throughout
Australia, the wisdom of the proceeding is unquestion-
able. Another wise innovation is the placing of male and
female lives on the same footing. Hitherto women have
been charged a higher rate than men, according to an
old-time English theory that the risks of maternity made
them a specially dangerous speculation. In Australia,
however, women, on the average, live longer than men,
and experience proves that the extra charge should rather
be put on the other sex.
