The long-postponed drawing in connection with
the “Mutual Freehold Property Distribution Society of
Sydney ” came off, in a mildly unobtrusive fashion,
on Friday (January 12). The alleged big prize—a
block of buildings at the corner of Park and Castle-
reagh-streets, Sydney—was dropped out of the lottery,
and so were 3000 other prizes, the biggest thing
left in being valued at a humble £5O. The
whole distribution consisted of £2230 worth of
property divided into 206 prizes, of an average value of
£ll. To the far-off investor the joy of getting £ll worth
of land, which, if realised by forced sale in a depressed
market,’ would probably only realise £6 or so, is
not very thrilling, especially if the happy winner
travels far to see his property, and puts up at a
hotel while he is looking for a purchaser. The
hundred investors who have got £5 worth of land
apiece will probably find the estate hardly worth calling
for—it is a depressing thing to win £5 worth of land
these times, and the lottery which gives out such things
is a miserable irony. The society professes to have dis-
posed of a trifle over 2000 tickets, which, in a concern of
145,000 tickets, is a painful breakdown. Further, the
“ad.” stated persistently for months that the prizes were
“largely rent-producing," and it would be interesting to
know just how rent-producing a £5 allotment can be—or
even a £5O allotment, for that matter. Taking it all
round, the “ Mutual Freehold Property Distribution
Society of Sydney” is about the most unsatisfactory thing
of its kind The Bclletix ever met with.

A large Australian institution has decided that
in future the managing director is to get £lOOO per
annum; the other directors—four of them—£l2s each ;
the manager, £500; and the auditors each £lO 10s. a
year. How much supervision is likely to be got for ten
guineas, and how much a £lO 10s. auditor is likely to
find out about the possible irregularities of a £lOOO
manager, are points that the shareholders can seriously
consider when the liquidation comes along. When £2OOO
worth of board wants to be watched by only £2l worth of
auditor it suggests several hundred things which need
not be further alluded to.

The New Oriental Bank, which was supposed
to have burst on strictly square principles and to be an
instance of purely undeserved misfortune, appears to be
pretty much like any other insolvent bank. There is talk
now of £1,000,000 which was advanced to rotten com-
panies, and there were personal loans of a wild kind
on shady securities —one set of influential people, for
instance, borrowed £BO,OOO, part of which, at least, is
among ttie bad debts, Also, there was much riotous in-
vestment in gorgeous premises and much opening of
unprofitable branches out of a mere desire to get ahead of
some other bank. The report of this eminent British
institution begins to read just like that of an Australian
land boom bank in liquidation, when Simpson is over-
hauling the accounts.
# • «
The lamentable Metropolitan Bank (Melbourne)
is now expected to produce just about 6d. in the £ for its
creditors—perhaps a trifle less. This is the awful institu-
tion which, a few months ago, some sanguine boomers
wanted to reconstruct on the ground that it was too
valuable a bank to be wasted. The shareholders, of
course, get nothing. The busted Metropolitan Bank and
the equally busted Metropolitan Building Society were
tangled up together in an unearthly fashion, and the bank
premises—vaguely estimated to be worth£l33,ooo—which
were “ taken over" from the Building Society, were, it is
now alleged, never legally transferred at all, because the
board, or somebody, quite forgot to attend to the matter.
Therefore, the shareholders of the Society are now thinking
of seizing the building and throwing the “ bank" into the
street without compensation. Also, the bottom has
utterly fallen out of £500,000 of other Metropolitan
“assets,” and the whole affair seems to be so hollow as to
be hardly worth liquidating.
There is something picturesque about the
Metropolitan’s style of hanking, though it isn’t much
more picturesque than the ordinary boom-bank system.
Taking them all round, the accounts seem to have been
kept by a Chinaman writing with his foot dipped in tar,
and the “securities” appear to have been mostly pre-
served in the coal-scuttle, and Qehazi, the auditor, appar-
ently look things pretty much for granted.
* « *
The N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
which has been already suspended for six months, is
further hung up till a meeting to be held in February,
the reconstruction having made no visible progress. The
present troubles of the L. and M. arise out of too much
brilliant financing. It owes money, first, to its current
account customers ; secondly, to its fixed depositors and
ordinary debenture holders; thirdly, to a set of special
mortgage debenture holders who, in 1892, advanced
£1,300,000 on the strength of an extra copper-fastened
lien on the uncalled capital; and, lastly, to the 8.N.Z.,
which is secured by the deposit of tangible property. The
ordinary debenture-holders are just beginning to realise
for the first time that they only come third in the eventof
liquidation, and are sour and disagreeable in consequence.
The holdersof theextracopper-fasteneddebentures, having
a lien on all the uncalled capital, won’t allow any of it to
be > called up except to pay their debentures, because if
it is called up for any other purpose it will diminish their
security'. And as for the Bank of N.Z., it positively
declines to listen to the voice of the charmer and give up
or vary its securities under any conditions. The brilliant
financier who tied up the great Loan and Mercantile in
all these hard knots now sits somewhere and knocks flies
off his ear with his hind leg, and considers hard upon the
situation.

The Melbourne bankruptcy returns for 1893 are
interesting reading. There were 51 compositions regis-
tered during the year, and 487 sequestrations, the total
liabilities being £3,362,945. This doesn’t include the
fearful and wonderful schedule of Miss Elizabeth
Spence: “Liabilities, £2512; assets, £3,772,408 ; surplus,
£3,769,896,” which is probably left out on the ground
that it is a purely humorous item. Also, it doesn’t
include several hundred “ liquidations,” covering several
millions of alleged assets and liabilities. The compositions
include three oases where Id. in the £ was paid on
£185,861; one instance of 2d. in the £ upon £11,950; five
of 3d. in the £ on £189.217; eleven of Od. in the £on
£279,016 ; one of Bd. in the £ ; three of 9d.; thirteen of Is.'
one of Is. IJd.; four of Is. 6d.; one of 25.; two of 2s. 6d.*
one of 35.; one of 3s. 6d.; one of 75.; and one of 19s. 6d.’
These last were all for small amounts; the big boomers
never went beyond Is. 6d, How the liquidations and the
sequestrations will turn out will be accurately known
about ten years hence. When the whole account is made
up it will probably be found that in 1893 Victoria went
broke for about ten millions, and paid eighteenpenoe in
the £ all round.

A Bendigo meeting lias re-opened the question
of preference share representation in Bank of Victoria
management. Under existing articles of association the
unfortunates who are compelled to hold £lO preference
shares can t assert themselves against those original
shareholders who are keeping their bank going with other
people s money. Six hundred and seventy-four ordinary
shareholders carry 3678 votes, whilst 2950 preference
partners in the concern have only 1688 voles among them.
Xhe arrangement is a monstrous scandal on the face of it
but nearly all reconstructions were faked on similar lines.
People dragged into the proprietary against their will
made no attempt to take up their proper position as chief
shareholders. The common or garden species of depositor
lost his head and was treated as the merest juggins in
the matter. 6

Dear Bulletin, —Be the losses of that big firm
and other similar matters, the “How was it managed?" is
easily answered. In the fat times I was a member of a sweet
little land-syndicate of about a dozen. Hard faces every
one. We would laugh at the idea of anyone finessing us.
Well, we took our manager, treasurer, and secretary from
the counter of a finance company, where he was earning
his £2OO a year, and gave him £6OO. He was a keen,
smart young fellow, and just suited us. Now, our
transactions, though big, were not numerous, and we
were all auditors, so to speak. That is, one or
the other was continually dropping into the office,
? ■ Pf e P 1,1 K uito the books, watching one another in a
friendly sort of way. What possible chance would you
that sweet young man had of working a point?
Yet, in eighteen months, he managed to “pinch" to
the extent of just on three thousand. He had a
duplicate pass-book, bill-book, ledger, everything. Also
an innocent method of attaching the butts of the paying-
in slips (forged) to the pass-book, and we simply kicked
ourselves when we kicked him out of the office. If with
us, mind you, who all fancied ourselves “smarties," and a
.airly controllable account, how much easier with hi®*
concerns with tens of thousands of yearly cash trans-
actions? At pres nt, city skilled auditors are worked
to death, so great is the scare. But I can point to
dozens of huge concerns, whose balance-sheets for years
have been made up in their own offices. One colossal
place especially has not had an outside audit for six years.
—Old Times.

The S.M.H. has advanced in a leader some
brilliant new ideas on diamond-mining. Cablegrams, Capt.
Rogers, and Streeter’s visit have hypnotised the Herald.'
“ Diamond-mining here is nothing in comparison with the
Cape.” Very true. “ But we do not know what Tingha
Inverell and Bingera have to reveal when the workings
are transferr d to syndicates which can test the ground.”
Another wholesome truth. “We do not know what
quantities of diamonds may not exist ; where some have
come from there may be other and better diamonds."

is here; it is his son who is looking at the N.S.W. diamond-
field. The cable-man hasb ?en playing 1 some serious jokes
on the people of Bingera. It appears that the Rothschilds
are not chasing the octogenarian diamond-king, that no
diamondiferous wash has been treated in England, and
worst of all bad luck, no co. has yet been formed in Lon-
don. Streeter pbre, says of the Bingera diamonds that he
hopes for the best, but it is almost impossible to cut these
stones. And that, so far, he can find no one to contract
for cutting quantities.

Mount Bischoff tin-mine put out 2400 tons tin and paid
£61,500 in divs. There is every indication of 1894 being
even more prosperous.
The West Coast is steadily forging to the front
despite the low prices for silver, lead, and tin. The s.s.
Tamho (1000 tons, drawing Oft. 6in.) now successfully
trades between Sydney, Hobart, and Strahan. No smelt-
ing is being done on the West Coast, all ore being sent to
other ports for treatment. This is against the field, as the
price of bags, sewing, handling, &0., is a big expense.
Local smelting will yet be a necessity. Two smelters were
erected in the boom days, but both have been liquidated
long since. The December shipment of ore from Mount
Lyell is worth £3OO per ton. This is shipped direct to
London.
• # *
Two men killed by the famous creep at the
Proprietary. “ Binney’s Cut,” the locality of the mishap,
is a 70ft.-deep quarry, the bottom of which has been
falling into the old workings below for months past, each
“movement” generally bringing a portion of the sides
down as well. The managerial gag that each fresh sub-
sidence really amounted to nothing at all, besides having
been “foreseen,” grows threadbare. Besides, a good
Steam crane was capsized by this creep.

to \vhom the Barrier owes much. Like every retrenched
mine-manager, he now turns Westraliawards.

Zebina Lane, who went to West Australia on
behalf of a Melbourne syndicate, has just returned. He
thinks a lot of the field, and says almost every reef cut
shows good gold, but that whenever a prospector strikes
a decent reef, he sits down and waits for the inevitable
syndicate to come along and take it off his hands. Zeb.
says the camels on the field are the poorest specimens he
has struck for a long time, but, for all that, they com-
mand tremendous prices for freight. He has got a couple
of properties for his syndicate.

N.S.W. Mines Minister Slattery is sending
more State foasickers on to abandoned goldfields. You
get your tools and fare, and take your choice. If you
fail, as nine out of ten do, you sell your pick, shovel, and
tin-dish, and walk to Sydney, and get another ticket and
outfit.

Though there are many antimony-mines in
Japan it imports large quantities of Star metal. With
State metal-works, N.S.W. could ship direct to Japan. At
present the province railroads and shipsso percent, waste
to London. Thence to Swansea and olher smelting dis-
tricts, then the metal goes to Japan and other parts of tha
world ; the worker pays all freights and charges.
Mr. C. Ernest Robin has reprinted seven
literary essays in a tiny volume. The subjects
dealt with are Shakespeare’s incidental characters,
Hamlet’s sanity, Bret Harte, Rudyard Kipling,
the new humour and the short storv. Air. Robin
shows knowledge, sympathy, and a capacity for
taking pains; but his insight is not deep, nor his
inspiration lively.
Dear Bulletin,— The wattle-and-mistletoe
verses which you illustrate in last issue, crediting
to the Kangakoo, a Sydney “ amateur ” papsr,
appear in “ A Century of Australian Song,” over
the name of D. B. W. Sladen, and constitute one
of the few poems by that ingenious poetaster
worth remembering. Don’t forget that the
kangaroo has a most catholic pouch.—Yours, A.

A correspondent :
Ed. Bulletin,— Re land-law administration in N.S.W.
Central Division. Wanted published, a plan of the pas-
toral holding surrounding this place (Derry) showing how
the run was divided under the ’B4 Act, and the large area
of reserves on this run ; also the way in which settlement
is blocked by the reserving of such large areas of such
well-placed and excellent agricultural land. This holding
(Ganmain) comprises about 200,000 acres of the finest
wheat-growing land in N.S.W., having a frontage of 20
miles to the Murrumbidgee; the Sydney-to-Hay line
passes through it. The lessees are paving Government
4d. per acre for the leasehold ; yet land is being let for
wheat-growing at ss. per acre in the locality I—Selector
Derry, Jan. 11, 1894.
In “ Barabbas,” Marie Corelli deliberately
appropriates the original idea of W. W. Story’s
great poem, “A Roman Lawyer in Jerusalem,”
his argument being that Judas betrayed Christ in
order that the latter’s divinity should at once be
made manifest. Corelli put this idea into the
mouth of Judith Iscariot, an apocryphal sister of
Judas, whoso influences him. Nat hi ess, Corelli
scores sometimes. Peter, raving at Judith over
the body of Judas, cries: ‘“God forbid that I
should show any mercy to the wicked ! ’ ‘ There
spoke the first purely human Christian! ’ mur-
mured a low, satirical voice,”
Dear Bulletin,— Re ivet sheep disputes in
shearing sheds. I was rather amused at “ Angle-
dool’s” statement that ‘‘some shearers vote
against their own opinions (re wet sheep) to further
what they imaginejto be the boss’s interests, viz., to
get shearing over quickly.” I can recall many in-
stances of some shearers voting ‘‘wet sheep” while
at the hut, and immediately afterwards, under the
boss’s eye, voting “ dry sheep.” The man in that
case, evidently, was fearful of incurring the boss’s
displeasure. That is one kind of “wet sheep”
shearer. There are generally one or two of him in
every shed. Another kind is one who has his
name in another shed following the one he is in.
Still another is the grab-all. Another—the
“ cdckey,” who wants to get home to dig his acre
of potatoes. Again the one—“ I’ll shear if anyone
else will”—sort of chap. Then he whom Mr.
Kitely llwing has wisely sent a long way from
his (the shearer’s) own locality, by train, and who
is obliged to work a dead-horse—his fare—and
then graft to get something for himself. This
latter variety is a recent addition to the “wet-
sheep ” shearing fraternity. I have found all these
and many more in my time, as a shearer, but have
yet to meet “ Angledool’s ” specimen. I have
seen bales put down twice, in the wool-press, to
squeeze all the water possible from the wool.—

“ It is a terrible thing to wake up at night,
when you are sleeping alone, and be compelled to
say, ‘ There’s a rascal in this bed.’ Ingeesoll.
About 20 years ago, at
Bendigo, a certain young
man hearo Jiev. Charles
Clark lecture on Dickens.
When the accomplished pro-
prietor of the childlike alto
voice reached the climax of
pathos in the “Death of
Little Nell,” he was seem-
ingly overcome by emotion,
and, taking from between his
well-ironed broadcloth and
his spotless shirt a little
snow-white handkerchief, he
slowly, sorrowfully, wiped
his eye. And then the
writer, who was a miner,
and bad no little, white
handkerchief to absorb the
tears which then welled up,
furtively wiped his eye,
partly on his grimy coat-
sleeve and partly with the
raspy back of his wrinkled
hand. That pathetic dis-
course was so sweet a memory
that when Parson Clark
again visited Australia two
or three years ago the scribe
went to hear him discourse
on Dickens once more. And
behold! at the very same
place the pathetic parson
wiped his eye again with
apparently the very same
snow-white handkerchief fur-
tively withdrawn from the
self-same recess between the
broadcloth and the fine linen.
But this time the writer
didn’t weep. He could have
slain that parson with a
brick. The pathos was all
in the business —it had been
there for 20. solid years.
There are but two fc>in-
gle-taxers in the present
Maoriland Parliament—
Buick, member for Wairoa,
and O’Regau, member for
Inangahua. Both are natives
of Maoriland, and.are among
the foremost local platform
speakers. Buick is a car-
penter by trade, and about
27 years of age. O’Regan is
24, and of magnificent phy-
sique. Three months ago he
was working as a railway
navvy, and threw down his
tools to become editor of a
Reefton paper. His attitude
on the platform recalls
pugilist Slavin; and the swaying of his arms,
his erect pose, and the earnest expression of
his countenance, all seem to convey the impression
that if anyone in the audience isn’t pleased with
Mr. O’Regan’s talk, he can “get satisfaction the
other way.”

according to the Constitution of England, the'
direct heir to the throne has to be born in the
presence of his Grace of Canterbury? Ergo : If

Some years ago there was a
duel fought in Sydney. Charley
P , who had returned some time
from Fiji, was in a city bar when
the late Mick Pender entered and
wanted to know what he meant
by being there, threatening to
throw him out. This Mick could
•easily have done, as he was a big
man not averse to pugilism, while
P was a particularly little
fellow. P allowed that Pender
could throw him out, and suggested
that pistols would put them on an
equality. To this Pender at once
agreed. So pistols were procured
and loaded, and, as they could
hardly fight where they were, they
agreed to hire a trap and drive to
a suburban hotel and there engage
a room to kill each other in. it
was dark when they started. Mick
■drove. During the drive Charley
■came to the conclusion that she
wasn’t worth it, and, as he had
charge of the pistols, determined to
draw the bullets-which lie did.
They duly arrived at their destina-
tion, engaged a room and locked
the door. Much to Charley’s sur-
prise some plaster fell from the
wall an inch or two from his head.
iSomehow or other a missile had
been left in Mick Pender’s pistol.

Cardinal Moran, we read, strongly favours
private charity and objects to the public free
breakfast. The Cardinal is right. The public
free breakfast is an institution whereat the
hungry man’s stomach publicly jumps upon his
self-respect. It is as odious an institution as the
ancient convict iron-gang working on the highway
right under the eyes of free men.
The son of Julius Vogel was a member of
Captain Wilson’s party of brigands, recently
wiped out by the Matabele. Fate has been very
rough on Julius of late, and this last blow will
probably about finish the gritty old man. Poor
old Vogel has always advocated capital punish-
ment for murder. The Matabele, it seems, prac-
tise capital punishment for attempted theft.
Friedrich Emil Heinrich Wulf Krichauff,
one of the retiring members of S.A. Legislative
Council, who seeks re-election, is a true democrat,
He first entered p arliament as member for Mount
Bark in 18 57„ the fi rst Assembly under con-
stitutional Govt.-and even in those far-away
days he advocated Protection, payment of mein-
bers, and the liberalisation of the land laws. He
used to walk backwards and forwards to Adelaide
to attend to his Legislative duties, a distance of
25 miles each way. He resigned his seat in March,
1858, but in 1870 came back to the Lower House
for Onkaparinga, and he again resigned in 1882
because of a projected visit to Germany. In the
interim he had been member of a Ministry which
F. E. H. W. KRICHAUEF, M.L.C.
lasted 19 days. In 1884, Mr. Krichauff was once
more in the Assembly, this time as member for
Victoria, in which capacity he acted for six years.
In June, ’9O, he stood for the Council, and beat
all opponents; and now, despite a weight of more
than 70 years, he is in the field again with excel-
lent prospects of success. The Germans, the
farmers, and the Labour bodies will all support
him, for he is the real father of the Homestead
Block Movement in South Australia, and has
always voted on the side of the People.
Mrs. White, High Priestess of the Second
Adventists, in America, is the star of the Brighton,
Melb., religious camp. She is a short, stout, ora-
torical lady, something like Bishop Gpe, and quite
as good. She wears a velvet jacket, with her
watchguard outside, and has much the best tent,
on the principle of the Canterbury Archbishop, or
Pastor Bevan at the Chautauquake.
Australians who have seen Melba lately
say she is not careful of her voice. Instead of
dining off a raw egg and a spoonful of salad oil, or
whatever is the correct diet for song-birds, goii g
to bed when off duty at 7.30, and generally having
a worse time than if she were a boarding-school
governess, she attends receptions of all kinds, and
eats and drinks what she likes. But “the gods
are just,” &e., and Melba is not singing nearly as
well as she did this time last year.
One of Rudyard Kipling's “ other stories.”
At Simla, a dignified veteran Colonel, familiarly
greeted by a peacocky young globe-trotter as
“ Old Chappie!” sarcastically replied, “ Old, per-
haps, but Chappie, , never, sir!” This was
overheard, and passed into a local colloquialism.
People used the phrase whenever they could drag
it in by the shoulders, until it stumbled on a
catastrophe. There was singing and piano-playing
one night in a bungalow, and the oil-lamp went
out abruptly. Witticisms scintillated in the dark.
The Major’s daughter, sweet, single, 17, made
some stupid ventriloquial remarks. “ One of the
foolish virgins, my dear,” reprovingly said her
father." She, embarrassed, remembered the phrase
and spake aloud—“ Foolish, perhaps, papa, but
” Here the story snaps off.

“After 2L years of Protection,” says G. H.
Reid, “ is there one Victorian industry ready to
say to the Victorian people: ‘ Take off the duties;
we can stand on our own feet.’ ” Reid himself
belongs to the most strongly protected industry
on earth, the close corporation of the law, and, up
to date, he doesn’t show any signs of a desire to
have the duty taken off, or any yearning to stand
on his own feet.
Barton’s great Federation speech was
nearly broken up by one solemn remark from a
smileless Scotchman in the audience. It was a
hot night, and Barton had worked himself up io
a frenzy of bounding indignation. “ Why should
Federation be delayed ?” he asked, wildly; “what
are we waiting for?” “We are waiting till some
of our politicians are dead,” said a funereal voice
down below, and great beads of perspiration
dropped off Barton as he stopped to consider the
matter.
Ignatieff, the stormy petrel of Russian
politics, announces that Russia will presently de-
mand that Austria shall give up Bosnia and
Herzegovina. This ought to be the beginning of
the great smash. For the best part of this cen-
tury Austria the Unready did nothing but cede
provinces, and it was a new sensation when she
got a chance to gnaw away a bit of the Sick Man’s
empire. Anybody who tries to get this acquisition
from her might as well try to deprive a tiger of
his raw ox-liver by hand,

HONEST GOVERNMENT.
SIRENRY SPEAKETH: “Alluding to what a system of govern-
ment might be, he said it might be oligarchical, it might be
despotism, it might be aristocracy, or it might be as nearly as

Lord Rosebery, it is now believed, will be
Gladstone’s successor as leader of the British
Liberals. If he is, British Liberalism will be put
back twenty years at the lowest estimate. Rose-
bery, just now, is probably the most liberal among
the two or three politicians who have brains
enough to be Gladstone’s successor. But he is a
peer, and, therefore, he must lead the Government
from the House of Lords, and no statesman can
sit year after year in that atmosphere of sloth and
old decay and utter sameness without uncon-
sciously petrifying, any more than a naked lord
can sit in a coal-scuttle without being defiled.
Lord Rosebery says, “It is really a politi-
cal crime that the news which is telegraphed from
the centre of the empire to its remotest limits is
not more accurately chosen.” It is indeed. The
cable news consists mostly of gossip about beetle-
browed sloggers, announcements of royal infants,
movements of the Prince of Wales, and the
thrilling statement that the Times is of opinion
that Australia, after due consideration, will find
that settlement on the land ought to be en-
couraged, while, at the same time, preserving a
due regard for the interests of the city population.
Also, now and then, there comes an announcement
that a peeress is going for a divorce, and that the
peer used to sleep in his hat, and use disgusting
language when he crawled home from his favourite
barmaid.
Wellington (M.L.) “ Union Parliament,”
in its mock address-in-reply to a mock Governor,
shows itself possessed of rather more sense than
the real Parliament:—
Conflicting views are held among us as to the various
measures proposed in your Excellency’s Speech. Some
of these proposals meet with the disapproval of gentle-
men on the same side of the House as the hon. member
chosen by your Excellency as our leader (hereinafter
referred to as the ‘‘lns’’) and some are approved by
gentlemen on the opposite side (hereinafter called the
“Outs”). But our first duty as members of this House
is not to our country or truth, but to our party, and that
duty demands of the Ins that they should endeavour to
stay in and keep the Outs out, and of the Outs that they
should endeavour to get in and turn the Ins out—each
party being united on the one common principle of
fighting the other. All the Ins will, therefore, do their
best to conceal their disapproval, and the Outs their
approval, of the proposals in question ; and each
measure, instead of being considered on its merits and
with a view to the country’s good, will be discussed solely
from the standpoint of party tactics, and be made a
mere episode in the eternal struggle between Ins and
Outs. The result will be that many had measures will be
passed, and many good ones will fail of passing ; the
process will be distinguished by a vast amount of bitter
wrangling, childish recrimination, and noisy insincerity ;
and the loudness of our clamour will be pointed to as the
measure of the interest taken by an enlightened demo-
cracy in its public affairs. Such a procedure may appear
idiotic in theory, and likely to be disastrous in practice,
but it will be good for the party which is for the time
being in the ascendant, and as it represents the time
honoured custom of our wise forefathers we intend to
adhere to it.

Democratic Premier King-
ston cut down the military dis-
play at the prorogation of S.A.
Parliament. The Lieut.-Gover-
nor was escorted to the scene by
mounted constables, and the
police band was in attendance,
while only the guard of honour
was composed of military men,
the Permanent force being uti-
lised to save expense. There
was no salute fired. Members of
the militia, who missed their
ordinary day’s pay, are furious
at what they term the robbery
perpetrated by the Government.
Hobart has a woollen fac-
tory with a great future but a
small present, and to help it
along it was decided that all the
police-uniforms should be of
locally - made cloth. Tenders
were called accordingly, and in
print the tenderers were warned
that only Tas. cloth would be
accepted. But in private the
tip was given that samples of
other cloth might be sent in, and
when accepting-day came round
it was the man who took the
hint that got the contract. Then
the resolution to use only Tas.
cloth in the uniforms was re-
scinded !
Cecil Rhodes, the Cape
filibuster, points out that “the
war against Lobengula was as-
sented to by religious bodies.”
The seems extremely probable.
Hasn’t every shady thing that
was ever done—including the
Crucifixion, and the burning of
Protestants by Catholics, and
the sawing off of Puritans’ ears
by Anglicans, and the flogging
of Baptists and Quakers by
Puritans, and all the rest —been
assented to by some religious
body or other ? For that matter
Lobengula’s massacres were also
assented to by religious bodies.
The Matabele rain-maker, or
witch-finder, or gree-gree man,
or ouganga, or whatever he is
called, is quite as much a religi-
ous body in his own way as the
Archbishop of Canterbury, only
he is greasy outwardly instead
of greasy inwardly.
Max Hirsch, the "Victorian boss Free-
trader, draws an inference from cement:—
If a man wished to make cement, but found that to
sell at the price of an English firm he must lose £lOOO,
he goes to the Government, who put on such a duty as
will not only allow him to sell without loss, but with a
profit. Say he realised a gain of £lOOO, then the people
paid £2OOO to enable the local manufacturer to make
£lOOO.
All of which is strictly true, for if a man wanted
to do something, and if he found out that some-
thing else would ensue, and supposing he then
fixed up a third circumstance, and say another
thing happened as a consequence, then something
else must inevitably follow. But the bald facts
of the case are that imported cement, when there
was no duty on it, sold in Victoria at 13s. per
cask, and the price of exactly the same article,
under a 20 per cent, tariff, is now 11s. Still
Hirsch’s statement is true in every detail.
If the thing had happened that didn’t happen,
then the circumstance which didn’t take place
would have eventuated.
Says the Southlander (M.L.) :
E. Withy, of Auckland, is a land-nationaliser, and
would allow present owners to retain possession for 18
years, when the land should revert to the State. A cor-
respondent of a contemporary says Mr. Withy is a wealthy
man—owning stocks, shares, securities of all descriptions
excepting land—and asks: “ Why should not these invest-
ments of all kinds also, after 18 years, revert to the
State ? If 18 years of possession is going to pay a farmer,
why is 18 years not going to pay Mr. Withy ?”
This is the old, old trouble. The Single-tax man
never owns any land, and has always a brilliant
scheme for saving his country by confiscating the
whole property of the other fellow.
WILLIAM SATCHELL (the gallant young athlete who
died in trying to save the unfortunate Hannah
M‘Lennan from drowning, at Melbourne, on Jan. 4.)
Betwixt the Britisher who goes
To foreign climes to wield his brand
And that vile wretch who slays the foes
That seek to steal his native land,
There is a wide distinction drawn :
One’s an assassin black as pitch,
The other is a hero born.
We cannot tell you which is which,
Confused by patriotic vapours.
Let’s hope that God don’t read the papers.
Is Life worth Living?
The following facetious reply to the above question,
“It all depends upon the liver,” is true in moie
senses than one. Most people have it in their
power to make life enjoyable or miserable. A
man’s happiness does not consist in a large bank
credit, or broad acres and smiling fields. All this
may be possessed and yet the owner may be un-
utterably sad. It is the sparkling eye that tak s
in the loveliness of nature’s dress, and the body
that is fortified with vigour and nerve that really
finds life a succession of joys and delights. It is
not to be expected that persons suffering witli
deranged liver can extract more than a modicum
of gladness out of years of pain and sickness
There is only one “ royal road ” to happiness, and
that leads by the way of health. Warner’s
“ Safe ” Cuke unquestionably helps thousands in
making life worth living by stopping the nervous
destruction and organic waste that certainly
follows at the heels of liver and kidney complaints.
It prevents a multitude of ailments and also acts
as a fine tonic to the exhausted body. Warner’s
“ Safe ” Pills are invaluable in all cases of lassi-
tude, weakness, or bilious attack. No person
should be without them, as they are of special
preparation and absolutely free from all injurious
ingredients.
The following certificate of Mr. Arthur Gordon,
originator of the Harbour Concerts in Sydney, is
appropriate here Sydney, N.S.W., February
22nd, 1893. I have much pleasure in acknowledg-
ing the efficacy of your “Safe” Cure as a remedy
for liver troubles, having been a constant sufferer
for several years from sluggish liver accompanied
by violent pains in the back and general lassitude.
After taking four bottles of “Safe” Cuke an 1
two vials of “Safe” Pills, I felt myself
thoroughly rejuvenated, my energy and aptitude
for business having returned with increased vigour,
my memory and mental faculties clearer than
ever, and I became a different man in every way.’’
M'Burney, Wallis & Co.'s Report.
Report. —For our opening sale of the new year we
brought forward a large and attractive catalogue. The
saleroom was again crowded with buyers, including
several from the Southern capital, and competition for
the better classes in good light condition was extremely
strong, but faulty lots were a shade easier. Scouredsweie
also in strong demand at prices quite equal to those
ruling prior to the Christmas vacation, and this also
applies to good greasy bellies and pieces. For a nice
parcel of greasy, well skirted and free, from the Yass
district, marked ED over Esdale, we obtained and

f Auckland, Thursday.—At Christchurch
last night a sensational incident occurred dur-
ing a performance at Fillis’ Circus. A Bengal
tiger was being put through exercises by Cox,
the trainer, who put his head in the animal’s
month, when it closed its jaws, inflicting a
painful wound op the trainer's face^
At the Roya’ Colonial iaslit-ite in London, recently, Bishop Webber, of
Queensland, said that Australia was a land of promise crying loudly for men.
and women.
N.&.W.: “Speaking tor myself, I should say mien and women yes, but not
for any more bishops and parsons.’’
Thus Secy. Teece at the meeting of the A.M.P. Society
“ Experience shows that among the general population of N.B. -
.Wales, female lives are better than male lives, at all ages, and
that is taking into account the periods between which women
are exposed to special risks. Now, in the case of males, the
deaths from accidents in Australia, are abnormally large.
The board, acting on the advice of the experts consulted, have
decided that in future male and female lives shall be treated in
exactly the same manner.”

Gold discoveries. Very rich fields have
been opened at Southern Cross, Coolgardie, Mur-
chison, &c., in West Australia, which will be one
of the greatest gold-producing countries of the
world. Land in Perth and Albany, the principal
cities, is ridiculously low in price, and must cer-
tainly increase very much in value in the next
few years. One of the best investments at present
time is to secure a few lots. The Intercolonial
Investment Co., Ltd., 309 Pitt-street, Sydney,
olb rs exceptionally well-positioned blocks from
JilO each on very easy terms, or for cash,
guaranteed title. The company has been estab-
lished in Sydney and Perth for many years, and
is well known throughout the colonies for straight
dealing. The manager is Mr. W. J. Douglas,
v ho will be pleased to forward plans to any
address, and, having a personal knowledge of the
properties, will select faithfully for intending
purchasers wterc required.

The rumours as to N.S.W Gov. Duff’s
intention to retire from his post at an early date
are quite unfounded. Gov. Duff will stay in N.S,
Wales seven years, if he can. He has arranged
his “home” business on that scale. Yet, if the
Parkes influence came back to power, existing
arrangements might, perforce, be altered. Parkes,
extra-confidentially, speaks clearly on the use of a
competent Governor as a link to'the Empire, but
of an incompetent one as a curse to Britain and
Australia. The conclusion, therefore, is that if
Parses or his influence comes back to power, Mr.
Duff must, not will, retire from office.
* * *
At Melb. District Court, the other day,
a ships steward gladly undertook to marry the
mother of his cheeild. Matrimony was suggested
to him, by a police inspector, as a rational means
of escape from the necessity of finding sureties for
carrying out a maintenance order, and he posi-
tively jumped at the idea. Hymen seems to have
a lot of friends in court. Also, it may be said (for
the sake of an epigram) that the police make more
“ honest women” than honest men.
* * *
Balaam: “The Seventh Day Adventists,
now camped near Melbourne, prove to be a collo-
cation of worthy and healthy citizens and
citizenesses, with a Jewish notion of keeping two
Sundays in the week, and an idea that a little tent-
practice may be good for the next generation.
The local cognoscenti are much interested in the
shadow pantomimes at bedtime, for the ladies
will insist on putting the light on the wrong side.
But they keep up the services so late that ballet
connoisseurs have to leave or miss their trains.”
* * *
Dear Bulletin, —I want to know how it
is that there are no Jewish policemen in Australia.
I have never encountered a solitary specimen,
neither can I find anyone who remembers one.
Yet, on the Continent (Europe) the Jews enter
the gendarmerie in numbers. As there is money
even in the common “cop” business please reply
promptly.—Yours, C.J.

# • «
Members of the 4th Regiment Band,
composed principally of coal-miners, at present
unemployed, had to do a house to house canvass of
Newcastle, recently, to get their Xmas dinner.
1 hey were the winners of the Centennial Band
contest, and are admitted to be the crack amateur
band of Australasia.
* * *
A Sydney solicitor was consulted the other
day by a lady, who wished to know whether she
could claim increased damages for breach of pro-
mise of marriage, on the ground that, to enable
her to enter into the engagement with the pros-
pective bridegroom, she had gone to great expense
to obtain a divorce from her former husband.

It moves. N.S.W. Justice Stephen in
Chambers. Motion—change of venue. Granted,
and two guineas costs allowed. Counsel: “ But,
y’Onah, counsel’s fees amount to two guineas.”
His Honor :“I don’t care. An attorney could
have attended to this simple matter. An articled
clerk a week old could have obtained the order.”
* * »
You can get a shower-bath on the Murchi-
son gold-field (W. A.). There is a bucket with
holes in the bottom rigged over a tub. The pro-
prietor pours water in the bucket and it drips
over you into the tub. Then he dips it out of the
tub, and repeats the process. Tariff : 2s. 6d. to
6d., according to priority.

# * *
An Auckland (M.L.) girl fell off the wharf
one night recently, and was nearly drowned, when
a young man jumped in and rescued her. The
hero immediately mingled in the crowd and was
lost. Verily a thoughtful and far-seeing young
man. There is nothing as dangerous to the
liberty and peace of a young man as the undying
gratitude of a marriageable damsel.

In certain Australian trade journals, which
only circulate amongst “well-informed people,”
there can be seen advts. notifying that some
companies have for sale white spirit “for Island
trade use.” That is to say, it can be sold as rum,
gin, brandy, &c., to suit the tastes of customers.
« • *
A Vic. millionaire built a superb mansion
for a lady to whom he was engaged, but she threw
him over, and he lately managed to marry another
lady, under the persuasion it was built for her.
Having found out that it wasn’t, she has actually
left him !

The girl-bicyclists who ride (“boy”
fashion) so neatly in Melb. Princess’s are said to
have had only three weeks’training before making
their first appearance on Boxing Night. They
graduated at the sewing-machine, very likely.
* • *
There seems to be little doubt that the
Australian native quality of voice is favourable to
colonial twang. Boys and girls (especially girls)
are born with a tendency to adopt the accent that
in London is called “ streety,” and this is nearly
always noticeable in untrained singing. On the
authority of a choir-master, who has tested and
trained hundreds of boys, we have it that the irn-
ported lad can be picked from a dozen by the
fuller tone of his voice, which, however, is not
usually as clear and penetrating as the little
native’s organ. A touch of jews’-harp quality is
there, unpleasant in the raw state, but a very
TIME: SUNDAY MORNING.
TOMMY: “Say, Bill, come up ter Jock Winterses, an’ we’ll rob his garden
—he’s laid up.”
BILL : “Can’t. Gotter go ter Sunday-school, but I’ll come this after-
noon.”
valuable quality when properly educated. This
opinion comes from a musical artist who can
scarcely be prejudiced, for he- states that the
young colonial twangers are, as a rule, more apt
pupils than the boys he taught in England.

Despite the fact that Kingston is Premier,
Chinese laundries flourish in Adelaide.
* * #
Korumburra, the Vic. coal township, is
going ahead like a comet. Last week some repre-
sentative inhabitants applied to Government for a
duly qualified police-court. An enterprising
Korumburrite is prepared to erect a suitable
building, next door to a pub., if the Law will
undertake to rent it. They can’t do fairer than that
for the Law. Commodious premises adjoining a
pub.
* * •
When newspapers fall out the honest truth
becomes known. Melb, A rgus, re a hollow meet-
ing to express sympathy with the Age versus
Speight, says that “two of the jurymen in the
case occupied a prominent position in the meeting,
and one made himself conspicuous by shouting out
a denunciation of the Argus.” In the case of any
ordinary “demonstration,” such as the Robt,
Reid luncheon of a few weeks ago, we find daily
journalism conspiring to sup press everything that
could throw a little light on the farce. There have
been other occasions, too, when a little public
information concerning jurymen in a cause celebre
might have proved interesting, but the Argus was
then as uncommunicative as all the other “ great ”
rags.
The Sydney parson who, to improve the
atmosphere of public-houses, urges members of
the cloth to visit these places frequently, paves
the way to a suggestion from the publican side —
namely, that clergymen should allow bars to be
erected in their churches, and drinks to be served
before and after service. “Why don’t men go to
church ?”
* • •
A Sydney softgoods firm fines its em-
ployes 3d. per word for every objectionable word
he or she is heard using on the premises. So far,
the threepennies have been raked in solely from
the females.

One of the cele-
brities of Sydney is a
cow which comes
placidly down King-
street every day to be
milked at the premises
of its owner, a local
butcher. The presence
of this cow gives a
refreshing, bucolic as-
pect to the locality
and is reminiscent of
hay and green fields.
It is the calmest,
gentlest, most affec-
tionate cow that ever
meandered with a dis-
tended udder down a
city street, and its
name is Fanny or
Polly or something like
that. All the children
know it, and pull its
ears in salutation, and
the adult dwellers
thereabouts smack it
affectionately upon the
tail and pass the time
of day with the animal.
When it has called in
at its owner’s premises
and been milked, it
wanders back to the
Domain with the same
reflective step, and
hies it unto the grass.
Busses and cabs give
way to this cow, and
even trams stop to let
the kindly animal go
by, and only whistle
at it when its move-
ments are too gradual.
All the old, reliable
Domain loafers know
it, and they use it as
a pillow at night, and
milk it surreptitiously
into their hats in the
dewy morn ; and the
Domain policeman
speaks kindly to it,
and gives it a friendly
whack with his large
hand on the spine when
they chance to meet.
The King-street cow is an institution in Sydney,
and when it dies the place will never look quite
the same again.
# • #
Dear Bulletin, —The man who writes
tender love poems for the papers should never sign
them unless he is young and faultlessly beautiful.
For instance, I came across a sweet, mournful,
little thing the other day in a Maoriland paper,
in which the bard called on his lady-love to come
and let him rest his head upon her bosom. Then
he put his full name and address at the foot. I
know that head which he wanted to “ rest”; it is
large and black and bullet-shaped and rancid, and
when I saw the signature and thought of the
grease-in irk he would leave on anybody’s bosom
all the joy went out of the poem. Also, I know
the lady to whom he is engaged, and to whom, I
suppose, the verses were addressed, and her bosom
is flat and bony, and not the place where anybody
could rest in comfort. If I hadn’t known these
two people it would have been all right ; as things
were it was pathos mixed with hair-oil.—Yours,
&c., T.
# * •
A feature of N.S.W. Highland Society,
which makes one almost forgive and forget its
bare legs, is that no official save the assistant
secretary draws anything from the funds. Every
penny raised—with that small exception - is
divided among charities.

Oranges are now regularly imported from
China to Townsville (Q.), and find a ready market.
What next ?
* • •
A man, fined in Sydney last week for
driving a lame horse, pleaded in extenuation that
he also was lame (which was visibly apparent),
and was driven by employers who gave him no
indulgence.

Richard Speight will be the cause of David
Syme’s death before all is finished. In the first
place, Richard is going to wade in cheerfully for
some more actions on the ten undecided counts in
his indictment, and this ought to keep Syme busy
for at least a year. Then the Age has been going
to pieces over the case in a literary sense, for no
human reader can stand 10 columns a day of the
DID HE MEAN IT?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Did he mean it, when in broken
Accents he besought my hand ?
Words of love so thickly spoken
One might well misunderstand.
Do I see a new life dawning—
Shall I shortly change my name ?
When we meet to-morrow morning
Will his manner be the same ?
Tom is sober, shy, and shrinking—
Not a horrid, vulgar “sponge ” ;
P’raps he nerved himself by drinking
Ere he dared to “ take the plunge.”
In the morning —will he boldly
Want to kiss his “ blushing rose,”
Or regard me icy coldly ?
Did he mean it? Goodness knows.
E. F.

And now that the trial is over for the time being,
the Age is filling up with one dreary column
after another of letters of condolence addressed to
David by sympathising readers, which letters are
heavy enough to kill any but the most robust
publication. The wailing of Job’s three friends
is light reading by comparison.

The very latest clerical lament (from a
Ballarat parson) : —“ I cannot help it if the ladies
are too fond of me!” No, a man can’t help it if
the ladies are too fond of him —he can only grin
and bear it.

# • •
One superfine writer on the tiresome sub-
ject of Young Australia’s pronunciation wanders
into the grammar question, which, by the way,
has nothing whatever to do with the case. This
individual says—“ Not excepting English-born
people, how frequently do we not hear ‘No, I
don’t think so,’and such like? There is another
very common expression, ‘ I quite forgot all about
it.’ All these faults simply need correction.’’ A
fair sample of the strict grammarian’s patronising
stupidity. If it be possible to nearly forget all
about a certain matter (which means more than
nearly forgetting a good deal about it), then the
expression, “I quite forgot all about it,” must
pass as sound in sense. Again, a man’s “ I don’t
think so,” following on the heels of his “No,”
seems a legitimate qualification of the negative,
or we may take the “ No ” as being meant to give
special force to “ I don’t think so.” The answer
breaks no rule of grammar, whilst fully conveying
the intended meaning. The captious critic’s own
words —“ ho v frequently do we not hear ”do not
grammatically express the remark he intended to
make. He inadvertently commits one of the
“common (real) faults ‘that need correction.’”
In the moat cultured and careful manner he
manages to stultify himself.
* * •
The daughter of a well-known Sydneyite,
who was married recently in England, met the
widow of a Sydney University Don a few days
before the happy event. When she told the name
of the fiance the professoress said: “ I somehow
think I know something of the gentleman’s
family.” “Oh, dear me, no,” replied the young
lady with an ’orty snort. “ I don’t think it at all
likely that you know any of his people.” Collapse
of the widow.
* * *
Miss Fanny Ethel Stephen, daughter of
Mr. S. A. Stephen, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), was married
6th Deo. last to Mr. Delasalle, formerly Bunn, at St.
Saviour's Church, Sloane-st., London—rather a
fashionable wedding-shop—the Bishop of Brisbane
officiating. The bride looked very well, and
particularly happy, and was accompanied by one
or two good-looking bridesmaids. There was a
plentiful absence of “colonials,” for we all become
very English, you know, after a short stay in the
old country.

Murder will not always out. The chances
are that the assassin of James Unwin —poisoned
at the Glebe, Sydney, the other day, by having
arsenic placed in a whisky-flask kept by him in
his buggy—will escape. All the same, the law
believes that it can put its finger on the guilty
party.

The site of anew cemetery for Melbourne’s
dead will have to be definitely fixed within the
next six months. There are symptoms of an in-
tention to work a miserable little job for some
land syndicate, or other. And that wouldn’t be
the first swindle perpetrated in connection with a
cemetery reserve. Slices of “God’s acre” have
been sneaked in days of yore by some of Mel-
bourne’s representative persons.
« » «
Australian Star has been taking a census
among the Sydney booksellers to find out what
books women mostly read. The inquiry reveals
the lamentable fact that the mild and greasy
novels of Mrs, Henry Wood top the poll. If any
one set of books is more calculated to undermine
the character of a nation than any other set, it is
Mrs. Wood’s. There is nothing immoral about
her works, and nothing approaching the indecent,
but they are full of pappy deans and rectors, and
good little boys who grovel to the deans and
rectors, and consequently rise to greatness; and
squires who are held in profound reverence by all
around them, and people of the “ lower ordahs ”
who are respectful to the squire and are content
in that station of life to which Providence has
called them. Also there is much profound hat-
lifting and respectful touching of scalp-locks on
every page, and a horrid tone of salaam runs all
through them. This squashy kind of literature,
which always ends in a beautiful girl dying of
consumption with boiled-pap accompaniments, is
about the saddest thing in the British language.

One of the first results of that awful yarn
concerning the Irish boy named Ferdinand de
Freyne Rienzi de Courcey, who drowned himself
so that his mother might get the insurance, has
turned up at Wellington (M.L.). The son of a
retrenched military officer insured his life for the
old man’s benefit, and then took poison, leaving
the usual pathetic letter behind him. As it
chanced, however, he didn’t take enough poison.
The plucky and popular Mrs. Hiddilston,
for years associated with the bar of that favourite
old hostelry, Aarons’ Exchange Hotel, was, on
the eve of her departure from Sydney last week,
presented by a deputation of gentlemen, represent-
ing chiefly the brokers and merchants of the
locality, with a purse of 130 sovs., as a token of
sympathy and esteem. Considering the hardness
of the times and the fact that the subscription-list
was circulated in but a narrow circle, the substan-
tialness of the testimonial speaks volumes. /
Mr. Topham’s fine collection of high-class
pictures is to be auctioned on 30th January at
Messrs. Newton and Lamb’s rooms, Spring and
O’Connell Streets, Sydney. Meanwhile, they are
now on view, and may be inspected daily from
10 a.m. to 5 p.m., at Mr. Topham’s rooms, 42
Norwich Chambers, until the 20th January, when
they will be removed to their sale-rooms, Spring-
street, where they will be on view on Thursday,
25th Jan., from 9 a.m. to 5.30 p.m.; Saturday, 27th,
from 9 a.m. to 1 p.m.; Monday, 29th, from 9 a.m.
to 5.30 p.m., and prior to commencement of sale
on Tuesday, January 30.
Dear Bulletin, —Re Missing Word Com-
petition, No. 9, and “Quiz’s ” comments thereon.
It may interest him to know that members of my
family have, in previous competitions, “plunged ”
to the extent of 60 guesses on various words—
generally 40 was the limit—and then did not hit
the correct word. In No. 8 competition we staked
ten chances on one word—“ frivolous ’’—and 26 on
other words, and had no luck, the missing word
being “capricious.” Did “Quiz” notice that in
Truth’s last competition a Broken Hill lady
struck the right word three times ? “ Quiz ’’
must be a very young person. However, I wish
him better luck with his next Is. 6d. —Yours, &c.,
Bessie M. Broke (for Nora and Jack Becke),
Bungalow, Wharf Road, Balmain.

The famous Henry Smith Fish was elected
the other day to be Mayor of Dunedin. There
was an exciting finish between him and the
other man, Chapman, and towards six o’clock,
when the poll closed, the impression was that Fish
was behind. Just then a last Fish supporter was
unearthed and was raced furiously to the Town
Hall to vote. As he got on the steps the clock
struck the first stroke of six, and the wife of the
other candidate threw herself on to the last man,
and holding him by the coat-tail declared that he
couldn’t vote because the poll was closed. The
coat-tail, however, was torn from her clutch, but
candidate Chapman grasped the voter and de-
clared that he couldn’t be admitted. The Fish
faction, however, was desperate, and with a heave
it rushed its supporter and Chapman in together,
and they fell over the door-sill just before the last
stroke of the hour. The vote was allowed, and
then it was ascertained that Fish had a majority
of 318, so that a devoted wife and mother had
made her struggle on the doorstep to no purpose.
Quarterly Review publishes an article
on Burke’s “ Peerage and Baronetage,” showing
plainly that the pedigrees of most of the “old
families” of Britain are absolutely bogus—that
their ancestral trees are a pitiable example of the
abject struggles of plain modern beer to graft itself
on'to ancient blood. The Fife family, in particu-
lar, gets a bad showing up. It starts from one
William Duff, who was made a peer 140 years ago,
and called himself Viscount Mao Duff in order
to establish some kind of connection between him-
self and the legendary Mac Duffs of ancient times.
Next the family borrowed the crest of the old-
time Mac Duffs, and looted a sixteenth century
tombstone from Cullen Church—the tombstone of
one Innes—which was solemnly erected in the
Duff mausoleum with a bogus inscription and date
setting forth that it was the monument of one
Duff of their line, who was a red-legged cateran
in 1404, and died under tremendous circumstances
which reflected great credit on the family. And,
finally, the Duff family, by means of stolen tomb-
stones and bogus records, which were boiled to
rmjke them look ancient, fixed up an entire
.pedigree which joins them on to the original Mac-
Duffs, and carries them right back to the Deluge.
The Coulthart family, which was bowled out some
years ago, went one better, for it fixed up an
entirely new pedigree from the beginning, and
put up something like 24 tombstones in various
degrees of dilapidation and apparent old age, ask-
ing the passer-by to tread lightly on the bones of
imaginary Coultharts, who died as far back
as 600 years ago. The Dearden family put
up a “ family chapel ” in Rochdale Church,
and having bought old funereal brasses from sun-
dry marine stores, fixed up the tombs of its
alleged ancestors in long rows, with effigies, slabs,
and assorted monuments. Another family of long
descent is alleged to be entirely bogus; the real
family died out, and one Smith or Jenkins bor-
rowed its name and bought the old mansion, and
took over the goodwill of the pedigree. As for
the names they seem to be mostly bogus. The
modern De Grey is an abject Robinson ; Neville
Percy is Smithson (“ Duke Smithson of Northum-
berland,” said the Anti-Jacobin) ; De Winton is
a miserable Wilkins ; De Montmorency is Morres;
De Freville is Green ; De Capell Brooke is only
Supple; De Heriz is Smith; and so on. The
British peerage generally, despite bogus tombstones
and faked church records, is about as genuine as
the Italian count whose real name is Waffles, and
who used to be cook in a hotel, and the old
revered, much-grovelled-to nobility is dust and
ashes and plain Jones.
As to the argument that girls, if educated
for mercantile life, would displace boys, it amounts
to nothing. The boys must take care of them-
selves as they can. Society is not prepared to
protect the male sex at the sacrifice of the females.
There are a large number of callings in which
women are out of place. There is an inconveni-
ence in a woman becoming a lawyer; she does not
compete successfully as a doctor; she cannot be a
soldier, or a sailor, or an engineer, or a follower of
any trade which requires muscular strength or
physical endurance. The boys must be con-
tent with these. In mere barter and sale
there is no reason why women should not
hold their own against men. Mrs. Hetty
Green, who more than holds her own, is an
exceptional case. She is a financial genius. She
is a miser, and the daughter of a miser. All that
there is of her—and there is a good deal —has run
to money-grubbing. She concentrated her very
remarkable ability, many years ago, upon the
science of stock speculation, and she mastered it.
Her system was exceedingly simple. She never
sold what she had not got, and she never bought
what she could not pay for. She always kept an
enormous sum of money on hand to pick up bar-
gains in panics. Before she bought a stock she
studied it "through and through. She lay awake
nights pondering over flaws which might be found
in it. When she invested in a railroad she was as
familiar with its history as if she had built
it. She never trusted any man’s word or his
faith. She put her money in John J. Cisco’s
hands because he was an exemplar of financial
solidity far safer than the banks; and when she
dropped in on him, on one day of panic, for her
balance of a million or two, and was told that the
house would require a day or two to call in their
loans so as to realise so large a sum, she went out
crying, sat down on the steps of the office, with a
cotton umbrella in her hand, and called all passers-
by to witness that Cisco had her money and would
not return it. Mrs. Green spends nothing. She
is said to be worth forty or fifty millions, yet her
annual expenditure is set down at four thousand
dollars. She has been noticed to run after a
nickel she dropped in the street. She will
carry a vast sum in securities in her leather
bag, and walk with it to her bankers rather
than take a cab. Few servant-maids are as
poorly dressed as she is. This lady is no type of
the Woman of Business, and no formulas can be
drawn from her career. She is simply a grotesque
phenomenon. As a rule, women are not a success
as gamblers. Whether, as the gentlemen of the
green cloth say, they are too emotional to thrive
at a pursuit which requires self-control, or
whether their minds are not adapted to form long
calculations embracing many factors, it is certain
that successful female speculators are so rare as to
be almost unknown. But many women grow
rich by hoarding.— S.F. Argonaut.
Apology.—Getting rid of some truth by a
lie.

FRANK RENAR.
MRS. CALAP GOES TO SEE MRS. MILLS
And it was not for an hour's chat over a cup of
tea that she went to see her, but on a much more
serious matter. For Mrs. Mills had been quoted
in the newspapers as having said something which
might be of importance to Mrs Calap, and also to
others. Now the newspapers print so many things
that nobody can make head or tail of, that Mrs.
Calap thought the only sure way was to go and see
Mrs. Mills and ask her if it was true what was
said. What Mrs. Mills told her is contained in
the annexed statement made about a year after-
wards :
“I, Jane Calap, of 3, Vincent Street, York
Road, Leeds, do solemnly and sincerely declare
as follows: —
“In the early part of November, 1887, I fell
into a low, weak state. I was.tired, languid, and
weary, and felt as if something had come oyer me.
All my bones ached and I had so much pain that
I did not know where to put myself. I was con-
stantly vomiting, sometimes a green, bitter fluid
came away, at other times frothy water. I had a
dull, heavy pain at the right side, and the whites of
my eyes were a yellow colour, and my skin was
sallow as if I had the jaundice. I had an awful
taste in the mouth, my tongue and teeth being
covered with slime so thick that I had to scrape it
away. My appetite fell away, and after eating
the simplest and lightest food I had so much pain
that it nearly killed me. I had always great pain
and weight at my chest and through to my back,
also a gnawing, sinking sensation at the pit of my
stomach. I was greatly troubled with wind which
rolled all over me, and gave me so much pain it
was like spasms, for I could not straighten myself.
I gradually got weaker and weaker, and felt so
weak and exhausted that I could scarcely drag
myself along. As time went on I wasted away
until I got as thin as a match, and could barely
walk across the floor. I felt so downhearted that
I used to say I shall never get better any more in
this world. I took all kinds of medicines, but
finding myself getting worse I got a recommend-
ation to the Leeds Infirmary, where I was at-
tended to by several doctors, who gave me medi-
cines, which I took month after month, but I got
no better. The doctors sounded my chest and
lungs, and seemed puzzled with my sufferings, for
they frequently changed my medicine. Getting
no better I next went to the Dispensary in North
Street, and persevered taking their medicines, but
it was all to no purpose. I now gave up taking
physic, for I had lost all faith in it, and my
sufferings continued until January, 1891, when I
heard a neighbour of mine, Mrs. Ann Mills, 40,
Bread Street, having been cured (after the doctors
had given her up) by a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup. I went with my
daughter to see Mrs. Mills, who told me that
Seigel’s Syrup had saved her life, and would do me
good. 1 got a bottle of the medicine, and after
taking a few doses I felt relief. I continued with
the Syrup, and after taking three bottles all the
pain left me, my food agreed with me and I grad-
ually gained strength. I can now take any kind
of food and never feel any distress and am as
strong as ever I was. After my recovery a lady
customer of mine said to me “ Mrs. Calap, what-
ever have you been taking, for you do look so
well. ” I told her, as I tell everyone, that Seigel’s
Syrup has made me a new woman, and but for it
I should not be alive. I wish others to know of
the benefit I have derived from the medicine, and
I give full permission to the proprietors to use
this statement as they may think fit, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing
the same to be true. By virtue of the provisions
of the Statutory Declaration Act, 1835 (Will. IV.
c. 62.”
“ Declared before me at Leeds this')
“25th day of January, 1892. I
(Signed) Alf. Cooke, J. P. |,
‘ * Ex-Mayor of Leeds. ” J
(Signed)
Jane Calap.
The public may remember the account of Mrs-
Mills’ illness and recovery, published some time
ago. We are glad that Mrs. Calap heard of it and
went straight to that lady herself for the infor-
mation she wanted. The visit resulted just as
might have been expected. Both our good friends
had suffered from the same disease, indigestion
and dyspepsia, and the remedy which cured in the
first case was equally successful in that of her
neighbour. No wonder Mrs. Calap had lost
all faith in physic, and if Mother Seigel’s Syrup
were “ physic,” we should not look for people to
have faith in it either. But it is a remedy, not
“ physic.” It doesn’t upset and disgust, it soothes
and heals. Men fall ill, to be sure, but women bear
most of the pain in this sad world, and when once
acquainted they and “ Mother Seigel” are ‘‘ever
the best of friends,” like Joe and Pip in Dickens’
story.

Zealand ; and Agencies i.i Tasmania, India and the East,
the United States, Hamburg and the United Kingdom.
The Bank allows interest on fixed deposits; collects for
its customers dividends of public companies, and interest
on debentures; and invests money on their behalf in public
securities in the Colonies or Great Britain ; undertakes
the agency of other banks; issues drafts, circular notes
and letters of credit, and conducts all customary banking
business.
The Bank also issues Circular Notes in sums of £lO or
£2O, negotiable on the Continents of Europe and America,
or in India and the East.
GEORGE MILLER,

JR HIS, Company is being formed to take over, as a
i going concern, the business, now, and for the past
fourteen years, carried on by Messrs. BEALE & CO.
in Sydney, and throughout New South Wales, as Manu-
facturers, Importers, and Distributors of Sewing Machines
and Musical Instruments; also the Stock-in-trade, Bal-
ances, and all debts due to the firm ; the Machinery,
Plant, and all materials.
The concern has branches and travellers all over the
territory. Its success is so well-known that comment
is needless ; suffice it to say that the business will be run
on the same, lines in the future that have ensured con-
tinued success in the past, and the extended confidence
between the present firm and its customers will be
studiously maintained.
The volume of business has steadily grown, and during
five years the trade has totalled the enormous amount of
.£411,178, while the profits have been large and steadily
maintained. By the system of liens on goods sold until
fully paid for, the losses by bad debts have been reduced
to a comparatively insignificant amount.
With the business there will be handed over the
following properties: the title and interest in the present
firm of the freehold land and buildings situated at 482
and 484 George Street, Sydney, immediately opposite the
splendid edifice now being erected for the Central City
Markets. These buildings toim a striking feature in the
centre of the busiest block in the city, and for solidity
and quality of finish are unsurpassed. The portions not
required by the firm are let to substantial tenants.
Also the Factory and Stores at Nelson Street, Annan-
dale, which occupy freehold land 30 feet by 200 feet, and
leasehold 60 feet by 400 feet. The principal Factory,
with engine and machinery, stands upon the freehold. All
the machinery is specially applicable to the work ; there
are ample supplies of seasoned timber for future require-
ments. Finally the freehold brick store and stables Myrtle
Street, city. All these properties are taken over at cost.
Stocks and Plant at valuation ; and customer’s balances
with allowance for contingencies.
Messrs. Beale & Co. take the whole of their interest in
shares; no good-will is demanded; no preference or
promoters' shares. Mr. Octavius C. Beale will act as
Managing Director for five years at least.
All the firm’s Trade Marks, will become the property
of the Company : and the system of protecting goods by
liens until fully paid for, which has stood the test of years
will be strictly adhered to.
The results contemplated in converting the busines
into a Company are—(l) Expansion by introduction of
additional capital ; (2) Co-operation of interest amongst
a wide-spread body of shareholders ; (3) the securing of
perpetuity of the business.
A sound progressive concern is offered, the results of
which in pa-t years justify the expectation of 10 to 12J
per cent, dividends, besides providing Reserves.
Full prospectus and information may be obtained from
the following agents, who are authorized to receive de-
posits on shares.
BEA.LE & 00.,
482 & 484 dearye Street, City;
And Agents in every City and Town throughout New
South Wales.

In London, Id. will buy a ’bus-ride for
two miles, lib, of sugar, a hot beefsteak pie, loz.
tea, a tie, candles, 11b. soap, your photograph,
a bundle of fish, or almost any book from Shakes-
peare to Dickens.
The “Canadian Club Whisky,” which is
compounded by Hiram Walker and Sons, at
Walkerville, Canada, has at least one powerful
recommendation—it has received the unanimous
approval of the last Australian Eleven, and what
these cricketers don’t know about whisky is pro-
bably not worth knowing. On their return
journey the Australian cricketers visited the huge
Walkerville distillery, and were much staggered
by what they saw. In Canada the Government
exercises a parental watchfulness over whisky—it
adopts it like an orphan, and brings it up tenderly
by hand. When the liquor is distilled it is
promptly seized upon by the State, and put in
bond to mature. It is watched over by the State,
bottled, corked and labelled by the State, or under
State supervision, and a stamp is affixed, whereby
the State stakes its immortal soul upon the age and
purity of the liquor. The process is not compul-
sory, but the whisky which is not thus guaranteed
is despised and sold as a cheap and inferior article,
and, therefore, the system of State supervision is
practically universal. The Canadian Club
Whisky is matured for five years—as attested by
the Government stamp—in oak casks, in ware-
houses with a capacity of 80,000 barrels—equal to
million imperial gallons ; and is sold by whole-
sale agents all over the world, from Monte Video
to Christiania, and from Guatemala to Japan.
Han-old Brothers, of Sydney and Adelaide, are
the N.S. W. representatives.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
TheN.S.W. contingent was very successful at
the Maoriland swimming- championship meeting, held
at Auckland, on 13th and 15th inst. W. J. Gormley won
the 880 yards and 220 yards in splendid style ; time,

# * #
The history of Abe Hioken’s fight with Foley
is known to few of the present day sports. Abe was a
member of “the rooms” in Melbourne, and it used to break
the heart of Joe Thompson, himself a born bully, for him
and his party to have to knuckle down to Hicken, under
the threat of a “slouching,” when any argument arose.
Joe commenced to cast round for a take-down of Master
Abe, and upon the advice of Jem Mace the match
was made with Foley. It is history how the men
sparred for over half-an-hour, Hicken leading for the
body and Foley drawing back in that peculiar style
of his, seemingly taking the hit each time, hut as a
matter of fact scarcely feeling it. Foley drew the old
’un on properly, until the right moment arrived, when
he drove the left fair on to the “mark,” and as Hicken
fairly doubled up, smash across came the right on the
side of the head. This virtually finished the fight.

« * «
Acting on a Supreme Court decision, the S.A.
police hold that betting, save through the totalisator, is
illegal; so after every race-meeting the “books” are
prosecuted. At Adelaide police-court 50 layers were
hauled up and fined. This policy has killed the small
meetings and crippled the big ones. With juvenile
Israelites who aspired to grow up to manhood and succeed
to pa’s tobacco and betting business, another happy
dream has vanished.

The Bulletin does not now stand quite
alone in the press on the capital punishment
question. Sydney Daily Telegraph in a leading
article on the butchering of Mrs. Knorr :
While the woman was awaiting her doom, the concern
tration of the public mind upon her case was intense, and
the circumstance of her sex appealed in especial degree
to the morbid sense of a certain order of mind among the
multitude. With such it was an absorbing topic of
conversation week after week, in the streets and in
many homes, in the hearing of, or participated in by,
young as well as old. What must be the natural, if not
the inevitable, effects of such prolonged converse on
themes so gruesome and forbidding? The reflecting will
be impressed by the danger that the results will be the
excitement of the lower and grosser depths of morbid
sentiment—as indicated by the debased craving evinced
for every detail of the wretched woman’s last moments—
with consequences so brutalising as to tend to increase
the murderous element already more or less present in
the community. The execution which took place yester-
day is a terrible incident to happen in the nineteenth
century, and while it is true that so long as the
capital sentence is maintained, and must be visited on
murderers irrespective of sex, it becomes a serious
question whether this form of vindicating the law does
not result in producing more crime than it punishes.
Amen 1 The gallows is the father and mother of
murder. The State, personified by Butcher
Patterson and co., can’t employ three hangmen to
choke one wretched, brandied-up woman, who
comes drunk on to the scaffold squawking “Safe
in the Arms of Jesus” without blood-manuring
the seeds of madness and homicide throughout
the land.
