A Tale of Three Skeletons.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
Hk;h up on a lonely, inaccessible shelf of rock
in the Maoriland Alps may be seen a gathering of
skeletons. At least they might be seen there were
it possible for anything unfurnished with wings
to gain so much as a bird’s-eye glance at that crag
which hangs in the air, midway between earth
and heaven. But the precipitous wall bulges
out above and obscures the view from overhead,
even supposing there were any place overhead
where a man or a goat could hang on and
consider things ; and the same precipice swells
out again beneath, and blocks the view from the
pass below. The pass itself is narrow, and no one
ever climbs the mountain on the other side of it,
partly because no one ever comes that way—
partly because there is nothing to be gained by
climbing, for the whole mountain would not sell
for a cent in the open market, being merely
good road-making material in a region where
there are no roads—partly because, if anyone
should scale that ghastly pinnacle it would only
lead him to a razor-backed ridge, and the ridge
itself leads nowhere—and chiefly because even the
prince of darkness himself, hanging on grimly
with horns and claws and tail, would find
no footing there. Consequently the one spot
which commands a view of the ledge and the
skeletons, is a spot which no one will ever reach,
and even if it could be reached no one would ever
come back again from it to tell what he had seen.
And the skeletons rest up there in peace
and the years go by, and the seasons come
and go. And in rain and shine and thunder
and wintry storm they sit and look out on the
vast monotony of the Alps, and ponder on the
silent, changeless majesty of the great unchange-
able mountains. They look as if they themselves
were wondering how they had reached that
amazing elevation, for neither in front nor behind
is there any possible pathway, and above and
below there is nothing but bare, slippery, over-
hanging rock. They are wrecked among the
clouds, and until the general resurrection they
will remain in their weather-beaten niche undis-
turbed.
There are six of them in all. But only three are
men. The rest are horses, and the horses do not
count. They are planted in single file, for the shelf
is just long enough to accommodate them, but not
wide enough to admit them two abreast. The man
in front—or the dry and creaking wreckage of what
was once a man—still sits on his tattered and
rusted saddle, but the animal beneath him has
collapsed, and is but a heap of bones held together
by the remains of its ancient harness. The saddle
sprawls on top of these remnants, and the man
sprawls on top cf the saddle with his skull
curiously jammed into an angle of the rock. He
has one leg stretched out behind him, and one
extended in front, as if he had died in the midst
of some acrobatic feat, and the rags of an old
brown hat are still upon his head. Also there is a
leer on the ruins of his countenance, and his pipe
is held rakishly between his teeth; it is also
jammed against the rocks, and unless he swallows
it he and the pipe will be found together, just as
they are now, on the day of judgment.
The next man in the line is also sprawling above
the remains of a horse, but he is lying on his back
and the rain has drifted in at his jaws and his
disused eye-holes, making a sort of high-tide
within his fleshless skull. When there is a spell
of dry weather he evaporates, and in wet seasons,
on the other hand, he is in a state of perpetual
overflow. It is evident this process has been going
on for a good many years, for his head is nearly
half-full of an alluvial deposit left by many suc-
ceeding storms. Otherwise there is nothing re-
markable about him. Pass on.
The third member of the party is practically
undistinguishable, for his horse is piled up on top
of him, and all that can be seen of him is not even
enough to found a reasonable tale upon.
There are a few other properties, almost too
small to be worth mentioning. A rusted billy-
can, the fragments of a gun, and a half-mast rag
that might once have been a blanket are about
all.
And for the rest, there is silence above and
silence underneath, for the winds seldom reach
that secluded shelf of rock which is suspended
between sky and earth, and the rushing
river at the bottom of the gorge is so far
off that its murmur is undistinguishable. There
is little light and much weird shadow in the
narrow pass, and the sunlight only creeps in with
difficulty, and the moon plays fantastic tricks
with the little group that sleeps on the un-
reachable mountain side. And when there is
neither sun nor moon, and the only light is the
gleam of the icy stars, a new life and expression
seem to awake in one at least of the three dead
men, but it is only the radiance shining through
the empty eye-holes of the individual whose head
is half-full of water and silt, and reflected back atan
acute angle through his half-open jaws. It gives
a sort of semi-humorous cast to his countenance,
but humour is wasted in a place where even a
goat could not climb up to join in the mirth.
And considering how little amusement there is in
the earth beneath him, it seems a wasted emotion
that the dead man should laugh his unchanging
laugh on the mountain with never an audience to
bear him company.
It
There were three of us in all, and we had gone
forth into the waste places of the Southern Alps
to look for gold. But all through the spring and
the early summer we wandered further and
further among the mountains, and the gold was
nowhere to be found. We prospected among the
rocks and cliffs, and on the bars of many a name-
less river, and up many an untrodden gorge, and
everywhere we panned out twenty hundredweight
of road-metal to the ton —only that and nothing
more.
Jack Hamilton was the leader and guide and
general support of the party. He acted as guide
because we were all equally ignorant of the
country, but Hamilton could plunge into an un-
known land with a grim and blasphemous con-
fidence which made up for his ignorance; and
though he was guiltless of geography he had
acquired by long experience a capacity for
talking about things he knew nothing of, whereas
I had mostly confined myself to subjects with
which I was more or less acquainted, and lacked
the ability to expound things 1 had never heard
of. Jack s strength lay in this one speciality, so
—as already mentioned—he virtually commanded
the little expedition. He was a weather-beaten,
dusky individual of 40 or thereabouts, and his
other qualifications were a wide, far-reaching,
complicated humour, and a power of misquoting
Scripture which was never equalled before or
since. Where he came from, or why he came
from there, I never knew.
Percy, the next member of the company, was
also remarkable, inasmuch as his other name was
Adolphus. He was a long, pale, silent British
youth, and a conspicuous failure in every way
that one man could fail without hiring out-
side assistance. He had been exported by his
uncle, who was alleged to be a nobleman and
almost the only remark which he ever made by
his own volition—except when he was roused to a
sort of simdy anger—was that he intended to
write for money to take him home. Owing to the
circumstances hereinafter mentioned, however,
he never did write, and if his uncle wrote to him
the letter is not likely to be delivered, lor he has
gone far beyond the beat of any eartuly postman.
The third member of the party—myself—was
noted for nothing at all except the conspicuous
foolishness which led him to clamber with a
pickaxe over a damp hillside on such an errand,
and in such company.
And so when the summer began to wane, and
the first breath of the chilling autumn was felt in
the breezes that swept down from the Alps, we
gathered up our camp and started for some newer
and wilder regions than any we had yet visited.
111.
“Boys,” said Hamilton emphatically, “it’s all
very good for you to laugh, but I told you we’d
find that mountain yet, and by the immortal
heavens —there it is.”
“That mountain” was Jack Hamilton’s pet
craze. He was filled with a tale concerning a
certain legendary peak which he had seen years
before, and somehow lost again past all recovery.
There was a deposit of gold there —great masses
and hunks of purest gold, and for some elaborate
reason he had brought none of it away with him
on the memorable occasion when Providence threw
the treasure at his head, in a manner of speaking,
and he had neglected to pick it up. His story
varied a good deal, and the mountain grew higher
each time we heard of it, and the gold more
abundant; but as far as 1 could make out the
original circumstances were something like these.
Jack Hamilton was a new chum at one time —an
unsophisticated lamb digging promiscuously in
the most unlikely places, and vainly plunging
around with a spade and a pickaxe upon the face
of Nature. He found nothing in patticular at
this period of his life, not even himself sometimes,
for being fresh and harmless he was unable to
turn a corner and find his way home again after-
wards. And in consequence of this failing he lost
himself badly on one occasion, and wandered
away among the hills, and there led a precarious
existence upon bark and grass and assorted
insects. By the most vigorous and ceaseless walk-
ing, and by rushing over every obstacle that came
in his way, assisted by the fact that he had taken
the wrong direction at first, and had succeeded —
though steering by the sun—in keeping wrong
ever afterwards, hecontrivedatlastto bediscovered,
delirious and almost dead, in quite a new part of
the country. Then the Samaritans who picked
him up conveyed him many miles to an hospital,
and left him there without staling where they had
met him. And in consequence of all this, when
Jack Hamilton woke up at last, and remembered
about the mountain, and the gold upon it, which
he had seen or dreamt of in his wanderings, there
was no geographical information to go upon.
He had grown middle-aged and horny and
profane since then, but he had never found the
lost peak and the lost millions. When our pro-
specting expedition began to grow weary and
unprofitable, he told Percy and me about the
great secret, and we also grew enthusiastic
for a while, but we had seen not a trace of the
gold, either. Yet, according to Jack’s description,
it was the sort of mountain that nobody could
pass unnoticed. It was square and vertical, and
on three sides absolutely inaccessible, while the
top seemed to be sawn right off as if some one had
removed the original summit and then planed it
smooth. And, most surprising thing of all, there
was on the fourth side a gently-sloping road
—it had probably been a hideously precipitous
path in the first place, but it had been growing
and expanding in Jack’s brain for years, until he
was almost prepared to swear that there was also
an iron gate and a knocker, and a hotel with
choice refreshments, and that ham and eggs grew
wild on the mountain side, while the ice-cream
blossomed on the trees. And about half way up
this track was Jack’s gold-mine—a species of cave
with all Golconda lying about loose inside.
We had long since lost faith in the mine—Percy
and I. We believed in it at first, when Hamilton
laid it before us one day as a bald, unvarnished
statement; but the next day he wandered into
frantic details about the millions it contained, and
we grew dubious; and the day after that he threw
in so many surprising facts about the scenery that
we became sceptical. And a little later he
told us in a solemn tone about the road, and
gravely propounded the theory that his mine was
the original mine of Solomon, and that the
road which led up to it was the work of some
Israelitish engineer, and then we set him down as
a bad description of lunatic, and prayed that we
might escape him, and get back safely to civilisa-
tion. But unluckily we were lost among the
mountains as effectually as Hamilton himself had
been when he made his great discovery, and that
experienced maniac was our sole hope and reliance
if we were ever to find our way back. So for day
after day he dragged us through a horribly in-
accessible country, and we followed him wearily
and in profound disgust.
And now, when we were utterly weary of the
whole enterprise, and would gladly have shunted
our guide into an asylum for dangerous cranks,
and sold our share of all the prospective millions
to be anywhere away from these endless wastes of
rock and sodden vegetation and desolate cliffs, the
mountain of gold was looming up ahead of us at
last. A moment before we would as soon have
expected to see the Himalayas in front of us, or
the peaks of Lebanon. Then the mist opened,
and straight ahead was a great square mass of
vertical rock, with the summit sawn straight off
as if the upper peak had been cut away and thrown
into the depths beneath.
We were toiling along through an enormous
cleft in the hills, when this vision burst upon us,
and on each side of us the cliffs rose like a wall of
inky blackness and blotted out the sunlight.
There was a little stream, or rather a little noisy
river, meandering through this pass, and we
scrambled painfully amid the shingle which lined
it on either side. When we came to a patch of
comparatively easy travelling we mounted our
packhorses, for there was now so exceedingly
little to carry that they were packhorses only in
name; and when the track was difficult—which
was usually the case —we dragged them after us.
As it chanced we were all three in our saddles
when Jack’s great discovery rose on the horizon,
and instinctively we pulled up and sat glaring
wildly into futurity.
Then Hamilton gave a wild, awful, joyous
execration that made the rocks ring and re-echo,
and kicking his bony black horse into a furious
scramble, he tore away and left us.
Then I said nothing, for I was too full of sudden
bewilderment for words, but I pounded furiously
on the ribs of my dejected steed like a man wlm
pounds his steed in a dream, and rushed after
him.
And finally Percy gathered up his scattered
faculties and followed us, and as he followed he
threw out a wild exclamation into the air.
“ By Jove ! ”
IV
Yes, the legendary mountain was there in al
reality. And, more wondrous still, the road was
there too, winding in a curious zig-zag fashion
away up in the misty air until it was lost among
the clouds. And as to the gold we never doubted
it now, even for a moment.
It wasn’t much of a road after all, but it was
enough for our purpose. It was cut in the vertical
face of the cliff and it afforded abundant room for
one horseman, but very little for two. It had
once been smooth and exceedingly regular, but
long centuries of ruin and frost and thunder had
dented it, and rotted away the edges, and filled it
with cracks and slippery places, and here and
there it seemed to have been repaired by some
rough and ready artist in masonry, and his handi-
work was now falling slowly into pieces. But it
was a road, and Jack Hamilton’s dream was
coming true after all.
“ I knew it,” he yelled, with such a yell as the
old rocks had not heard for ages. “I knew I
would find it. It’s all there thousands —
millions—hundreds of millions—it’s been waiting
for us all these, years, and now we’re the richest
men on earth. We are—we —”
“Yah —ha—aha —yaha ! ”
It was a strange, long-drawn, musical cry that
came from some tremendous distance overhead,
and apparently a dozen pairs of human lungs were
combined in the chorus. And it was so sudden
and wild and unexpected, that Percy turned his
horse’s head there and then, and fled for a quarter
of a mile down the pass.
“ What in is thatV’
Then we both sat and listened, and as we did so
the same cry rose again, and swelled and rolled
with a solemn reverberation through the air, and
awoke wild, musical echoes among the gnarled old
Alps.
“ Yah —ha—aha—yaha ! ”
“ By the Immortal—we’re too late ! Our claim’s
jumped !”
All the joy and energy went out of us in an
instant. Hamilton turned an ashen grey, and
wiped great beads of perspiration off his forehead,
and as for me, I climbed slowly out of my saddle
and sat down helplessly on a boulder. It seemed
as if I had actually owned all these millions only
a moment ago, and now they had dissolved and
fled away into absolute nothingness, and all that
remained of that magnificent wealth was an old,
battered, grey horse, two ancient blankets, and a
billy-can.
“ Let’s go on, anyhow,” suggested Jack. “We
may as well see what’s happened. But if there’s
anybody up there they must have found the claim
long ago. Nobody but a corpse could miss it, and
a pretty old corpse at that. O Lord, the gold’s
just staring you in the face —heaps of it—piles of
it. Well, we may as well look at it anyhow, even
if we don’t get a cent. But, after all,” and here
lie brightened up visibly, “ there may be only
three or four fellows altogether, and if that’s
so we’ll get a share or there’ll be a row —such a
row there will be. Come on; we’ll thin out these
pioneers a bit if we’ve any luck.”
“We haven’t any luck, Jack,” I answered.
“ That’s just where it is.”
Then we started up the hill, in a dejected pro-
cession. Percy came back cautiously, and tailed
on to the rear of the caravan, but he was obviously
scared and uneasy, and his ears seemed to stand
up more conspicuously than usual on either side of
his head as he listened, and sniffed the air in all
directions. But there was no repetition of that
wild, pathetic wail that had come apparently
from the clouds, and, stranger still, there was not a
footprint to be seen anywhere, nor a single thread
of smoke against the sky, nor was there the echo
of a pick stroke to be heard in the silent atmos-
phere, and the old stillness of the untrodden Alps
bad settled down once more on all our surround-
ings. And the ancient highway seemed to be
crumbling to pieces beneath our horses’ hoofs, and
even when we came at last to a yawning gap
which we crossed with difficulty and considerable
danger, there were no signs of any attempt at
repair or improvement. When Hamilton saw
this he drew a long, long sigh of relief.
“Boys,” he said solemnly, “I believe it’s all
right after all. If anyone was working the claim
he must have left some signs, and there hasn’t
been a foot or a hoof on this road for years and
years. And as to these voices it doesn’t matter a
rap where they are so long as they aren’t here.
Well, I’m d d, some more ! ”
Right above our heads came a long, shrill blast
like a conch or a tuneless horn—a blast so shrill
and so long-sustained that I thought any human
lungs must have burst under the effort.
“There’s one man about anyhow,” I said; “but
where is he ? He must be a mile up in the air
from the sound he makes.”
“ He isn’t a white man—that I’ll swear,” said
Hamilton. “Great Scott! did you ever hear
such a note as that ? ”
“ He isn’t a native, either. There aren’t any
about these parts.”
“ Well, I don’t care whether he’s man or
devil. We’re going straight on. We haven’t
got so far as this to be scared by any fool with a
brass trumpet. Inside an hour we’ll reach the
mine, and then we’ll find out the meaning of this
circus. Mind where you’re going now,” he added,
for a piece of the rock suddenly crumbled away
in front of us and went thundering down the
mountain side, leaving another awkward chasm to
be leaped over.
“ The mine’s just about half-way up,” said Jack
thoughtfully, after another long pause. “ Now
this festive orchestra seems to be holding its per-
formance right on the top, and that’s what I can’t
understand, for, so far as I know, the road
ends just beyond the cave. All that’s left after
that is a dead wall like the side of a house, and
there’s no place where a goat could get any further.
And I don't believe there’s another track up the
hill anywhere—l’m dead sure there isn’t —I’d bet
my life on it. Well, then, how did that party get
up there with his trombone, if he is up there, and
if he isn’t there—where is he ? ’■
“ Vou didn’t hear anything when you were here
before, I suppose ? ” asked Percy, nervously.
“ Do you suppose somebody’s been up there
for fifteen years playing his blessed music and
camping out for the fun of the thing ? ”
“ No-o, I don’t mean that. But how would it
bo if they can’t get down
“Then how on earth did they get up?” 1
asked. “ And supposing they did’get up and yet
can’t get down again by the same track, how do
you think anybody lives up there? Do you sup-
pose they plant potatoes and grow oats and have
a high old time, and rear families up among the
clouds? ”
“ Perhaps they’ve been up there always.”
“ Always ! Been born up there you mean, and
never came down, and can’t find a way out of it.
Percy, I’ve known a good many fools, but for an
ass you beat any ass I ever heard of.”
I stopped then, for there was a horrible, roaring,
clattering sound overhead, and a perfect avalanche
of stones came rushing down over our heads.
They hew past us so closely that I could feel a
rush of cold air on my face, and one immense
boulder struck a projecting knob just above us and
broke into a thousand splinters like a bomb-shell
when it bursts.
“I say, for God’s sake let’s get out of this!”
shrieked Percy as he turned a ghastly white.
“ Lie down, you idiot,” yelled Hamilton. And
suiting the action to the word he doubled himself
into an amazingly small compass and squeezed
himself flat against the cliff; “Lie down, and
hold on!”
We were down in the smallest fraction of a
second. Above us there was a crashing, rending,
humming noise, as if some stupendous object
was tearing its way through a mass of vegeta-
tion. Then there was a concussion that made
the ancient rocks shake and quiver, and an
immense tree flew out almost horizontally above
our heads and disappeared into the great, empty
depths below.
And behind it came
It was a man, spinning madly through the
air. He was brown-skinned and naked, and his
long hair flew out behind and all around him.
He came and passed like a flash, but I saw him
turn over and over as he went —now doubled
up—now spread out as if grasping at space for
some support, and then he landed right in the
centre of the little brawling river that foamtd
along far underneath our feet, and was seen no
more. But doubtless the river clutched the un-
recognisable fragments of him with its arms of
foam, and rolled them over and over, and cast
them up, and sucked them in again, and
churned them round and round in its angry
whirlpools, and tore them against stones
and snags and jagged rocks. And at last when it
was tired of this play, it bore away the scattered
remains of the wild man of the mountains, and
swept them out into the far Pacific, where the
long, black locks of his hair rose and fell like sea-
weed witu the ceaseless rhythm of the unrestful
waters, and the shrimps and the prawns ate his
ruins, and the crabs and the lobsters wrestled for
the debris of the feast, and the patient barnacles
walked over his battered bones as they lay on the
floor of the great ocean.
And then far, far above our heads came that
same mournful chorus.
“ Yah-ha aha-yaha ! ”
When the naked visitant from the upper air had
passed and vanished, I deliberately lay down on
the pathway and. clutched the rock with both
hands to keep myself steady, for earth and sky
and mountains seemed to be all dancing a frenzied
jig around me, and gyrating in endless circles,
and tearing round and round in a hideous waltz
that was timed to some dismal rhythm shrieked by
all the lost souls of an Alpine hell. Percy lay
beside me, almost insensible from terror. And
as for Hamilton he craned his neck outwards and
upwards to see if any new dangers were threaten-
ing, and seeing none he finally sat up and laughed
hysterically with chattering teeth, and passed his
hands through his hair.
“ I think we must have disturbed a whole city-
full of niggers,” he said. There’s the rest of the
menagerie raising the same old hymn. What a
chorus!”
He spoke in a strange, jerky fashion, and seemed
to grope for ea,ch word before he uttered it, as if
he were looking for it in the pages of a large
dictionary, and was uncertain about the pronunci-
ation even when he found it.
“Th —th —that was shave,” I gasped; “
close shave—l mean. I suppose that—tree was
meant—for—for us, wasn’t it?”
“ I should rather think it was. And the bricks,
and the rest of the furniture as well.”
“ For heaven’s sake, let’s make a run for it, then,
and get out of this,” implored Percy, huskily,
“ It’s certain death to stay here.”
“We can’t go back, my boy, if that’s what you
mean. We couldn’t get to the bottom again in
less than an hour, and that gang of native
dynamiters ’ll shoot trees at us all the way. We’ll
run for the cave and lie there till dark, and then
we must bolt and —and leave all the gold behind
us,” he added regretfully. “No, I’ll see that
tribe d d twenty-thousand times over before
I’ll do that. I’ll come back and blast every one
of them off his perch with nitro-glycerine first.
I’ll float a company, and we’ll turn the whole
mountain upside-down and shake them off the top
of it into the river. There’s another rock coming
down I expect —come on —we’ve got to run for it,
and no mistake.”
And we ran. The horses had stampeded long
ago, but we travelled faster than any horse could
have carried us over such ground. It was about
200 yards, and we covered it in a few seconds,
though it seemed to take whole ages. There were
awkward turns and angles in the road, and as we
shot round each one I expected to find a vengeful
crowd of strange-looking aborigines ru-hing
down on us, but no single human being
appeared in sight. The wailing chorus had
died away; there were no more missiles booming
down the mountain side; there was nothing, in
fact, but a profound stillness —the Sabbath calm
of long, long centuries, and yet above our heads
somewhere a tribe of raging heathens were per-
vading the mountain top, and getting ready some
new project for our destruction. Then suddenly
we came on a strange sight—a solitary tree grow-
ing out of this world of stone, and a tiny stream
of fresh water that trickled out of the rock, and
beside them was the cave where all that countless
wealth of gold had lain hidden for ages waiting
for us to come and share these untold millions,
and be rich past all human comprehension.
It was only a little depression in the rock—only
that and nothing more. Its walls were bare and
hungry stone, brown and barren and unspeakably
dismal. There was no sign of gold—there had
never been any—there never would be any if we
dug and delved and bored and blasted till the
millennium. It was all emptiness and fraud and
hollow delusion and ruin unutterable. I could
have made as good a gold-mine any day in the
middle of a pavement—l could have made a better

one in an old dry well. It was the only part of
Jack’s recollections that had no foundation —the
mountain and all the other surroundings were
there, but the gold was a delusion and a delirium,
and the whole of that mighty peak wasn’t worth a
copper. And instead of the golden millions there
was a tribe of howling savages overhead, and just
as we g'ained the scanty shelter of the cave the air
was filled with an ear-splitting yell of ferocity and
m dice, and a huge mass —ten tons or more of
solid rock—came plunging down the mountain
side.
It was the first good shot they had made. Ten
yards behind us it struck the crumbling pathway,
and the rock split and rent asunder as if it had
been a wall of earth built up against the hill-
side. And when it was gone we were wrecked
up in the air—stranded hopelessly among the
clouds, with nothing—absolutely nothing—re-
maining but the ledge on which we stood, and the
wall of rock behind us, and the abyss in front and
on either side.
YL
It was nine o’clock when we caught our first
glimpse of the golden mountain, and by eleven
the gold had vanished and the great vision faded,
and everything was over.
The night came and found us still sitting there,
and indulging in dreary speculations as to the
meaning of the great mystery.
“It was hundreds of years, I suppose, since
this road was made—or thousands perhaps,” said
Hamilton. “ The upper part of it must have
fallen in somehow, and that tribe of fiends has
been wrecked at the top and lived there ever
since. Just think of it. Likely enough their
ancestors were there when the Christian era dawned.
Perhaps they were there in Abraham’s time, or
before the Deluge. There may be some of
the patriarchs among them, or some of the giants
that were left over from Noah’s days, or maybe
they’re a lot of ancient Israelites. And I suppose
they’ve lived and married and grown old and died
on the top of this accursed mountain, and they’ve
raised vegetables up there, and caught birds, and
lived on in just the same howling savagery as
their fathers did at the beginning of the world.”
He was in the first stages of a raging fever.
His hands were burning and his eyes glassy and
his cheeks flushed, and he talked on with a wild,
incessant volubility that was maddening to listen
to. ...
“ And all this time they’ve been picking and
gnawing the mountain away, for the only way to
get down is to cut away the rock under their
feet. And when they’ve grubbed the entire peak
to pieces, and worked down to the level ground,
they’ll come out into the world—their remote
descendants will, that is—and what a world
it’ll bo for them. I suppose this was the
highest peak in New Zealand once, and they’ve
torn it up bit by bit with their hands till they’ve
worked down —down— ”
“Jack,” I said; “for God’s sake be rational.
How are we to get out of this ? Never mind
about all the rest.”
“ I suppose we’re the first strangers they’ve
seen for ages, and it’s the nature of the animal,
when lie sees a stranger, to throw something at
him. And that cheerful old father that came
through the air so suddenly, all scattered and
spread out, and with his hair flying behind him—
he must have been lifting for all he was worth to
start the tree over on our heads, and he was too
near the edge, I expect, and when the thing
went over a little faster than he expected,
he went after it. What a surprised-looking per-
son he was as he went past,” and Jack laughed
hysterically at the recollection. “I wonder who
he was ? Some of these old patriarchs in the
Bible seemed as if they never would die. Perhaps
he was one of them. I suppose a patriarch would
last a long time in a healthy atmosphere like
that?”
“Jack, stop that talk, for heaven’s sake. We
must find a way out of this.”
“ Out of this ? Why, there isn’t any way. We
can jump down, of course—the same track that
the naked father took this morning—there isn’t
any other road now. What an ungodly smash
that rock made; if they’d blasted the path away
with dynamite they couldn’t have done it better.
I wonder if we could get up, seeing we can’t get
down, and then we might join the naked clan on
the top, and marry some plain native female
apiece—ha, ha^—and bring up. families and—oh,
what a joke, ha, ha —ha, ha !”
He gave another yell of maniac laughter, and
crawled out along the ledge that still remained
intact in front of the cave. But in a second or two
he turned back again.
“There’s nothing there but the beginning of
universal emptiness —only fresh air and a
cliff that a fly couldn’t climb. No, this is
the finish—the whole game ends right here—a
wall of rook above us, and below us, and on each
side of us, and here we are sitting on our shelf
with our feet hanging over, and there’s no gold
after all—l must have dreamt it, I suppose,
or ”
“Jack, there’s always a chance that some one
may pass along away down there—a prospector or
something of that sort. If we were to call for help
somebody might hear us, and ”
“ Nobody’ll come this way till next century
now. And what difference would it make, any-
how ? It would take half-a-mile of ladder to do
us any good, or else a balloon. They couldnt
shoot meat at us with a gun, or fire jugs of water
at us; you know, or anything like that. It’s all
up, I tell you—oh, I wish the duke there would
stop groaning. He makes me ill.”
Percy was lying at the furthest extremity of the
ledge, a mere huddled-up, half-insensible mass of
clothes and misery and blank, despairing wretched-
ness. Poor Percy ! Some of these clays, when
his lordly uncle has drunk himself into the family
mausoleum, and left all the mortgages and the
pedigree and the hereditary spoons behind him,
somebody will go looking for the heir to all these
gorgeous properties, and no one will dream that
ho is lost among the kites and crows away up in
the Southern Alps.
By-and-by the moon rose and made curious
shadows among the gaunt crags and peaks, and
we tailed off into dejected silence, and sat looking
out solemnly on the silent, weary, changeless
magnificence of the great, unchangeable moun-
tains. And then, after awhile, I somehow fell
into a horrible sleep and dreamed.
I woke again with a start. There was some-
thing touching my face —something that was
slimy and chill, like the cold, dank feet of a bone-
less reptile. And in my ear there sounded a grue-
some, crunch’ng, gritting noise that ——

Extract from the Inangahua Times, 4th
January, 1891
A man, name unknown, was brought up at
the police-court yesterday, charged with being a
dangerous lunatic at large. He made an inco-
herent statement to the effect that he had taken
to drink owing to his skeleton, and the remains
of two other men, being on the top of an inacces-
sible mountain, and not able to get down, and he
asked the court if it had a ladder half-a-mile loner.
The arresting constable reported that he had
searched the prisoner, and found in his pockets,
two buttons, a piece of rock weighing three
pounds, and an almost illegible manuscript in an
nnMulshed condition. The case was remanded for
a week.

WHAT IS XT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or baking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you? Will you do your business at

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
January 17, 1894.
Sydney D.T., flushed with the success of its
wordy warfare on the subject of “Should Bachelors
Marry?” and the columns of cheap copy it led to,
set another literary bell-wether adrift last week,
in the person of a correspondent asking why men
don’t marry ? The writer finds that “ colonial ”
man doesn’t marry because he is becoming a
“ unique antithesis of nature ” and a philosopher
per medium of books (which is only skin-deep
philosophy, after all). But there is nothing in
either theory. There is only one general reason
why men don’t marry, and that is—cold cash.
It is not only men who should be blamed for
the depressed marriage-rate - except you find
fault with them as financial failures —on the first-
cause principle. The girl of the period is smart
and wide-awake. She earns money—oftentimes
more money than the swain who comes to woo—
and weighs whether it is a better speculation to
keepin a congenial billet on a neat screw than go
housekeeping with Gussie on less per annum than
her dressmaker’s bills come to in her professional
spinsterhood.
If man is shy of marriage woman is still shyer,
for pecuniary reasons. Danae in the shower of
gold is the allegory of the nineteenth-century girl
—like Danae, she is only approachable in one
way, and there is just as much want of horse-sense
in asking why don’t men marry as in asking
why hasn’t every man a good bank-account? Of
course, every woman likes to own a man at some
time or other, just to show the other women how
she can manage him —but he must be well-to-do,
or there isn’t a bit of fun in it. Woman at the
altar, or woman at the bargain • counter, is
actuated by the same emotions. A wife l'^ 63 to
show off a better husband than she intrinsically
deserves, just as a bargain-hunter tells you with
pride that she got that lovely remnant for two-
and-six. And until a matrimonial bargain comes
her way (which is never, probably), the level-
headed woman keeps very cool in the marriage-
market.
Take my own case, for example. Am Ito fall a
viotiirt to the bow and arrow of some small-salaried
youth who will tell me, perhaps, that he is saving
up to marry me, and will preach economy at me
indirectly all the time we are “ walking out,” so
that I won’t waste the cold mutton in our cottage
by the sea ? Ideal prospect! But still it does not
tempt me—nor does it attract other women who
follow the remunerative professions and prefer
to do a man’s work from “ sun to sun ” than a
woman’s which is “never done.” Hardly one
woman in a hundred who can support herself in
fairly good style, is lured to give up her defined
profession for the uncertain joy of chivvying a
small ’servant round a suburban villa, and listening
to the fall of her bridal china.
Some of the booksellers have fairly revelled in
cheap advt. over the “What do women read”
question. One thing is plainly proved—the novel-
reading female—the curl-papered housekeeper who
“scamps ” her duties to get back to the thrilling
moment when the. long-legged duke goes on one
knee to the virtuous ballet-girl—is still in the vast
majority. Two leading booksellers and the cheap-
subscription-library people say that “ Sydney
ladies are stronger lovers of pure fiction than any
other class of literature. I underline the adjec-
tive, which I regard as a doubtful fit for the
situation.
Because, you see, I don’t believe the virtuous
bookman (whose name figures largely in the article
hereinbefore alluded to) when he says there is no
feminine “enquiry for Zola” at his discreetly-
patronised establishment. What else could he
say ? There are some questions a man of the
wrld never answers, and the bookseller who
advertised his establishment as the resort of
realistic readeresses would find his clients dropping
off in favour of the establishment where the wily
proprietor “ testified ” freely to the rush of Jane
Austen over the front counter, but—lay low about
the steady exit of Balzac by the side door.
The world will never really know for certain
what women read until married men expose the
amount of red and yellow realism that is tucked
away under almost every matronly pillow and in
every connubial fancy work-basket in the land ; or
until innocent small boys detail (in print) what
sister sends them to the library for. You might
as well get at what women drink by deep study of
the or what society women like with
midnight oysters on the authority of the waiter,
as go round with pencil asking for incriminating
data from specified bookshops which know exactly
what to say. To really fathom the workings you
should be a woman and glide up to the counter in
the dim end of the establishment, where you ask
in a disguised voice and three thicknesses of
gossamer-veil for Zola’s very latest, unabridged,
with footnotes, if you please.
The very latest Queensland divorce-case has a
basis of grilled steak, although the lawyers will
doubtless find other terms for the real cause
of the rupture. They were neighbours, and
one day his wife went away (taking the servant
either as a precaution or because she wanted her),
and her husband departed also for the day. When
lunch-time came He tried to grill a steak, and,
failing, handed it over the back fence to Her. She
did it to a turn, and was just handing it back to
Him with the addition of a few vegetables when
her legal steak-eater arrived by tha back-gate and
—but the case won’t be heard in camera , so that’s
anticipating.
Apropos of divorces, I was amused to be told,
the other day, that any lawyer who succeeds in
putting a lady-client through the mill and return-
ing her to society in her maiden name is always
the object of little marks of gratitude for the rest
of his professional life, provided, of course, that
the lady doesn’t wing her way to distant climes.
Bub while she can reach him personally or by
letter, the man of law never knows at what
moment a golden-haired woman with “a past,”
and in the act of handing him a pair of crewelled
braces, won’t be discovered in his office by his
wife. And though even lawyers may be believed
at times—that is emphatically one of the occasions
when the beautiful truth will avail them nob.
A male divorce client, I am told, is not fond of
the lawyer who sets him loose, although there is
an authentic instance of one Sydney lawyer who
is absolutely persecuted with wallabies, sent him
that he may wallow in kangaroo-tail soup (for
which he had owned a partiality, to back-blocks
Lothario, in an unguarded moment). Still, the
male client doesn’t throw a halo of sentiment over
his defender—that he has to pay the piper is,
probably, the reason why he doesn’t follow his
deliverer up with fancy-work after the battle. Bub
emotional woman, it appears, is obstinately grate-
ful for her freedom, and never forgets Dr. Briefless.
I know one Sydney lawyer who is kept in beauti-
ful flowers by a thankful and nob altogether
blameless divorce.
A week ago “ Pro Bono Publico ” and “ Cui
Bono’’were on their mettle in the dailies over
the “white slaves” question, the pallid helots
being certain working girls who labour over nine
and a-quarter hours a day for seven shillings a
week, or from 6 a.m. to 1 a,m. for five shillings a
week, overtime paid at the rate of threepence per
hour ! Think of these exorbitant wages for long
hours of heavy work the next time you “in-
stinctively” draw away from the painted wreck
you meet in the streets at midnight. “It is
easy,” said Becky Sharpe, “to be virtuous on a
thousand a year.”
A woman comments upon the “white slaves’’
question in Sydney. “This matter of grinding the
female labourer down must be settled by Act of
Parliament, and if the employers will not be just,
they must be compelled. I arn proud of our
woman suffragists, who understand politics far
better than some of our paid legislators.” In-
directly accusing men of creating a state of affairs
for which women are largely to blame 1 Who is
it keeps the “ bargain ” counters—the sweater’s
outlet —so brisk? Woman, the cheapener and
haggler, who has a special memory for “sacri-
fice ” sales, and who wouldn’t care if every stitch
were a drop of blood, so long as she saved three-
ha’pence on the article. So, perhaps, the redemp-
tion of the white slaves of Sydney will come more
gracefully from the feminine camp.
I really think we must be growing out of matri-
mony when this kind of advertisement blossoms
brazenly in Sydney D.T. :

Even the pious youth with the goloshes is taking
to the trial system now.
A Sydney daily which lately published a
“Domain ” article calmly stated, with a relieved
expression, that “ only four women— in each case
accompanied by her husband and children —were
met with, but it was stated by those who knew
the nooks well that a number of women slept
nightly in the open. One case was particularly
distressing. “A family camped under the rocks
had only one blanket, and this was shared by the
woman and her child.”
Still, four women without a shelter is a depress-
ing average for a city like Sydney, and reminds
one that since Miss Myra Kemble gave her
successful performance of “Dr. Bill,” in Sydney
Town Hall, in ’92, there has been no organised
show for the sole benefit of the unemployed
women, in spite of the women-championship of
women which is so popular at present.
Talking of benefit shows, a good charity-concert
is billed to take place on the 2nd Feb., under
most influential patronage headed by the Governor.
MM. Deslouis, Kowalski, Stael, Wiegand, and
other well-known artistes contribute, and all the
proceeds (which I hope will be large) go to the
three little children of Mons. and Mme. La
Meslee, who were drowned in the recent boating
accident in the Harbour, leaving their family
totally unprovided for. The concert promises to
be rather a huge affair as M. La Meslee was con-
nected with the Civil Service and several learned
societies whose members are actively interested in
the affair.
As I predicted, the clever Crystal Maze in
Sydney Strand, where you see yourself north,
east, south and west without turning your head —
is a happy hunting-ground for the wearers of new
dresses (and new suits, for that matter, since the
masculine mind also appreciates a pleasant back
view) who can see if the double skirt suits them
all round the figure and then have tea and ices (in
a cool grotto with a sparkling electric fountain —
the first in Australia) without extra charge. On
these lines the Maze prospers. Clever is the
showman who includes a free cup of tea in his list
of attractions.
A Sydney evening paper has discovered that a
“well-known society lady ” is giving dual swim-
ming parties in a suburban ocean “basin” by
moonlight. However, the fact that the lady in
question bathes with her assorted friends is no
criterion that “good society” is addicted to
entertaining its visiting-list in a hired pool by
moonlight. As a matter of fashion, dual-bathing
is not good form at all in Sydney, and isn’t likely
to become so under present arrangements.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMIH.
Melbourne, Jan. 17th, 1894.
The Hopetouns are off on a driving tour.
Every nerve is strained to live up to the bush,
and if the belted Earl can’t easily persuade the
billy to get its steam up he feels his discomfiture
very keenly. I am told Lady H., mixing a
damper, is picturesque, but should the hereditary
De Moleyns’ hands suffer thereby, the South
Yarra people will miss the rapture of seeing a
Countess remove her gloves in the tabernacle and
knit her naked fingers in prayer.
Of late, though, the Sunday morning pilgrimage
to South Yarra is quite a rare event. St. Paul’s
Cathedral has been “taken up” by the first
worshipper in the land, and when the air is
balmy (and the odour of ooze from the river
doesn’t get into one’s piety) she may seen
walking across Prince’s Bridge to Guv’ment
House, a bright, Christian example in a dim
religious frock, attended by lots of suite. Several
of the suite things in men are staunch supporters
of the cathedral. They drop in for afternoon
service during the week, and have been heard to
remark that the musical entertainment is not at
all bad, doncherknow.
A scion of the most famous “ boom and bust ”
families nearly won a considerable academical
distinction, the other day. Wasn’t it she, who,
though one of eleven, had a lady’s-maid all to her-
self, and, after the manner of budding Vere de
Veres, came down to dessert every evening in
velvet frock and silk stockings? Allis lost but
brains, which she can possess without detriment
to other people, if that’s much consolation. And
brains don’t seem to count for much at the critical
moment when a vacuous young man commences
to wink his other eye. That’s where the female
intellect breaks down, somehow.
“Pinafore,” by the Hawthorn amateurs, was a
financial if not a musical triumph. Non-profes-
sionals never understand “making up,” and the
sisters, cousins, and aunts, on this occasion, were
no exception to the rule, one lady’s “pearl-
creamed” ears standing out with ghastly effect.
A popular medico made a tempting A. 8., and all
the leading lights of the district were to be seen
in the audience.
My ideas concerning the Royal visit fiasco are a
little late, but I throw them into the discussion
without apologising. Premier Patterson, as an
old family man, knew what he was about in
inviting the Yorks at the present “juncture.”
Two Ministers of State must be present at the birth
of an heir to the throne. Imagine the honour and
glory accruing to the Victorian Premier had he been
called upon to wait in the ante-room upon so
auspicious an occasion to bear witness to the bona
fides of the red little stranger!!
You may be sure that York at once put down
his foot. “I ain’t going,” he said, gloomily, as a
vision of life on the ocean wave, with a cargo of
doctors, nurses, feeding-bottles, god-fathers, god-
mothers, Lords-Chamberlain, &0., &c., crossed his
mental vision. And May interpolated her dulcet
tones: “Suppose that George Albert Victor
Edward Alexander George Leopold Duncan
Patrick were born with the colonial twang!”
They would none of them take kindly to the
notion of tightening the silken bonds with a
“ twang.”
“Victoria’s most “ horsey ” heiress seems wedded
to her serge gown, pale-blue blouse and straw
sailor hat. Perhaps, to atone for some very
venial, blue-blooded error, she sports this garb in
lieu of sack-cloth and ashes. No other explana-
tion suggests itself to me after a fifteenth consecu-
tive meeting with the same cheap toilette. But
what wouldn’t some of us wear if we did the Block
in her boots!
At the Sargoods’ moonlight garden-party, just
before Xmas, the moon and the women were
wrapped in clouds, and neither showed to advan-
tage. In Spain, or even Sydney, where colds in
the head are unknown and complexions only “ so-
so,” women may look their best strolling by the
light of the moon, robed in white. It’s different
with the Melb. girls. They are too blooming to
fear daylight, and when muffled in shawls on a
dull night, are not ethereal enough to avoid com-
parison with cows looming through a mist.
Rumour has it that a law-suit is impending over
the Chief Justice's .£5O prize, which falls to the
top scorer in the final law exam. Two young
“ softies ” are said to have agreed that if either
of them gained the award, he would share it with
his chum. The winner now refuses to pay up,
and his quondam friend has issued or threatens
a writ against him for breach of agreement.
Talking of rash promises, the town has been
amused by the adventures of a youth in a some-
what conspicuous circle, who, at the tender age of
18, promised to marry the lady-help, a young
thing of 29, and was sternly held to his word.
Pa burst into tears on hearing of Voung Hopeful’s
matrimonial fix, exclaiming—“My poor blue-eyed
boy ! ”
Gov. Hopetoun is ageing in wisdom consider*
ably. At school speech-days he is most effective!
his frock-coat being in itself an educational in’
fluenoe. No buttonhole blossom invades the
sanctity of this garment, which (as I heard a
strong-minded lady observe) has enough stuff in it
to clothe half-a-dozen families. Now, at flower-
shows and exhibitions our Gov. reminds one of
the girl who thinks it suits her to be animated.
Rolling his eyes from side to side in simulated
ecstasy, he heads straight for t:ie nearest escape
door, chanting en route the praises of the exhibits
he hasn’t attempted to notice.
Like Max O’Rell’s ‘‘Mrs. John Bu’l,” Lady
Hopetoun follows meekly at the guid man’s heels,
her Sphinx-like silence in marked contrast to his
loquacity. By-the-bye, there is no blinking the
fact that her ladyship is developing what old-
fashioned novels call “a majestic presence” and
will soon be the typical British matron in appear-
ance.
Bishop Webber (Brisbane) still frisks in Eng-
land, leaving his curates and lambs in general to
wander where they will. Last trip his celibate
clergy seized the chance and got married. ‘‘lt’s
all very well,” they grumbled, “ for ‘ Brisbane’ to
talk platonically about ‘Ladies X. and Y., de-
voted noble women,’ who take such interest in
Brisbane’s career, but then he’s a weathi r-beaten
old prelate, while we’re all under 25, and were ex-
pressly chosen, with rosy cheeks and small hands,
for proselytising purposes.” Spinster interest in
the careers of such of these is not to bo put off
with a mere brotherly blessing. I know of at least
one northern damsel who carried her point by
seating herself unexpectedly upon a chubby
cherub’s knee and asking him to hear her say her
catechism. An offer of marriage was made and
accepted within five minutes.
Depression in Melb. is so marked that the only
way for even the upper-crust mothers to get their
daughters off is to take in a few “ paying guests”
—the new haul ton name for lodgers. This ruse
is occasionally successful, as shown recently by a
very fashionable—on one side, anyhow—suburban
wedding.
The “Seventh Day Adventists ” are out for an
airing at Middle Brighton. One can take a tent
only by solemnly subscribing to the doctrine that
men with pipes or women in corsets cannot pos-
sibly enter the kingdom of Heaven. Otherwise,
worse ways might be found of spending a holiday.
The brethren tie up their heads in mosquito-net
bags, but whether from religious scruples or
merely to keep off the flies I did not discover. The
sisters are comely, and one, seated on a crazy quilt
in an aesthetic tent, seemed to be appreciated. The
Rev. Mrs. White, of Amurrika, who boasts that
her voice comes from her stomach, delivers dis-
courses on “ the raring of chil-dern,” “ the lik-ker
appit-tite,” and other exciting topics. An un-
girded style of dress and vegetarian diet have
dealt hardly with this lady’s outer husk, but I am
told she is spiritually beautiful.
Talking of spiritual beauty, D’Argo’s nether
complements have never appeared to better advan-
tage than in the dreamy whirl she gives us for an
encore in the Princess’ Panto. Her Blue Ballet
efforts are grotesque in the eyes of most women,
whatever they may have been in the opera-glasses
of the balder sex. The toe-waltz of D’Argo up-to-
date is a graceful, rhythmical dance from the
shoulders downwards (though stiff-neckish at the
summit), and it fetches the boys exceedingly. The
other Saturday afternoon I met the muscular Sig-
norina, who is quite young and freshly pretty,
emerging from the stage entrance passage clad in
tight-fitting tweed and bearing a brace of bouquets.
She had done her turn and was away to the
adjacent boarding-house to put her trophies in
water. They evidently represented a pair of hard-
hit Johnnies, and had nothing in common with
the ordinary bunch of stale vegetables provided by
a management.
Little Vi. Varley seems to have dropped out of
the floral tribute struggle since her engagement to
a member of the profesh was cruelly given awav
to the outside world. Reports as to Violet’s
wedding fixture are conflicting, by the way. It is
whispered in some places that Tapley will claim
his bride at the end of the pantomime run, yet
elsewhere they speak of a long wait intervening.
Theatrical marriages, although made in Heaven,
like the rest of them, are apt to be regulated in
their course by business contracts.
VAN M.
Premier Nelson repeats the statement that
Queensland kanakas are kept to field labour—“not
employed as in the old days as nursemaids, grooms,
garden hands, and so on.” A few months ago the
present writer was on a Queensland plantation,
not far from Townsville, where kanakas did all
these things, and chopped wood, cleared roads,
shod horses, and worked in the mill as well.
The owners of the plantation stood well at head-
quarters, and the inspector, who was getting con-
victions against small farmers at Mackay, knew
better than to interfere.
M’llwraith must have credit for this, cut
from Singapore Times, 12th Dec. :
Reporter: “ Why do you exclude Chinese ; they would
be of greater use to you in developing your country,
would they not?”
M'llwraith: “ Well, the fact is they have been tried,
but unsuccessfully. They are most difficult customers to
manage in large numbers. Then, when they enter in to com-
petition with white men there can be only one result. I be-
lieve that a white man will get through six times as much
work as a Chinaman—[Nonsense.—Ed. B.]-and will do it
more satisfactorily for his employer. Idonot think it fair to
our own men to ask them to compete with the Chinamen.
You know how little suffices for a Chinaman. On the
other hand we have been educating our working men and
teaching them the advantage of having pretty, well-
furnished homes, of living in more luxurious style and
dressing better, and they cannot do all that if wages are
to be reduced by competition with the Chinese.”
A Hobart Mercury correspondent :
Christraas-bells are ringing, and the choristers are sing-
ing, while thousands of good people are assembled in
sacred edifices to publicly declare once more their adora-
tion and their joyful acceptance of God’s wonderful gift.
Soft breezes pass in through the window's and fan the
faces of the worshippers, and toy with the ladies’ laces
and the pretty blossoms in their new summer bonnets,
then after caressing the locks of the children in the
various pews they lightly touch the preacher’s forehead
and pass out to loiter in a clump of trees, while other
wandering zephyrs find their way into the church and re-
peat the fanning, toying, and caressing for a litlle space.
Then the collection-box man came along and the
correspondent felt in all his pockets for that
threepenny-bit he knew he had left after last
night’s last drink.
The greatest treat you can give yourself,
or your friend in the country, is a Maoriland
smoked blue cod. The blue cod, as prepared for
and sold by the N.S. W. Fresh Food and Ice Co.,
King-street, Sydney, is the premier fish of Aus-
tralasian waters. Eating smoked blue cod is nob
a mere habit—it is a disease. This incomparable
fish, which, with a great variety of others (list on
application), is received per every steamer by the
F. F. and I. Co., should be on every breakfast-
table in town and country. The provincial hotel-
keeper who fails to supply the drummer with
smoked blue cod for breakfast doesn’t understand
Ids business and is on the high road to the Insol-
vent Court.

A Ponapean Conveuance.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Here also, as at Yap, the youngest wives of the chiefs
visited the frigate. . . . Somewhat shocking at first
to our feelings as Christians. . . . Yet to have de-
clined what was regarded by these simple and amiable
people as the very highest token of their regard for the
officers of the Expedition would have been bitterly re-
sented. . . . And, after all, our duties to our King
and Queen were paramount- i.e., ‘the foundation of
friendly relations with the people of this Archipelago.’
. . . The engaging manners and modest demeanour of
these native ladies were most commendable. That this
embarrassing custom was practised to do us especial
honour we had ample proof.”
# * *
Chester, the trader, laid down the book
and looked curiously at the title—“ A Journal of
the Expedition under Don Felipe Tompson
through the Caroline Islands.” It was in Spanish,
and had been lent him by one of the Jesuit fathers
in Ponape.
“Ninety years haven’t worked much difference
in some of the native customs,” thought he to
himself. “ What a sensation Don Felipe would
have made lecturing at St. James’ Hall on these
pleasantly curious customs ! I must ask Tulpe
about these queer little functions. She’s chock-
full of Island lore—and perhaps I’ll make a book
myself some day.”
* * *
“ Huh /” said Tulpe, Chester’s native
wife, whipping off her muslin gown and tossing it
aside, as she lay back and cooled her heated face
and bared bosom with a fan, “ ’tis hot, Khesta,
and the sun was balanced in the middle of the sky
when we left Jakoits in the boat, and now ’tis all
but night; and wind there was none, so we used
noc the sail.”
“ Foolish creature,” said Chester, again taking
up his book, “and merely to see this new white
missionary woman thou wilt let the sun take thy
hands and feet black.”
Handsome, black-browed Tulpe flashed her
white, even teeth as she smiled.
“Nay, but listen, Khesta. Such a woman as
this one I have never seen. Her skin is white
and gleaming as the inside of the pearl-shell.
How comes it, my white man, that such a fair
woman as this marrieth so mean-looking a man?
Was she a slave ? Were she a woman of Ponape,
and of good blood, Nanakin the Great would take
her to wife.”
“ Aye,” said Chester, lazily, “and whence came
she and her husband ?”
“From Kusaie (Strong’s Island), where, for
two years, have they lived, so that now the woman
speaketh our tongue as well as thee.”
“ Ha !” said the trader, quickly; “ what is their
name ?”
She told him, and Chester suddenly felt uncom-
fortable.
* w *
Two years before, in Honolulu, he had
met that white woman. She was waiting to be
married to the Rev. Obadiah Yowlman, a hard-
faced, earnest-minded little Yankee missionary,
who was coming up from the Carolines in the
Planet. There had been some rather heavy love-
passages between her and Chester. He preserved
his mental equilibrium she lost hers. The
passionate outburst of the “little she-missionary ”
as be called her when he bade her good-bye, he
regarded as the natural and consistent corollary
°f moonlit nights beneath the waving palms of
white Hawaiian beaches. When he returned to
Bonape he simply forgot all about her.

The Social Theorist.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
As one who leadeth men ’mid ice and mountain
glooms
To fairer lands beyond the barren snows,
Feels with his staff the way before he goes,
Lest in some cavern deep their feet should fall
And over them the whitened darkness close —
So, standing ’mid the murk of human ills,
The orphan’s hunger and weak woman’s tears,
The strong man’s present hopes and future
fears—
He who would lead mankind to happier things
Thrusts first his thought before him through
the years.
A dreamer is he called. For charlatans.
For knaves who in her markets buy and sell
Rise earth’s deep-throated plaudits : anthems
swell.
He points the road to mortal paradise,
And his reward on earth is—hell.
Broken Hill. Chas. A. Cameron.
The Uniform of a Caste.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Scene : A barrister’s chambers in Eliza-
beth-street, Sydney. Frantic voice yelling over
the bannisters, “Hi ! stop him 1 stop him ! He’s
got my coat!”
Fortunately for the owner of the coat, a large
Q.O. was just coming up the stairs and effectually
blocked the way. The thief gave in at once and
turned back.
“It’s very sad to see such a respectable-looking
man going in for this sort of game,” said the Q.C.
as he shepherded him up.
“ I don’t know what made me do it. I’m sure
I don’t know what made me do it. Such a thing
has never happened before,” said the respectable-
looking man; “ I suppose you’ll send for the
police ?”
“ I suppose I shall,” said the owner of the coat,
shortly, snatching it out of his hand. “ Edward ”
—to the suckling who acted as his clerk—“go out
at once and get a constable.”
Edward presumably looked diligently for a
constable. Presently, however, he came back
without one.
“There’s no p’leecemen anywhere, sir.”
“ Damn fool! ” said his master; “ I s’pose I’ll
have to go for one myself.”
But his indignation had oozed out considerably
daring the delay.
“Yes, it’s inexpressibly shocking to see a man
of so respectable an appearance in this position,”
tentatively observed he to the large Q.O.
“Very sad!” It was obvious they were now
somewhat inclined to mercy.
“My God ! I’m sure I don’t know how I could
have done it. Never before, on my honour, never
gentlemen,” declared the respectable-looking man,
giggling convulsively.
“Poor devil, he’s hysterical!” said the large
Q.C. Aside: “ Don’t press it.”
The owner of the rescued coat nodded.
