Utilisation of Australian
Timbers.
. The settler upon the river-lands of New
South Wales, when battling with the gigantic
vegetation that seems to him a barrier to all his
hopes of future comfort and independence, prac-
tically despises the timbers which his all-com-
pelling cross-cut saw, axe, or forest-devil “drops,”
uses them up in fencing, or burns them up as
rubbish—anything to get rid of them! But at
least one eminent and enterprising Sydney firm
has demonstrated the fact that Australian timbers,
skilfully manipulated, possess a sterling com-
mercial value applicable to the utilitarian arts.
“ Australian timber is the very best timber in
the world for furniture-making and durable
cabinet-work, I don’t care what other timber you
name, or whence you obtain it. It is better
than Spanish mahogany or American walnut.
Those timbers do not come near the Australian,
either for beauty or durability, which are the two
essentials to be looked for,”
The speaker was Mr. Stutchbury, manager of
Beale and Co.’s factory, at Nelson-street, Annan-
dale.
Beale and Co. constitute a Sydney firm whose
name is a household word throughout N.S. Wales,
and which has branches in almost every town of
any importance within the province. It owns a
block of two handsome six-storeyed warehouses at
482 and 484 George-street, Sydney, with a base-
ment leased as a cafe and billiard-rooms, and a
cellarage beneath the basement. It also owns the
factory referred to.
Beale and Co. are popularly associated in the
public cognisance with sewing - machines and
musical instruments—organs, pianos, &c. But
their propagation of native industries is but im-
perfectly known. A visit to their Annandale
factory is at once a revelation and an education.
• • *
The familiar sewing-machine, the house-
wife’s friend and potent ally, is usually pictured
by the uncommercial imagination whenever its
consideration comes within the mental horizon, as
an article imported bodily from abroad —from
America, or Germany, or England. In many
instances in which such a presentment is filed it is
a true bill.
Beale and Co., so far as their very large output
is concerned, “have changed all that.” They
confine their importations merely to the “heads ”
of sewing-machines, and to the interiors of organs
while they manufacture all the “ tables ” and
“ cabinets ” and “ frameworks ” themselves. And
in this manufacture they use nothing but Aus-
tralian timbers, using up many hundreds of thou-
sands of feet (superficies in sawn planks) per
year.
“ Wei maintain,” avers the manager, “ that we
are selling a better, a better-looking, a more sub-
stantial, and a higher-class article than any im-
ported cabinet-work.”
The favourite timbers used are rosewood (well
known) and red-bean and black-bean—the beans
being peculiarly beautiful woods for every kind of
artii-tic furniture-work.
“ We are just now getting ready for our New
Year’s stock,” remarked Mr. Stutchbury;
“several hundreds of logs. In fact, some of it
has already arrived and is being cut to our re-
quired thicknesses at Goodlet and Smith’s saw-
mills, Sydney. We believe in giving Australian
industries every possible chance. We use no im-
ported timbers. Our factory thus assists to keep
going Australian timber-getters, Australian ship-
pers, Australian saw-millers. All the money that
the factory sends into circulation is spent in °’K
own country.”
• • •
Following the adventures of an Australian
log, after it has left its birthplace ; It is shipped
per coaster to a Sydney sawmill, there sawn into
planks, and conveyed to the stacking-yard of
Beale and Co.’s Annandale factory, which stands
upon a considerable area of ground in that manu-
facturing Sydney suburb. The planks are stacked
upon timber-staging, about 4ft. from the ground;
laid horizontally, with small battens between
each plank, so as to permit the air to circulate
freely through the interspaces.
“ This seems a small matter,” observed the
manager; “it is really a first essential. Many
builders, as you may have noticed, stack their
weatherboard timbers upended. Well, these get
tolerably dry and sound at the end that is aloft;
but the end that is on the ground retains moisture
and sap. Consequently, not only does the timber
warp, but it weakens and developes a tendency to
split. We find out all these little details by
experience. I have been in the timber business
all my life.”
The planks, when stacked, are given at least 12
months’ natural seasoning—seasoning in the open
air, away off the ground, with each plank free
from every other, as explained already. Some-
times the planks get 18 months’ or two years’
natural seasoning ; 12 months is the minimum
limit. After this preparation, the timber is ready
for being handled. But it has to go through other
stages of artificial seasoning in its process towards
artistic utility.
The timber is now cut into the various lengths
necessary for the several purposes to which it is to
be applied ; and, being so cut—by the thousand of
each sample—it is placed in a drying-room, the
maximum temperature of which is about 130 deg.
Fahrenheit. Here, it has a preliminary perspira-
tion, but its troubles have only commenced. From
this it is taken into a second and more sweltering
drying-room, where it stays for about a month, in
a temperature whose midday maximum rises to
about 170 deg. Fahrenheit. This Sheolian process
is designed, not so much with a view to dry the
timber, as its longnatural seasoning has thoroughly
achieved that result, but to test its fibre, its capa-
city of durability, and to ensure that it will not
split when subjected to the further trying tortures
of the cabinet-maker’s art.
# » #
One of the most useful and uncomplaining
employees of the factory is the “ waste-steam ”
from the boiler, if it be not paradoxical to call
“ waste ” that which is used up in a hundred
ways, over and over again, before it is allowed to
escape to liberty in the atmosphere. One engine
drives all the numerous mechanical machines in
the factory; it turns, in the first place, 99 feet of
mainshafting, and, in the second, some 150 feet of
counter, or branch, shafting. The principal fuel
used is the “waste” of the factory—shavings;
and a roaring furnace they make. The furnace,
by the way, is the only flame to be found on the
premises; and, as that is banked up every night,
and everything near the furnace is carefully
<wept up and cleaned away, there is no risk of
fire in connection with any of the working opera-
Uons. All the heat-producing work of the factory
is performed by the “ waste-steam ” aforesaid.
An impressive lesson in utilitarian economy is
afforded by this “waste steam.” After it leaves
the boiler, it circulates through pipes that visit
various drying - rooms, “hot-boxes,” glueing-
chambers, etc., working ceaselessly in the testing
of timbers, boiling of glne, heating of apparatus,
and performing a number of other services too
trivial to mention, but highly necessary to the
uninterrupted regularity of the work of the estab-
lishment. Not the least grateful of its uses is its
heating of a spare boiler, by simply turning on a
tap, so as to enable the factory “ hands ” to boil
water for their midday or other tea. The ap-
pliances areso arranged at various vantage-points
that, if it is desired to heat, melt, or boil any-
thing, the turning of a brass cock effects the
desired end by placing the steam-service at the
operator’s disposal. The working fire-risk is thus
absolutely nil.
• * *
Beale and Co. do not limit their cabinet-
work to sewing-machines, but also do a large
manufacturing trade in organs, for the excellence
of which the firm is famous. It is a popular fallacy
that an organ is imported whole by Beale and Co.
Not so. The firm merely imports what the trade
technically, if somewhat barbarously, styles “the
gut ’ of the instrument—that is to say, the reeds
and the keyboard. The bellows and the case—
how handsome and ornamental some of these cases
are, patrons of the firm well know—are manu-
factured in the Annandale factory. And every
inch of the timber used in this high-class and
artistically-finished work is Australian, not one
scrap of foreign timber entering into the finished
framework.
• • •
The timber is first cut in the rough, in
sizes for tables, flaps, panels, doors, drawers, etc.,
all the machinery arrangements being so exact
that every one table of a certain pattern is exactly
the same size and shape as every other table of
that pattern ; each drawer is interchangeable with
every other drawer of that size, and so on. The
timber is planed by machinery, sorted out into
various sections, and sent for its probation in the
first hot-room (130 deg.). This room, though the
first as regards the reception of cut-up timber, is
the last in respect of the “waste-steam,” which
has circulated all through the building before it
arrives here, where is its escape-pipe, whence it
issues hissingly, as if glad on account of its final
happy release.
Having successfully endured the testing experi-
ments required by this hot-room, and its more
grievous successor (170 deg.), the timber, in work-
ing samples, passes through the various depart-
ments, starting from the rear of the premises “ in
the rough,” until it emerges from the “issuing-
room ” in front as finished work, part and parcel
of the handsome articles of cabinet furniture one
sees in that apartment.
* • #
There are two planing-machines, steam-
worked, which shave the various samples to the
exact thickness—or should it be called thinness?—
required; and their nicety of operation and speed
is so noticeable as to force the thought that the
possibility of such results would be unbelievable
were the living facts not before one’s eyes. A
band-saw for cutting ornamental suites is a thin,
ribandy, fine saw, fixed vertically; and which,
working always from the outside of a piece of
timber, is capable of the most astonishing curvi-
linear feats, performed in a few seconds—with a
whizz (so to speak).
The fret-saw is also fixed vertically, but the
work of this beautiful tool is all from the inside of
the timber, and has to be started by means of an
auger-hole. Not that any time is lost by a man
painfully boring holes by hand in the timbers to
be “fretted.” Apiece is simply held against a
horizontally-placed and swiftly-revolving auger in
a boring-machine moved by steam ; five seconds,
and the hole is made through an inch-thick board
—l2 a minute; 720 an hour, with no trouble, and
with such simplicity that an idiot boy could per-
form the task.
The fret-saw is then fixed, so as to work from
inside the auger-hole, and the operator at the
table can now produce the most complicated
designs of filigree work, with a swiftness that is
marvellous, the designs being simply traced in
“carpenter’s leadpencil ” on the wood beforehand
traced, probably, by outlining from a metal
pattern, The secret of the fret-saw, thus
arranged, is that its teeth are so set that it only
cuts with a downward stroke; the up-stroke is
lost. The saw is, otherwise, thin and narrow,
like the band-saw, and so, with a wasted up-stroke,
it is able to turn a sudden line at right-angles to
the line it has been cutting, without any disfigure-
ment of the angle, and with the exactitude that
can turn out thousands of samples, any one of
which is interchangeable with any other.
The writer saw a large number of sewing-
machine tables dealt with in a few minutes—say,
half-a-minute to each—the fret-saw cutting out
that four-sided “hole” through which the “head ”
of a sewing-machine uplifts itself. The operator
simply ripped them through “like winking,” the
saw seeming to cling to the exact pencil-mark by a
sort of lightning instinct.
• • *
All the lower floor is known by the
*’ house ” name of “ the mill,” and it is here that
all the preparatory and rougher work is done.
Yet, “the mill” shows some beautiful processes.
The “ variety moulder ” is a tool that astonishes
an outsider. Its purpose is the making of mould-
ings for any class of cabinet-work. It can be
gauged to any size, and it can cut and “ mould ”
to any shape, according to the article required.
At the same time that it is executing the mould-
ing of (say) a sewing-machine table, it can cut out
the outline after any given pattern—square, or in
serpentine curves, for instance.
The “ variety moulder ” in the mill is an excep-
tionally large and powerful one—much larger and
more powerful than any that can be imported,
without being specially manufactured to order.
This machine, indeed, was made by Beale and Co.
themselves, as a necessity for their work. It has
a wonderfully strong spring, and cutters that
mean and perform business. A feature here is a
very heavy guide, and the prevention, by means
of protecting guards, of all possibility of hand-
approach to the cutters. Thus, though all the
moulding can be done with the greatest ease,
speed and accuracy, it is not possible for a careless
workman’s hand to slip on to the cutters or
knives. Of course, a skilled workman could
operate without these guards—with “an open
head,” as it is_ termed—but factory experience
shows that the time comes at last when a momen-
tary inattention, a second’s carelessness, lets him
in for a life-maiming—the loss of a finger or two,
at the very least. Here, it is not possible for the
most careless operator to come to grief. The
guide keeps the timber in its proper place; the
guard prevents the operator’s hands from coming
near the cutters even by accident. It is a rigid
policy in this factory to have every otherwise
dangerous machine thus protected.
After (say) a table—and by a “table” in this
factory is generally understood a sewing-machine
table —comes from the planing-machine, though
it looks smooth to the eye, it awakens a roughness
to the touch. Therefore, it is sent on to the sand-
papering machine—another ingenuity of Beale
and Co.’s own make. 'Hie table is placed flat
upon a strong stand, held by screw leverage in the
exact required position, and sandpapered by
machinery. This is effected by means of a drum,
revolving laterally with great rapidity, and
depending by a shaft from a sort of moveable
crane arrangement which permits this revolving
drum to be whisked along over every part of the
surface of the wood at the will of the operator.
The table comes out with a surface as smooth as
glass, and is now ready for the polishing-room.
* # *
Did you ever notice the “ corner orna-
ments ” on the mouldings of a sewing-machine
cover like variations of gigantic chess-pieces?
These are not what they seem. Two are some-
times really only one; again, four are only one.
That is to say, the “ornament” or “chess-piece”
is first turned out and polished as a whole, and
then divided by the finest of fine circular saws.
Perhaps a quarter is wedged out, as one wedges
out a slice of cheese, and the resulting parts —the
three-quarter piece and the quarter—are re-
spectively inserted in the mouldings, with great
ornamental effect, to look like two wholes. Per-
haps the “chess-piece” is quartered (lengthwise)
and the four parts are placed so as to seem four
whole ornaments. The writer witnessed a sample
of this quartering. It was done, as everything
else in the cabinet-making line is done, by steam
machinery. There was no trouble nor complica-
tion. The “chess-piece ” had merely to be placed
in the adjustment and run along against a cir-
cular saw, which was horizontally revolving. This
saw is so exceedingly fine that it makes its razor-
like cuts without seeming to take away any of the
wood at all, a few grains only of sawdust being
perceptible as the waste of its work. This useful
tool was also made on the premises specially for
the work to which it has been put.
• # #
Upstairs, the visitor arrives at the “cabi-
net shop.” His first encounter is with another
“ variety moulder,” and as this is a bought one
he is enabled to at once see the vast superiority of
the larger and more powerful home-made one in
“ the mill.” The “ variety moulder ” upstairs is
a very small one and is only fit for light work,
such as the moulding of sewing-machine drawer-
ends.
Sewing-machine drawers are turned out by the
thousand, each size of drawers has every one of
each sample exactly the same fit in every parti-
cular, so that if a drawer at any time is broken or
lost, the owner of the damaged machine need only
send to Beale and Co. and say—“ I want such-
and-such a drawer,” and a new one can be imme-
diately forwarded, warranted to fit exactly the
vacant compartment.
The drawers are creditable specimens of fine
workmanship. They are all dovetailed, or what
is called “ lock-cornered.” The “look-cornering ”
is executed by a most ingenious machine, which is
difficult of description in an untechnical way.
Around a horizontal shaft there is a “worm ” of
cutters or blades, each blade being at a carefully-
arranged distance from the next. When station-
ary, the blades are in the mutual attitude of
points on a straight spiral; but, set the shaft
revolving rapidly, and the blades cut equi-distant
parallel slots, actually while in motion performing
the work of as many saws as there are blades. It
is the same in effect as if there were so many
parallel saws working upon the underneath sur-
face of a body passed over them.
The drawer-sides are packed, ends downwards,
in an adjustment, screw-levered securely, and
caused to traverse over this revolving bladed
worm. The writer saw about 400 slots thus cut in
one operation that lasted not half-a-minute. And
every slot was exactly the counterpart of every
other slot, and every projection fitted every slot
in every other piece, so that by just putting the
two slotted ends together, and giving a couple of
taps with a hammer, one had as beautiful a piece
of dovetailing work—staunch and firm—as one
could desire. When further dovetailed with the
other side and with the bottom of the drawer, and
submitted to the insinuation of glue, a drawer
becomes practically unbreakable, except by sledge-
hammer violence. n
• * *
Some excellent quilted panelling is turned
out by a specially-constructed grooving or milling
machine. The panel is “ reeded ” first; that is
to say, parallel mouldings, all running lengthwise,
are formed. Any pattern can be followed, and
the ingenuity of the manager and others is con-
tinually being exercised in the invention of new
designs, some of which are very elaborate and
strikingly picturesque. After being “reeded,”
the piece is turned sideways, and the mouldings
are crossed by others, thus producing a quilted
pattern that quite charms the vision.
The machine that performs this fine work is
nearly automatic in its action; a man could
almost handle it blindfolded. It is of Beale and
Co.’s own making, and after Mr. Stucchbury’s
own resign. “There is not another machineflike
it in New South Wales, doing the same class of
work,” proudly says the designer; and, indeed,
the machine executes moulding of any kind
reeding, quilting, crosscutting, ribbing, grooving,
&c., &c., after any fanciful pattern the operator
may please.
The cabinet-shop is supplied with similar appli-
ances to those found in the “mill,” but mostly
lighter and for smaller and more delicate work.
Up here, we meet again our old friend, the
“ waste steam,” Vessels of glue, boiling away
without any apparent cause, betray its presence
and influence, and here again is another “hot-
box,” in which the parts to be dovetailed and
glued are heated. The assigned reason for this
heating is that the heat opens the pores of the
wood; the glue then enters those pores, as the
two parts to be joined are dovetailed with each
other; all the glue that has not entered into the
pores is thus squeezed out, and the two pieces of
wood are veritably and actually amalgamated into
one piece. Great stress is laid on the fact that
there is no cake of glue in between the dove-
tailing, or joints; and it is said that, in nine cases
out of ten, the timber would break everywhere
else before the joinings will give way.
One revolving-drum in the cabinet-shop runs by
itself a sort of semi-sub-department, and drives
four small machines devoted to the work of
cutting-up thousands of tender pieces of small
stuff into the various sizes necessary for the sides
and ends of small drawers. There is a circular
crosscut saw, so fixed that its “fence” is square
with itself, and thus when the “fence”is pushed
forward, presenting to the saw the sample to be
worked up, the operator cannot help cutting each
piece truly square ; and, by the aid of the gauge,
each of equal size with the rest. Thus again pro-
viding thousands of pieces, of exactly similar sizes
and shapes, any one of which may be substituted
for any other, without the least inharmony of fit.
The four small machines alluded to were all
made by Beale and Co. themselves, the desiderata
sought being light running and high speed, both
of which purposes have been successfully achieved.
• • •
Stopped to admire some very convoluted
fretwork—it was an ornamental front panel of an
organ—the manager remarked to the writer: “I
suppose you’d fancy that this was only one piece ?”
“ So it is ; isn’t it ? ”
“No, sir. Although this is so thin, it is 3-ply.’’
The explanation that followed revealed the fact
that, in the thin but tough panel under notice
there were actually three plates of timber. The
grain of the two outside plates ran vertically ; the
middle plate, or inserted timber, was so placed
that its grain ran horizontally. The three plates
had been so manipulated in a glueing press as to
have virtually amalgamated into one panel not
more than one-eighth of an inch in thickness. The
reason for crossing the grains of the plates is that
this arrangement makes it impossible for the wood
to split. Although so thin, the fiercest attempts
to split it (as one easily can a single thin plate of
any sort of wood) fail. As Mr. Stutchbury re-
marked : “This makes it as tough as a bit of
leather.’’ The thinness of such a panel is pre-
served, because it gives a richer and better sound
to the instrument for which it is destined.
Numerous samples of admirable fretwork may
be seen in various departments, undergoing the
successive processes necessary before their final
issue. All the fine panels are “ 3-ply ” work, and,
by way of illustration, some apparently fragile
pieces were thrown about and suojected to various
abuses by knee and foot, but sustained no damage
whatever.
• * *
Returning downstairs to “the mill,” the
big “variety moulder” is seen at work upon
sewing-machine “ tables,” turning out pattern
after pattern of the same size, same shape, and the
same ornamentation. Sometimes a series of
square-sided patterns ; anon a succession of tables
with fancifully curved edges; whatever shape
is fancied, that shape, no matter how erratic the
design, is carved out by the faithful machine, so
that no sawing at all is necessary. So rapid is its
execution that one is not surprised to learn that
the head which contains the cutters revolves at
the tremendous speed of 5000 revolutions a minute.
It is started by a kick of the foot displacing a
lever, and is stopped by kicking the lever back
into its place. It is thoroughly under control,
and to those not in the secret of the foot-lever
there is something weird and almost magical in
its starting and stoppages “at the word of com-
mand.” It will run its two cutters all day long at
the rate of 5000 revolutions a minute, and yet the
bearings will not get heated. “ These,” said the
manager, “are better results than I have ever
seen with any imported tool of its kind.”
• « *
Wandering around, a resinous and balsamic
odour acquaints one that he is in the polishing-
room. Beale and Co. manufacture their own
polish.
Here we see “cabinets ’’awaiting the attentions
of the polishers ; also the scattered parts of organ-
work, which are all polished seriatim in this
department.
The bulk store contains the rough ironwork,
which is sent from there to the fitters’ depart-
ment. The rough iron castings are imported. In
the fitters’ room they are drilled with holes as
required, fitted, &c. The fitters attend to all the
boxes and templates (a kind of gauges), so that
every sample part shall be interchangeable with
every other similar sample part, whether made
to-day, or six months’ hence. The drilling
machines are light enough to be worked by ,a
youth, but are very strong.
After being drilled and bored, the iron castings
are taken into an adjoining room to be japanned.
This process is simplicity itself. There is no
brush-work. There is simply a deep bath of
liquid japan, and each casting is dipped and hung
up on an 3-hook to drain. The japan is thus dis-
tributed evenly over every part of the surface, and
blobs are avoided. Beale and Co. make their own
japan in another compartment of the premises.
They import their asphaltum direct from Trini-
dad. This is broken up and placed in a boiler,
which is then filled up with gasoline—a highly
inflammable article, dangerous to use in the
proximity of flame. Here, once more, come in
the invaluable services of the “waste steam.”
By turning on a tap, the pipe passing beneath the
boiler becomes charged with hot steam, the
asphaltum and gasoline are boiled together with-
out the presence of any flame, and the syrupy-
looking japanning fluid is “tried off” underneath.
After the japanning, comes the gilding of the
ornamental designs of each casting, and the letter-
ing giving the name of the firm.
There is a curious burnishing-machine for
burnishing the nickel parts. But, first of all, it
would be as well to mention that the nickeling is
accomplished in an isolated shed away from the
main-building. This shed contains a small
dynamo, and all the batteries, baths, and acids
necessary to the carrying out of nickeling opera-
tions. The burnishing-machine is of Beale and
Co.’s own make. There is a horizontal shaft sus-
pended by an upright in its centre from another
shaft, like (say) an ordinary gas-pipe with two
burners equidistant from the rod that suspends
them from the ceiling. At one end of this hori-
zontal shaft is a felt-wheel; at the other is a loose
pad consisting of circular discs of common calico
fastened together like those rag pen-wipers that
used to be made up of circles of rag sewn together
through the centre. This calico arrangement is
quite loose, and any number of its “ leaves ” may
be folded back and separated from the rest by the
fingers—so long as the machine is at rest, lb is,
in fact, as limp as the sort of pen-wiper already
described, of which it is a facsimile, only that it
stands like a wheel on the end of a horizontal rod.
But when that rod is revolving—2ooo turns a minute
here also—the centrifugal motion causes the loose
circles of calico to strain together and afford a
compact resistance to any surface presented to
their attention. The felt-wheel is for the first,
and the calico-leaved wheel for the finer and more
subtle, burnishing.
Emery-wheels are visible, too, in a dozen dif-
ferent degrees of coarseness, from the spark-
emitting rough emery to the finest one capable of
doing the most delicate work. These are neces-
sary for grinding down different parts of the iron-
work, which has to be fashioned with as much
exactitude as the woodwork, so that any one
sample shall be interchangeable with any other
similar sample. Once a week all these wheels are
cleaned and re-emeried. This is part of every
Saturday’s work, and Monday sees a fair “tart
with a full supply of olean emery-wheels.
• • •
The fitters’ room contains a lathe that
should be very hard to be excelled. It executes
with equal facility and precision—slotting, mill-
ing, screw-outting, and, in fact, any turning
work.
Here, in apparently profuse confusion, lie num-
berless parts of the ironwork of sewing-machinery
ready for any orders as to new fragments, or as to
exchange for old fragments. These castings are,
of course, not done on the premises, but are made
to order, from the firm’s own designs. So that,
should any one of Beale and Co.’s clients require*
any particular part of the ironwork of a sewing-
machine, they can be accommodated on the spot,

Rn The U same observation applies to all the finer
steel parts (imported) of the machine-heads-
handle checkspring, tuokmaker, cogs, check-
levers ’ cams, shuttle, needle-bar, presser-bar,
carrier slide &c. Seeing that there are over 120
different “ parts ” to a sewing-machine, and that
an injury to one or two may throw the whole ap-
oaratus out of gear, the advantage conferred on
Beale and Co.’s customers, in being able to send
for one needed part only, is seen to be very great
indeed As every part is of exactly the same pat-
tern as its fellow, all are equally interchangeable,
But these remarks really belong to the sampling-
room
In this department are stored the importations.
Beale and Co. import only the ‘‘heads ” of their
sewing-machines. These “ heads ’are manufao-
tured by Seidel and Naumann, of Dresden (Let-
many), after a design patented by Beale and Co.
The “ heads ” arrive in various pieces, and these
have to be separately examined, cleaned, and
sampled. .
“ Since you do so much purely Australian work,
would it not be possible to manufacture the
‘ heads ’ of those machines here.
“ Of course, it would be_ possible, said the
manager; “ but it is impracticable, io lay down
a set of plant to make that one machine, and all
the appliances attached to it, would cost £100,005.
Then, when you had got the machinery, in order
to make it remunerative you would have to turn
out so many machines that Australia could not
consume them.
* * *
The cabinets vary in design and orna-
mentation, which variations are only possible by
doing locally all the work that it is possible to do
in the country. Some cabinets have a flap which
gives an extension of table-surface; the door being
opened supports the flap ; when the door is closed,
the flap falls down, and the machine can be be-
stowed within a smaller space. .
Another ingenious application to the flaps is
found in the quarter-cabinet family machines,
which do not possess doors, but are open always,
front and back. This is known as the “table-
catch,” and is a japanned iron catch adjusted
underneath the flap, and also attached to the
under-part of the table’s border, to which the flap
itself is hinged. You raise the flap and the
“table-catch” automatically falls into holding
position, and the flap remains up. You rap the
catch gentlv and the flap falls. The idea is
simplicity itself
-The locking ’of the drawer in these quarter-
cabinets also locks on the cover, the one turning
of the key moving two catches. To take off the
cover, the drawer must be first unlocked. In
another class of machines, the full cabinets with
doors, the putting on of the cover locks the doors.
These are very handy machines, and are fitted
with inside pockets in which may be placed
”“ dle W ° rk “ d “ Wi " g
Then there are cabinets without flaps and whose
doors can be opened so as to turn right back
against the sides of the machine.
The panellings of these cabinets are of varied,
handsome designs, all worked out with marvellous
celerity by the wonderful “ variety moulder,” and
all of Australian wood. Some magnificent speci-
mens of inlaid cabinets are specially attractive,
and would make handsome pieces of furniture in
any drawing-room; nor, probably, would th e
nature of the article be suspected by the visitor, so
long as the doors remained closed, and the cover
remained on. Customers, however, generally
prefer the solid wood, picturesquely panelled, and
Beale and Co. only execute a moderate amount of
inlaying.
The ironwork consists of light castings, which,
by reason of their patterned shapes—designed by
the firm—are unusually strong. It is an expert
opinion that they cannot be beaten by any other
style yet invented.
In the sampling-room, every part of each
machine is finally put together, and forwarded to
the issuing-room, in a finished state of perfection.
It is not necessary to follow from this room any
machine to its destined home.
• # *
A visit to Beale and Co.’s Annandale
factory is industrially educative, and gives an Aus-
tralian cause for satisfaction in respect that at
least one eminent firm is not blind to those native
resources that belong to its own line of business.
The site of the handsome, six-storeyed establish-
ment at 484 George-street was purchased by Mr.
Beale eight years ago when it was a veritable mud-
hole. Now it is a palatial edifice, with an interior
that does great credit to the art of interior archi-
tectural decoration, as well as to the solidity of
utilitarian building. A novel feature in its con-
struction is the use of zinc ceilings, which, besides
being capable of picturesque intricacies of orna-
mentation, are remarkably durable. A zinc ceil-
ing never cracks, changes, nor alters, and cannot
fall. They are more expensive than the usual
ceilings to be found in business houses, but they
are absolutely permanent and they are certainly
very handsome.
Every office —at a rough outside guess, there
are some 60 or 70 offices—in this noble pile is
tenanted. Art studios, doctors, engineers, den-
tists, musical artists, and all manner of profes-
sional and commercial tenants occupy the accom-
modation on the various floors. In the basement,
as already stated, are a restaurant and a billiard-
room (with four tables) leased to the popular host
of Tattersall’s, George Adams. While mention-
ing the billiard-room, it may be pointed out that
its four tables (Alcock’s) are testimonies of the
valuable nature of Australian woods. No one
would ever dream of importing foreign timber in
the endeavour to compete with this class of work.
Beale and Co. employ several hundreds of hands;
considering their numerous country branches, it is
impossible to estimate offhand the total number
they employ.
Besides organs and sewing-machines, Beale and
Co. largely import pianos. Australia has not yet
got over its prejudice in favour of foreign-made
pianos, but the day will come assuredly when
piano-manufacturing will also be a safe and
remunerative Australian industry.
Beale and Co. endeavour to place their goods
within reach of all classes of customers, and there-
fore sell on easy terms.
PROPOSED LIMITED LIABILITY CO.
A company, to be called Beale and Co., Limited,
is now being formed to take over, as a going
concern, the manufacturing, importing and dis-
tributing business so successfully carried on by
Mr. Beale for more than 14 years ; and to acquire
the title and interest of the old firm in the free-
hold lands and buildings at 482 and 484 George-
street, Sydney, and the Annandale factory and
stores. The company will take over these at cost,
the freehold having been bought at a low price
many years ago. A freehold brick store and
stabies, at Myrtle-street, Sydney, will also be
transferred to the company.
Beale and 00. do not ask for any cash payment
to themselves, but are perfectly well satisfied to
take the value of their present interest in paid-up
shares. The idea is that the flotation into a
company will act co-operatively towards extend-
ing the business throughout Australia. The
capital is placed at .£150,000 in 150,000 shares to
be fully paid up to £l, with no further liability.
Directors ; Octavius Charles Beale, chairman and
managing director ; John See, M.P. (Col. Trea-
surer), vice-chairman ; T. M. Slattery, M.P.
(Cabinet Minister). Norman Frederick Giblin,
William Yero Read, W. H. Readett, and Robert
Amos. Bankers ; Bank of N.S.W. and Bank of
New Zealand. Solicitor : Thos. Read.
The average profits of the business, taken over
a series of years, are equal to the provision of a
reserve fund. Dividends, by the articles of
association, are payable half-yearly.
Beale and Co., Limited, will be in alliance with
the great firm of Seidel and Nauraann, Dresden
(Germany), an establishment that employs 1500
men and produces 80,000 sewing-machines and
8000 bicycles annually.
Nothing is charged for goodwill, and the com-
pany will start without loading of any kind. All
trade names and letters of registration owned by
the firm will become the absolute property of the
company, as applied to goods indicated by these
” Further particulars respecting this alluring co-
operative project may be gleaned from the pros-
pectus of *" Beale and Go., Limited,” which appears
in our business columns.
mi , . . , ,
The scheme is one that may be warmly com-
mended to the hearty sympathies and business
instincts of all classes. Not only are its remuner-
ative returns already assured, bub it has golden
prospects that promise to distribute benefits alike
to shareholders, to consumers, and to native
industry. When most, if not all, other firms are
languidly importing foreign timbers, Beale and
Co Limited, are determined o develop Aus-
trahan resources, thus providing employment
to Australian bushmen, shippers, and me-
chanics, and demonstrating to ourselves and
to the_ outside world that native wealth
is here in abundance, and that the search for it
will magnificently reward the earnest and persis-
tent seeker. By thus intelligently developing
Australia’s hidden treasures, waste is converted
into wealth, wealth into currency, and the dis-
tribution of all the outflow is among our own
people and not diverted, with numerous unavoid-
able contingent expenses, to absentees, who sap
this country of its natural vigour, Beale and Co.
Limited is, at one and the same time, a reliable
and remunerative business, and a patriotic move-
ment in a most commendable direction.
The promotion of Coroner Ghoul’s orderly,
Constable Davidson, has raised a row amongst the
500 or so senior men thus passed over. It was a
press job, done in gratitude for reams of “copy.”
There’s a morgue-keeper’s advantage. He is an
overflowing fountain of information, and practi-
cally hypnotises the news-scavengers if he is
decent." Davidson is famous for his cheerful
method of dealing with corpses, and taking their
photos. He deserves his “ sthripe.”

Little to choose between a bailiff and a
hangman. They are both servants of the sheriff,
and live by executions.
Liberia wished to be represented at Chicago
Exhibition, but was bankrupt. So the Govern-
ment gave its Commissioner .£lOOO worth of
unused postage-stamps, with which the represen-
tative visited most of the stamp agencies and col-
lectors in the States. Only £4OO could he raise,
and the Liberian Government still owes the Com-
missioner’s board-bill.

Among the applicants for a Sydney re-
portership recently was one with credentials from
London Savage Club. The scribe accompanied
his application with an original version of a cur-
rent incident, but the proprietary passed him by
because, in his two first “slips,” he introduced
ne plus ultra, ultra vires, nemine contmdicentc,
and videbilur infra. Which shows that in jour-
nalism the classics should only be used horateo-
pathically.
My little girl has dimpled cheeks
Whose healthful colour comes and goes ;
The teeth she flashes when she speaks
Are whiter than the winter snows.
Love’s light is gleaming in her eyes,
Her shoulders make a choice display ;
But far beyond those charms I prize
That “ fringe as worn by Princess May.”
I bought it as a glad surprise
For Angela, the other day.
Her slender waist she doesn’t pinch ;
It measures twenty by the tape—
She might reduce another inch
And still look natural in shape.
Her nose, a sculptor would allow,
Is fashioned in a classic way :
And, O ! you ought to see her now
In “ fringe as worn by Princess May.”
I placed it first upon her brow
(Was half-a-guinea much to pay ?)
Sweet Angela ! she is my bride,
My wife for better or for worse ;
This moment sitting by my side
Delighted with this clever verse.
And when swift years have flitted by,
And Time has left his streaks of grey—
Old Time who spares not royalty—
I’ll stroke her silken hair, and say
(Provided neither of them dye)
“ The same as worn by Princess May.”
E.F.

Briggs, of Ballast Quarries.
[FOR THE BULLRTIN.]
They’d built a school at Quarry Camp, had the
blessed Government,
And, you bet your boots, a proper sort was the
teacher man they sent !
He'could talk like twenty navvies, he could drink
like twenty more,
HebOOld do his cheque up quicker than was ever
done before;
He would play it up at poker, or at euchre, or fan-
tan—
Oh, yes, Briggs, of Ballast Quarries, was a proper
kind of man },
When the famous tug-of-war with Brown’s at
Quarry Camp was made,
And the Jackson party triumphed—with the
donkey-engine's aid —
It was Briggs, of Ballast Quarries School, who cap-
tained JaSkaon’s, and
He had to fight no less than nine of Brown’s
defeated band :
There was nothing less would do them than that
Brings should fight the mob,
And for seven Sundays running Briggs was fairly
on the job.
But when he’d belted nine of ’em in Bill, the
blacksmith’s, shop,
The rest made up their minds that they’d just let
' the matter drop.
There was never such a hungry man for fight since
fights began;
He was pointed out to visitors as “Briggs, the
fighting man — ”
There was never such a terror to go on a raging
tear,
And he’d blast the vegetation when he really tried
to swear;
And he’d say, with verbal trimmings—he was
never at a loss—
That he wasn’t even frightened of his sanguinary
boss !
They were kicking up a shindy once —a sort of
hell’s delight—
Down at Doolan’s, and it promised to be kept up
all the night,
And the chap in number seven—he was School
Inspector Crow —
Couldn’t sleep a half-a-minute for the noisy crowd
below,
But he knew the drunkest of them all—the tightest
of the tight—
The man with gory features, who had just pulled
. off a fight,
Was a teacher in his district; so he sent him down
a note -
In the hope to stop his acting of the silly, giddy
goat;
But it didn’t frighten Briggsy, and he didn’t stop
his song,
And he never checked the fellow who was thump-
ing on the gong;
Never stayed the half a fraction of the most un-
holy din,
And he answered to the landlord, when he brought
the message in,
“ What the devil is your name, sir, do you know,
- man, who I am ?
Tell him ‘ Briggs, of Ballast Quarries, and he
doesn’t care a damn ! ’ ”
But the work at last was finished, and the camp it
didn’t stay,
And Briggs’s school was shifted, and himself was
sent away;
And I never came across him, for about a year or
so,
Till I met him out at Boggy Creek, a week or two
ago.
But I found him sadly altered, to my great and
lasting grief,
For the change in Briggs was awful —it was
almost past belief!
He was settled in a little German township—
Doragee—
Where the people are as pious and as sleepy as
can be;
Where they never have no functions, and where
no one can get tight,
For there ain’t a pub. about there, and you never
see a fight,
And you wouldn’t find a pack of cards, no matter
how you’d search,
And the folks object to dancing, and go twice-a-
day to church;
But the darkest, saddest feature of the melancholy
case
Was that Briggs of Ballast Quarries seemed to
like the blessed place !
He was dressed up like a masher, and he wore a
watch and ring
(At the camp he used to fasten up his moleskins
with a string).
And his boots were highly polished, and his mo.
was nicely curled,
I believe a bigger swell than Briggs there isn’t in
the world !
And he didn’t seem to want me, and he didn’t
even show
E’er a particle of interest in the coves he used to
know;
And it didn’t seem quite proper, nor harmonise
at all
With the joyous ancient style of Briggs before his
dismal fall,
That he’d take it all so easy—might as well be
underground
As be living where there ain’t a pub, for twenty
miles around;
Where the folks get up at dawn of day, at sun-
down to retire,
And be going to tea-meetings, and be leading in
the choir,
And be looking sadly pious where the church folks
congregate,
And — Briggs, from Ballast Quarries, to be hand-
ing round the plate!
But they tell me down at Sandy, where they seem
to know it all,
That it ain’t at all a trouble to account for Briggs’
fall;
That he used to be as gladsome and unsober as of
old,
And the navvy songs he warbled, and the navvy
yarns he told,
And his boots were always busted, and his morals
were the same,
And his cheque was always mortgaged twenty
days before it came.
But he tore his ancient habits up completely by
the roots
When he drifted into goodness and to wearing
shiny boots.
And the reason why he’s altered all his former
sinful ways,
And his style of talk no longer sets the atmosphere
ablaze,
And his Quarry Camp acquaintances he doesn’t
recognise,
And he’s going in for study, and promotion, and
a rise,
And he wears a stand-up collar, and a whisker
with a curl,
And is sad and straight and steady, is —that
Briggs has got a girl I
A. CHEE.
At a Street Corner.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.0
The footsteps of the crowd were in his ears, the
voices of it, its sighing and its laughter. His
fingers lay motionless across the raised letters of
the great book on his knees, and his eyes were
strained forward but they saw nothing save his
own misery. It was the only thing in the
heavens above and the earth beneath that he had
ever seen. They thought he was asleep, the tram-
waiting crowd around, for his head had fallen a
little forward on to the placard he wore upon his
breast, and his lips were quiet.
And the crowd went home, little by little, till
the corner was nearly deserted, but he did not
move. Then there came a slow, shuffling step
along the foot-path, the step of the old, old man
who sold papers daily at the same corner. And
the money-box, near the great book, was sud-
denly grasped by a thin, old hand and the
shuffling steps went away rapidly and died
in the distance. But the boy sat motionless.
Almost a smile parted his lips. “No one
ever stole from me before,” he whispered to
himself, and lifted his head a little, as if the act
had placed him for once on an equality with a
world that always pitied. He smiled faintly
again. There must be someone in the city as
miserable as himself if they had come to such
a pass as to steal from him.
But the next day this mood had passed away,
and nothing but bitterness was in his heart.
And when the slow, shuffling step came past
him again, and he thought of the comfort the
stolen money would have brought him, a very
demon came into him.
He stood upright in his place, and laid the
money-box and the great book down on the
ground, and took two quick steps in the direction of
the slow, shuffling ones, felt for the figure that
carried newspapers, and knocked it heavily down
across the pavement.
The nerves of the crowd were immeasurably
shocked. The great book lay in the dust ; the old
man was slowly raising himself, in injured sur-
prise, and there was the blind, young, passionate
face before them or they would have thought their
eyes had played them false.
There was a man of the church amongst them
and he went forward and laid a quiet hand on the
boy’s sleeve. But the young face was still ablaze
and the poor, wild eyes straining forward.
“ Let me go!” he said, trying to free himself.
But the hand still held bis sleeve and the colour
dropped from his face again.
“I am blind,” he said, as if the calamity had
just come upon him.
They led him back to his seat and placed him
upon it gently, and laid the book on his knees
again.
“ Read,” the man of the church said softly, and
the boy opened the great Book and laid his quiver-
ing fingers on the first page.
“ And God said, let there be Light,” he read.
Then his voice vibrated. “ And God said, let
there be Light, ” he fepeated.
But no one even expected him to finish the
sentence.
ETHEL S. TURNER.
Red Gravel.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
On the graded side of a hill in West
Maoriland grew six acres of currant-bush
clumped as densely as bamboos, and consisting of
slight saplings, averaging about 20ft. in height. A
lad who, from the top of the hill, was venture-
some enough to climb as far as ho could to any
chosen swaying tops-any two afforded ample
support—might enjoy a slide down the grade of
bending tops for IQOyds. or so. Nearing the end
of his journey he would select his sapling and be
gently swung down to the ground. It was
exliilarating and dangerous ; therefore, it was the
chief delight of the boys in that vicinity to climb
in a squad to the top of the hill, and then—a
glorious aerial trip, the course of the trapezists
being visible only as are the tracks of the wind in
standing corn. One evening a merry troop
entered the clump and screamed joyously adown
the trapeze. Some emerged before others. The
troop went home. Save one. He was missing
and had entered. That was all that was known.
Diligent search revealed nothing. Gradually the
incident faded out of the memories of all except
one—the lost one’s mother.
* « *
Three years afterwards, and a youth,
alone, entered on this strange trapeze. He passed
through the whole distance, was swayed down
and just about to let go, when he looked down-
wards. Horror ! In the dim spaces between the
ferns he saw all around him—red gravel ! Cling-
ing desperately, he regained his position, crept
back to the roadside, and went home, quivering in
every limb at his narrow escape. For that red
gravel had only one meaning—a deserted shaft!
And he had been in suspension plumb above it.
* * »
Two more years had elapsed, when a slip
on the hill-side, beginning at the shaft, which had
been 40ft. deep and half-full of water, laid bare
the bones of our lost schoolmate. Thus strangely
had the first boy lowered himself into eternity.

The Coolgardie gold boom is the only Australian boom for some years past
which shows signs of permanence. Coolgardie’s chief drawbacks are dust and heat
and myriads of flies, and the desert, variegated only by occasional
brackish mud-holes, which lies between it and the nearest civilisation. But the
gold is there, and gold is the one essential.
